The Guardian (al-Wali) and Types of Guardianship in Marriage
Defining wali and explaining the two types of guardianship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the legal wali, state his conditions, distinguish between wilayat nadb and wilayat ijbar, and list the four causes of compulsory guardianship.

Core Idea
Guardianship in marriage is either optional for the mature woman or compulsory over minors, insane women, and slaves.

In this passage from al-Durr al-Mukhtar, the author defines the wali (guardian) and the two types of guardianship (wilayah) in marriage. Understanding these distinctions is crucial because they determine who has authority to contract marriage and when that authority is binding.

Guided Path
1
Define wali legally
Identify the wali as the person whose word is executed over another. Note the conditions: the wali must be sane and adult.
Linguistically, wali means opposite of enemy, but legally it has a specific technical meaning.
2
Distinguish the two types of guardianship
Wilayat nadb (optional guardianship) applies to the mature woman (mukallafah). Wilayat ijbar (compulsory guardianship) applies to the minor, the insane woman, and the slave.
The mature woman can marry without the wali's permission; the wali's role is recommended.
3
Identify the causes of compulsory guardianship
Compulsory guardianship is established by four causes: kinship (qarabah), ownership (milk), clientage (wala'), and trusteeship (amanah).
These causes justify the wali's power to compel marriage.
4
Connect to practice
Recognize that a mature woman's marriage is valid without wali's consent in Hanafi view, unlike Shafi'i where wali is a pillar.
This has real-world implications for marriage validity and family disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوليُّ لغة : خلافُ العدو، والمراد به في مثل هذا المقام : هو الذي ينفذ قوله على الغير شاءَ أو أبى، ويشترط فيه أن يكون عاقلاً بالغاً. والولاية نوعان : ولاية ندب ؛ وهي على المكلّفة. وولاية إجبار ؛ وهي على الصغير والمعتوهة والمرقوقة. وثبتت ولاية الإجبار بأربع : قرابة ، وملك، وولاء، وأمانة. كذا في «الدر المختار وحواشيه». والكفؤ المثلُ المساوي من المكافأة، بمعنى المساواة، والمراد هنا : المساواة المخصوصة المعتبرة شرعاً.
Wali linguistically: opposite of enemy, and what is meant by it in such a context: he whose word is executed over another whether he wishes or not, and it is conditional that he be sane and adult. Guardianship is of two types: guardianship of recommendation, which is over the mature woman; and guardianship of compulsion, which is over the minor, the insane woman, and the slave. Guardianship of compulsion is established by four: kinship, ownership, clientage, and trusteeship. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses. And kafa'ah: the likeness, the equal, from mukaafa'ah meaning equality, and what is meant here: the specific equality considered legally.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms for the chapter on guardianship and suitability in marriage. First, wali (guardian) is defined linguistically as opposite of enemy, but legally it refers to a person whose authority is binding on another. The wali must be sane and adult. Wilayah (guardianship) is of two types: (1) Wilayat nadb (optional guardianship) over a mature woman, meaning she can act without him but his involvement is recommended; (2) Wilayat ijbar (compulsory guardianship) over minors, insane women, and slaves. Compulsory guardianship is established by four causes: kinship (qarabah), ownership (milk), clientage (wala'), and trusteeship (amanah). Second, kafa'ah (suitability/equality) is defined as similitude and equality, but legally it is a specific equality considered by the Shari'ah. These definitions are from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): The person whose word is executed over another; the guardian in marriage.
ولاية ندب (wilayat nadb): Optional guardianship; the mature woman may marry without the wali's permission.
ولاية إجبار (wilayat ijbar): Compulsory guardianship; the wali can force the ward into marriage.
قرابة (qarabah): Kinship; one of the four causes establishing compulsory guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Over whom does a wali have compulsory guardianship (wilayat ijbar)?
A) Any free mature woman
B) The minor, the insane woman, and the slave
C) Only the minor
D) Anyone who has not given birth

Key Takeaway
Guardianship in Hanafi law is not monolithic; it is optional for the mature woman and compulsory for minors, insane women, and slaves.

Legal Suitability (Kafa'ah) in Marriage
Defining kafa'ah as legal equality in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kafa'ah legally, explain its significance in marriage, and identify that it is a specific legal equality considered by the Shari'ah.

Core Idea
Kafa'ah is not mere social equality but a specific legal consideration that affects the marriage's propriety and the wali's right to object.

This passage defines kafa'ah (suitability) as 'the specific equality considered legally.' In Hanafi fiqh, kafa'ah is a condition for the marriage to be considered appropriate, though it is not a condition for validity. Understanding this definition is key to appreciating the role of equality in marriage contracts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic root
Kafa'ah comes from mukaafa'ah meaning equality or similitude.
Linguistically, it simply means being equal.
2
Note the legal qualification
The passage says 'the specific equality considered legally' – meaning not all forms of equality, but those defined by Shari'ah.
The details of what constitutes kafa'ah (lineage, religion, profession, etc.) are elaborated elsewhere.
3
Recognize the definition's scope
The passage only gives the general definition; it does not list the criteria. The student should understand that kafa'ah is a legal concept, not just cultural.
Hanafi considers kafa'ah in lineage, piety, profession, freedom, and wealth.
4
Connect to practice
Kafa'ah gives the wali the right to object if the husband is not suitable. If the mature woman consents to a non-kuf', the marriage is valid but the wali may seek annulment.
This has implications for family disputes and court cases.

Source Text

Wali linguistically: opposite of enemy, and what is meant by it in such a context: he whose word is executed over another whether he wishes or not, and it is conditional that he be sane and adult. Guardianship is of two types: guardianship of recommendation, which is over the mature woman; and guardianship of compulsion, which is over the minor, the insane woman, and the slave. Guardianship of compulsion is established by four: kinship, ownership, clientage, and trusteeship. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses. And kafa'ah: the likeness, the equal, from mukaafa'ah meaning equality, and what is meant here: the specific equality considered legally.

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms for the chapter on guardianship and suitability in marriage. First, wali (guardian) is defined linguistically as opposite of enemy, but legally it refers to a person whose authority is binding on another. The wali must be sane and adult. Wilayah (guardianship) is of two types: (1) Wilayat nadb (optional guardianship) over a mature woman, meaning she can act without him but his involvement is recommended; (2) Wilayat ijbar (compulsory guardianship) over minors, insane women, and slaves. Compulsory guardianship is established by four causes: kinship (qarabah), ownership (milk), clientage (wala'), and trusteeship (amanah). Second, kafa'ah (suitability/equality) is defined as similitude and equality, but legally it is a specific equality considered by the Shari'ah. These definitions are from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses.

Key Terms
الكفؤ (al-kuf'): Equality in lineage, religion, profession, etc., as legally considered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Any similarity between spouses
B) Equality defined by Shari'ah in specific matters
C) Only equality in lineage

Key Takeaway
Kafa'ah is a legally defined equality that affects the marriage's propriety and the wali's right to object.

Ruling: Marriage of Mature Woman Valid Without Guardian
Stating the ruling and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the marriage of a free mature woman without a guardian and explain the distinction between validity (nafadh) and irrevocability (luzum).

Core Idea
A marriage without a guardian is valid but not binding; the guardian can object unless a child is born.

In Hanafi fiqh, a woman's marriage without a guardian is valid, but the guardian retains a right to object. This ruling distinguishes between the marriage being legally effective (nafadh) and being irrevocable (luzum). Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the law in family matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a free, mature (mukallafa) woman marrying herself without a guardian. The ruling is nafadh (valid) — the marriage produces legal effects like divorce and inheritance.
Nafadh here does not mean luzum (binding).
2
List the conditions for validity
The woman must be free and legally responsible (mature, sane). No guardian's consent is required for validity.
Even if the husband is not a kuf' (equal), the marriage is valid.
3
Identify the guardian's right
The guardian (asaba by himself) can object and have the marriage annulled by a judge, but only if the woman has not yet given birth.
Childbirth removes the guardian's right to object.
4
Distinguish between nafadh and luzum
Nafadh means the marriage is sahih (valid) and all consequences follow. Luzum means it cannot be undone. This marriage is valid but not binding because the guardian's objection can dissolve it.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفذ نكاح حرّة مكلّفة ولو من غير كفؤ بلا ولي، وله الاعتراض هنا. قوله : نفذ؛ المراد بالنفاذ : الصحة المستلزمة لترتب أحكام النكاح؛ كالطلاق والتوارث وغيرهما ، لا اللزوم؛ فإنَّ اللازم ما لا يمكن رفعه، وهذا يمكن رفعه باعتراض الولي. قوله : بلا ولي؛ بلا رضاه وإذنه. قوله : وله الاعتراض؛ دفعاً للعار. عنه، هذا ما لم تلد، فإن ولدت فلا حق للولي في الفسخ، ذكره قاضي خان. قوله : الاعتراض؛ أي لو زوجت نفسها من غير رضى الولي، له أن يعترض فيفسخه القاضي بشرط أن يكون عصبة بنفسه، ولو غير محرم.
The marriage of a free, legally responsible woman is executed even if from one not her equal without a guardian, and he has the right to object here. His statement: 'is executed'; what is meant by execution: validity that necessitates the consequences of marriage, like divorce, inheritance, etc., not irrevocability (luzum); because what is irrevocable cannot be removed, and this can be removed by the guardian's objection. His statement: 'without a guardian' means without his consent and permission. His statement: 'and he has the right to object' to avert disgrace. From him, this is as long as she has not given birth, for if she gives birth, the guardian has no right to annulment, as Qadi Khan mentioned. His statement: 'objection' means if she marries herself without the guardian's consent, he may object and the judge annuls it on condition that he be a residuary heir by himself, even if he is not a mahram.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This is a ruling statement establishing that the marriage of a free, legally responsible (mature) woman is valid (nāfidh) even if she marries a man who is not her equal (kuf') and without a guardian's consent. The term 'nāfidh' here means sahih (valid) i.e. it produces legal effects like divorce and inheritance, but not necessarily binding (lāzim) — because the guardian can object and have it annulled. The woman marries without the guardian's consent or permission. The guardian has the right to object to avert family disgrace. However, if she gives birth, the guardian loses the right to annulment. The condition for the guardian's objection is that he must be a residuary heir (asaba) by himself, even if he is not a mahram (unmarriageable kin). This is based on Qadi Khan's ruling.

Key Terms
النفاذ (al-nafadh): Validity that entails legal effects, as opposed to irrevocability (luzum).
الاعتراض (al-i'tirad): The guardian's challenge to a marriage made without his consent, leading to court annulment.
اللزوم (al-luzum): Binding nature of a contract, meaning it cannot be unilaterally dissolved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the guardian lose the right to annul the marriage?
A) Immediately after the wedding
B) After the woman gives birth
C) After one year
D) Never

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a mature woman's marriage without a guardian is valid (nafadh) but not binding (lazim); the guardian may object only before childbirth.

Hasan ibn Ziyad's Narration: Marriage Without Guardian Not Permissible
Presenting a conflicting transmission from Hasan ibn Ziyad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on marriage without a guardian as transmitted by Hasan ibn Ziyad, explain its rationale of 'fasād al-zamān', and distinguish it from the well-known zahir al-riwayah position.

Core Idea
When social corruption threatens, the Hanafi school may adopt a weaker narration to protect guardians' rights.

You have learned that the well-known Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah) permits a woman to marry without a guardian. However, a separate transmission from Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Abu Hanifa himself held that such a marriage is not permissible. This lesson examines that minority report and the later preference for it due to fasād al-zamān (corruption of the times).

Guided Path
1
State the well-known and alternate positions
Identify the majority view (marriage without guardian is valid) and the Hasan ibn Ziyad narration (not permissible).
The alternate view is weaker in transmission but later preferred.
2
Identify the source of the alternate view
Hasan ibn Ziyad transmitted this from Abu Hanifa. Recognize that this is a nawadir narration, not part of zahir al-riwayah.
Hasan ibn Ziyad was a student of Abu Hanifa.
3
Examine the rationale (wajh)
The commentator states the justification: 'deflecting harm from guardians and the corruption of the times.' Understand that this is a social consideration.
The ruling adapts to prevent harm in corrupt times.
4
Determine the fatwa authority
Qadi Khan gave fatwa on this narration. Later Hanafi muftis often prefer it due to fasād al-zamān.
This shows usul al-iftaa: choosing among riwayat based on need.
5
Contrast with the well-known position
The zahir al-riwayah remains the doctrinal position, but the mufta bihi for practice may shift.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate and reflects flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروى الحسن له عن أبي حنيفة له عدم جوازه، وعليه فتوى قاضي خان . قوله : وروى الحسن له؛ أي ابن زياد، وجه هذه الرواية دفع الضرر عن الأولياء وفساد الزمان.
And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa the non-permissibility of it, and upon it is the fatwa of Qadi Khan. His statement: 'And al-Hasan narrated from him' i.e. Ibn Ziyad; the justification of this narration is repelling harm from the guardians and the corruption of the times.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This is a report of an internal Hanafi disagreement. Hasan ibn Ziyad (a student of Abu Hanifa) transmitted from Abu Hanifa that a marriage without a guardian is not permissible (i.e., invalid). The commentator explains that the rationale for this narration is to prevent harm to guardians due to the corruption of the times. Qadi Khan based his fatwa on this narration, indicating that in later times this view was preferred for practical reasons despite contradicting the well-known zahir al-riwayah position.

Key Terms
رواية الحسن (riwayat al-hasan): A transmission from Abu Hanifa through Hasan ibn Ziyad that differs from the well-known view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the justification given for Hasan ibn Ziyad's narration that a marriage without a guardian is not permissible?
A) It is based on a stronger hadith
B) To avoid harm to guardians due to corruption of the times
C) It follows the opinion of the Shafi'i school
D) It is the view of all the early Hanafi scholars

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school may adopt a weaker narration as mufta bihi when social conditions necessitate protecting guardians from harm.

Intra-Hanafi Differences on the Guardian Requirement in Marriage
Statements of the three Hanafi imams on the validity of a woman's self-marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the validity of a woman's self-marriage and identify the established (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school's established view allows a free, sane, adult woman to contract her own marriage without a guardian.

This lesson examines the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf among the three founding Hanafi imams on whether a guardian (wali) is required for a woman's marriage. The passage shows that while there is a minority narration from Abu Yusuf requiring a guardian, the well-known position (zahir al-riwayah) is that all three imams consider the contract valid. This has practical consequences for fatwa and practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a free, sane, adult woman marrying herself. Determine the hukm (valid or invalid) according to each imam.
The act is the marriage contract (nikah).
2
Locate the positions of the three imams
State what each imam holds: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Note that the text says according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is valid, and according to Muhammad it is also valid.
The text explicitly states Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold validity, then mentions Muhammad's view.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the extra narration from Abu Yusuf that requires a guardian and makes the marriage invalid without one.
This is a minority narration (riwaya) that is not part of the zahir al-riwayah.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The later consensus of the Hanafi school is that the marriage is valid. This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.
The established view (zahir al-riwayah) agrees on validity.
5
Compare with other schools
Note that the Shafi'i and Maliki schools require a guardian, so the marriage would be invalid without one according to them.
This cross-madhhab difference has practical implications in pluralistic contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ الحرَّةَ العاقلةَ البالغةَ إذا زوَّجَتْ نفسها، فعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له ينعقد، وفي رواية عن أبي يوسف الله لا ينعقد إلا بولي، وعند محمد له ينعقد.
Know that the free, sane, adult woman when she marries herself, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is concluded; and in a narration from Abu Yusuf it is not concluded except with a guardian; and according to Muhammad it is concluded.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage clarifies the positions of the founding Hanafi imams on whether a free, sane adult woman can validly contract her own marriage. The established view (zahir al-riwayah) is that according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the marriage is valid (yan'aqid). There is a separate narration from Abu Yusuf that it is not valid without a guardian. Imam Muhammad's position is that it is valid. The commentary later in the text notes that there are multiple narrations from each, but the settled opinion is the agreement on validity.

Key Terms
ينعقد (yan'aqid): The marriage contract is validly concluded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known view (zahir al-riwayah), which imams hold that a woman's self-marriage is valid?
A) Abu Hanifa only
B) Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad only
D) All three: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's established position is that a free, sane, adult woman can contract her own marriage without a guardian.

Resolving the Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Validity of a Woman's Self-Marriage
Resolving an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding Abu Yusuf's final view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between al-Sarakhsi and al-Tahawi regarding Abu Yusuf's final position on a woman's marriage without a guardian, and state the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The settled Hanafi position is validity; al-Tahawi's contrary riwayah is not thabit.

Within the Hanafi school, there is an internal dispute about whether a woman may marry herself without a guardian. The majority view is that it is valid, but some early authorities report that Abu Yusuf retracted to requiring a guardian. This passage clarifies which transmission is the established (thabit) position, resolving the ikhtilaf for the purposes of fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine the legal act under dispute: a woman's marriage (nikah) without the presence of a guardian (wali).
The issue is about validity, not just permissibility.
2
Recognize the two rivayat (transmissions)
Al-Sarakhsi reports that all three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agreed on permissibility. Al-Tahawi and al-Karkhi report that Abu Yusuf's final view was non-permissibility without a guardian.
The disagreement is not among the imams themselves but about which report is correct.
3
Apply Hanafi tarjeh (preference) principles
The author declares that the thabit (confirmed) position is the agreement on validity, based on al-Sarakhsi's explanation. This is a tarjih (preference) of one riwayah over another.
Consider the authority of the sources: al-Sarakhsi's work is part of zahir al-riwayah.
4
State the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon ruling is that a woman's self-marriage is valid, whether to a kuf' (equal) or not.
This is the position for fatwa and qada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتحصلُ أنَّ الثَّابت الآن هو اتِّفاقُ الثَّلاثة على الجواز مطلقاً من الكفؤ وغيره، هذا على الوجه الذي ذكرناه، وهو ما ذكرَهُ السَّرَخْسِي، وَأَمَّا على ما ذكرَهُ الطَّحاوِيُّ من أن قول أبي يوسف الله المرجوع إليه عدم الجواز إلا بولي، وكذا الكَرْخي في «مختصره»، فلا.
So it is established that what is confirmed now is the agreement of the three on permissibility absolutely, from the equal and other than him, this according to the way we mentioned, which is what al-Sarakhsi mentioned. But according to what al-Tahawi mentioned that the view of Abu Yusuf which he returned to is non-permissibility except with a guardian, and likewise al-Karkhi in his Mukhtasar, then no.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage resolves the internal Hanafi dispute by stating that the settled (thābit) position now is that all three imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad—agree on the permissibility (jawāz) of a woman's self-marriage, whether to an equal (kuf') or not. This is according to the explanation of al-Sarakhsi. However, al-Tahawi and al-Karkhi held that Abu Yusuf retracted to the view of non-permissibility without a guardian. The author indicates that the first view (the agreement on validity) is the established one, though there is a difference in transmission.

Key Terms
ثابت (thabit): The confirmed and relied-upon ruling within the school.
جواز (jawaz): Permissibility; here meaning the act is legally valid and allowed.
مرجوع إليه (marju‘ ilayhi): The view that a scholar retracted to; his final position after revision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lesson, which scholar's report is considered the thabit (established) position on the validity of a woman's marriage without a guardian?
A) Al-Tahawi
B) Al-Karkhi
C) Al-Sarakhsi
D) Abu Yusuf's own final statement

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi is that a woman's marriage without a guardian is valid, based on the agreement of the three imams as transmitted by al-Sarakhsi.

Can a Woman Contract Her Own Marriage? Hanafi vs Shafi'i/Maliki
Distinguishing Hanafi view from Shafi'i and Maliki

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a woman's capacity to conclude her marriage contract and distinguish it from the Shafi'i and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
A woman’s own marriage contract is valid in the Hanafi school if her guardian consents; in Shafi'i and Maliki, only the guardian may pronounce the contract.

This passage draws a sharp contrast between the Hanafi school (via Abu Yusuf) and the Shafi'i and Maliki schools on the role of the woman's expression in marriage. The key point is whether a woman can validly contract her own nikah or if the guardian must speak. Understanding this difference is crucial for applying the correct ruling in marriage contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Abu Yusuf's view as presented: a woman's own expression can conclude a valid marriage if the guardian is present and consents.
This is one Hanafi view; the school generally permits the woman to contract with guardian's consent.
2
Identify the opposing position
Note that Shafi'i and Maliki hold that a marriage contracted by a woman alone is invalid under all circumstances, regardless of guardian consent.
No exception: even if the wali agrees later, the contract is void.
3
Analyze the basis of the dispute
Although proofs are not given in this passage, locate the root: Shafi'i/Maliki rely on the hadith 'No marriage without a wali', while Hanafi considers consent sufficient.
The Hanafi response reframes the condition from the wali's utterance to his consent.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: in Hanafi, a woman may arrange her own marriage with guardian approval; in Shafi'i/Maliki, the wali must pronounce the contract.
This affects who says 'I accept' and when the contract is valid.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Note that the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school is the validity of the woman's contract with guardian's permission, as stated by Abu Yusuf.
Later Hanafi masters generally follow this view.

If she marries herself, it is not valid at all, whether the guardian permits or not. Commentary
This passage draws a clear contrast between the Hanafi position (specifically as attributed to Abu Yusuf in one view) and the positions of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik. According to the Shafi'i and Maliki schools, a woman cannot validly contract her own marriage under any circumstances; the wali must be the one to pronounce the marriage contract. This applies whether she acts as agent (wakīla) or principal (asīla). Even if the wali agrees later, the marriage is void. In contrast, according to the Hanafi school (and specifically the view of Abu Yusuf mentioned here), the woman's own expression can conclude a valid marriage provided the guardian is present and gives his consent. Thus, the Hanafi school allows greater autonomy to women while still requiring the guardian's approval for full effect.

Key Terms
عبارة النساء (ibarat al-nisa'): The woman's own words of offer and acceptance in the marriage contract.

The Hanafi school validates a woman’s marriage contract if the guardian consents, unlike Shafi'i and Maliki who require the guardian’s direct pronouncement.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage Without a Wali: Hanafi Positions and the Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Dispute on validity of a woman marrying without a wali among Hanafi imams and vs Shafi'i/Maliki

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on the validity of a woman contracting her own marriage without a wali, and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki counter-argument using the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
The woman's capacity to contract is affirmed, but wali's consent is required; the ikhtilaf is on the effect of its absence.

This passage addresses one of the most well-known inter-madhhab disputes in Islamic family law: can a woman validly marry without a guardian (wali)? Within the Hanafi school alone, there are multiple narrations from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, leading to a range of rulings. The passage also presents the Shafi'i/Maliki objection and the Hanafi response, allowing you to see the legal reasoning in action. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply usul al-fiqh to hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the woman's capacity
Identify the consensus among the three Hanafi imams that a free, sane, adult woman has the legal capacity to contract her own marriage. This is the foundation of the Hanafi view.
Even Abu Yusuf's narration requiring wali presence still allows the woman to utter the contract.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
List the different narrations from Abu Hanifa (valid), Abu Yusuf (three narrations: invalid, valid from kufu' only, valid absolutely), and Muhammad (suspended or valid). Note the later reliance on the agreement of absolute validity per Sarakhsi.
Al-Timirtashi later opts for non-permissibility due to corrupt times, showing the dynamic nature of fatwa.
3
Identify the opponent and their proof
State the Shafi'i/Maliki position: the wali must be the contracting party; the woman cannot contract at all. Cite their primary evidence: hadith 'La nikaha illa bi wali' and the hadith of 'Aisha declaring such marriages invalid.
The opponents consider the hadith to be decisive (qat'i) in meaning.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opponent's dalil
Explain how Hanafi scholars respond: the hadith only prove the necessity of wali's consent (rida wa idhn), not that the woman cannot utter the contract. The repetition in the 'Aisha hadith emphasizes the gravity, not absolute nullity.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad both require wali consent, differing only on the consequence of its absence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Describe the practical difference: In the Hanafi school, the marriage may be valid, suspended, or invalid depending on the narration, but the possibility exists. In Shafi'i/Maliki, the marriage is automatically invalid regardless of consent, and the woman cannot contract at all.
This affects real-world cases where a woman marries without wali involvement.

Chapter of the Guardian and Competence. 70. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa the non-permissibility of it, and upon it is the fatwa of Qadi Khan, i.e., the guardian has the right to object in the case of a non-competent match. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa the non-permissibility of it: i.e., the non-permissibility of marriage from a non-competent match, and upon it is the fatwa of Qadi Khan. Know that a free, sane, adult woman, if she marries herself off, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is valid, and in a narration from Abu Yusuf it is not valid except with a guardian, and according to Muhammad it is valid. [1] His statement: 'And Al-Hasan narrated from him' i.e., Ibn Ziyad; the basis of this narration is the repulsion of harm from the guardians and the corruption of the time. ... (2) His statement: Permissibility of marriage; i.e., if she has a guardian who did not consent to it before the contract, but if she has no guardian then it is valid by consensus. ... (3) His statement: 'And in a narration... etc.' He said in Fath al-Qadir: From Abu Yusuf there are three narrations: (1) not permissible absolutely if she has a guardian, (2) then he retracted to permissibility from a competent match only, (3) then he retracted to permissibility absolutely. And two narrations from Muhammad: (1) its validity is suspended upon the permission of the guardian, if he permits it takes effect, otherwise it is void; (2) a narration of his return to the apparent narration. The conclusion is that now established is the agreement of the three on permissibility absolutely from a competent match and others. This is according to the way we mentioned, which is what al-Sarakhsi mentioned. But according to what al-Tahawi mentioned that the position Abu Yusuf retracted to is non-permissibility except with a guardian, and likewise al-Karkhi in his Mukhtasar, then not. (4) His statement: 'It is not valid except with a guardian'; the difference between it and the statement of al-Shafi'i and Malik is that marriage according to them is not valid by the expression of women, whether she is a proxy or principal, rather her guardian must marry her by his expression; if she marries herself it is absolutely invalid, whether the guardian permits or not. According to Abu Yusuf, the condition is the permission and consent of the guardian, so it is valid by the woman's expression if he is present and consents. (1) Al-Timirtashi chose the narration of al-Hasan in Tanwir al-Absar: and the fatwa is given of its non-permissibility at all due to corruption of the time, and it was supported in Manh al-Ghaffar, and the apparent words of al-Tahtawi in his Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar indicate his choice, and likewise the author of Fath chose it. ... (The text continues with hadith citations and commentary) ... And the response of our scholars, disregarding the criticism of their chains, is that these hadiths do not indicate the invalidity of the marriage contract by the woman's expression; rather they indicate that the consent and permission of the guardian are necessary. For this reason Abu Yusuf and Muhammad chose the condition of it based on the fact that marriage is intended for its purposes, and delegating it to women is detrimental due to their lack of intellect, except that Abu Yusuf relied on the apparent meaning so he ruled that it is not valid except with a guardian, while Muhammad considered that the defect is removed by the guardian's permission so he ruled that it is valid but suspended.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed discussion on the validity of a woman contracting her own marriage without a wali (guardian). It is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute. The author begins by mentioning a narration from al-Hasan (ibn Ziyad) from Abu Hanifa that a marriage to a non-kufu' (non-competent) match is not permissible, and this is the relied-upon fatwa in Qadi Khan. Then he clarifies the well-known ruling: a free, sane, adult woman who marries herself — according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the marriage is valid (in the apparent narration), while a narration from Abu Yusuf says it is not valid except with a wali, and according to Muhammad it is valid. The commentary then details the different narrations from Abu Yusuf (three) and Muhammad (two) and concludes that the established position today is the agreement of the three on permissibility absolutely, based on Sarakhsi's presentation, though Tahawi presents a different retraction. Next, the author distinguishes Abu Yusuf's position from that of Shafi'i and Malik: Shafi'i and Malik hold that a woman cannot contract marriage at all; the wali must do it. Abu Yusuf requires the wali's consent but allows the woman to contract it if the wali is present and consents. The passage then provides the hadith evidence used by the opposing schools ('La nikaha illa bi wali' and the hadith of 'Aisha: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her wali, her marriage is invalid, invalid, invalid') and the Hanafi response: these hadiths only prove the necessity of the wali's consent, not that the woman cannot contract the marriage herself. Hence, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad both require the wali's consent but differ on the effect: Abu Yusuf rules the marriage invalid without explicit wali involvement, while Muhammad rules it suspended pending wali approval.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): The guardian of a woman who has authority over her marriage. In this context, it refers to the person (usually a male relative) whose consent is required for the validity of the marriage.
كفؤ (kufu'): Competence/equality in marriage; the requirement that the husband be suited to the wife in terms of lineage, religion, profession, etc. A non-kufu' marriage may be objected to by the wali.
انعقاد (in'iqad): The formation/validity of the marriage contract. The term is used to discuss whether the contract is legally effective (yan'aqidu) or not.
إجازة (ijazah): Permission or consent given by the wali after the contract; in Muhammad's view, the marriage is suspended (mawquf) pending this permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a woman can contract her own marriage, but wali consent is required; the dispute centers on whether lack of consent invalidates or suspends the contract, and later fatwas may declare it invalid due to corrupted times.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
The Requirement of a Guardian (Wali) for a Woman's Marriage
Inter-madhhab dispute on the requirement of a wali for marriage validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a woman's marriage without a wali, identify the proofs and intra-school differences, and compare it with the Shafi'i/Maliki position.

Core Idea
A woman's marriage is valid without a wali, but the wali may object if she marries someone incompatible.

This lesson examines a key point of disagreement between the Hanafi school and others regarding the role of the guardian (wali) in marriage. The source passage presents the proofs for requiring a wali, the Hanafi response, and the nuanced positions within the Hanafi school itself. By understanding this dispute, you will appreciate how the Hanafi school relies on Quranic texts and rational analogy, and how the different opinions among the Hanafi imams have practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa holds that a woman's marriage is valid (sahih) without a wali, but the wali can object if there is a deficiency in kafa'ah (compatibility) or mahr. The marriage is not invalid without a wali.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools require the wali's presence for the marriage to be valid. Their primary proof is the hadith: 'Any woman who marries without her wali's permission, her marriage is invalid.'
The hadith is reported by multiple sources but has some weakness in its chains, though strengthened by multiple paths.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The hadith 'لا نكاح إلا بولي' and the longer hadith from 'A'isha are cited as explicit evidence that a wali is essential. The opposition argues that these texts are clear and should be taken at face value.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response has two layers: (1) The hadiths indicate the necessity of the wali's consent, not the invalidity of the contract. (2) Abu Hanifa relies on Quranic verses (2:230, 2:232) that attribute the act of marriage to the woman, showing her capacity. Additionally, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differed within the Hanafi school: Abu Yusuf held the marriage is invalid without wali, while Muhammad held it is valid but suspended (mawquf) pending ratification.
The interpretive difference hinges on whether the hadith is taken as negating validity or negating completeness/binding effect.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa), the marriage is valid, but the wali can seek annulment if he objects based on kafa'ah or mahr. According to the Shafi'i/Maliki schools, the marriage is invalid from the start without wali, requiring a new contract.
This difference affects how couples and families approach marriages in practice, especially when a wali is unavailable or objects.

لا ينعقد بعبارة النساء. حجة من قال بهذا حديث: «لا نكاح إلا بولي»، أخرجه أبو داود وابن ماجة والترمذي من حديث أبي موسى الأشعري، وفي سنده كلام بسطه الترمذي والحاكم في مستدركه بعد روايته. وأخرج أبو داود والترمذي وابن ماجة، وابن خزيمة، والحاكم وقال: على شرط الشيخين وابن عدي في «الكامل»، وغيرهم من حديث عائشة رضي الله عنه مرفوعاً: «أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن وليها، فنكاحها باطل، فنكاحها باطل، فنكاحها باطل، فإن دخل بها فلها المهر بما استحل من فرجها، فإن اشتجروا فالسلطان ولي من لا ولي له». وفي الباب أحاديث أخر مخرجة في سنن الدارقطني، والبيهقي، ومعجم الطبراني، ومصنف عبد الرزاق وغيرها، وفي أسانيد أكثرها ضعف، لكنه يجبر بكثرة الطرق. والجواب عن أصحابنا مع قطع النظر عما تكلم في أسانيدها أن هذه الأحاديث لا دلالة لها على عدم انعقاد النكاح بعبارة النساء، نعم هي تدل على أنه لا بد من رضى الولي وإذنه؛ ولهذا اختار أبو يوسف ومحمد اشتراطه بناءً على أن النكاح يراد بمقاصده، والتفويض إليهن مخل لكونهن ناقصات العقل، إلا أن أبا يوسف استند بظاهرها، فحكم بعدم انعقاده إلا بولي، ومحمد نظر إلى أن الخلل يرتفع بإجازة الولي فحكم بانعقاده موقوفاً. وأما أبو حنيفة فاستند بظاهر الآيات التي أضيف فيها النكاح إلى المرأة، قال الله: {فَإِن طَلَقَهَا فَلَا تَحِلُّ لَهُ مِنْ بَعْدُ حَتَّى تَنكِحَ زَوْجًا غَيْرَهُ}، وقال: {فَلَا تَعْضُلُوهُنَّ أَن يَنكِحْنَ أَزْوَاجَهُنَّ إِذَا تَرَاضَوْا بَيْنَهُم بِالْمَعْرُوفِ} مع أن النكاح تصرف في خالص حقها، وهي من أهله؛ لكونها عاقلة بالغة؛ ولهذا أجاز لها التصرف في الأموال واختيار الأزواج اتفاقاً، فلا معنى لعدم انعقاده بعبارتها، ولاشتراط الولي لصحته. غاية ما في الباب أن يكون للولي حق الاعتراض إذا قصرت في أمر بأن تزوجت بغير كفؤ أو بأقل من مهر المثل، والأحاديث المذكورة عنده محمولة على نكاح الغير المكلفة، ومن لا يملك التصرف، أو على نفي الكمال ونفي اللزوم، ويؤيده أن عائشة رضي الله عنها وهي أحد رواة حديث اشتراط الولي زوجت حفصة بنت أخيها عبد الرحمن وهو غائب بالشام، كما أخرجه مالك في الموطأ.
It is not concluded by the woman's own words. The proof of one who says this is the hadith: 'No marriage except with a guardian', reported by Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and al-Tirmidhi from the hadith of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and its chain has discussion which al-Tirmidhi and al-Hakim elaborated in their works. And Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, Ibn Khuzaymah, al-Hakim — and he said it is upon the condition of the two shaykhs [Bukhari and Muslim] — and Ibn 'Adi in al-Kamil, and others reported from 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) marfu'an: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her guardian, her marriage is invalid, her marriage is invalid, her marriage is invalid. If he has consummated with her, then she is entitled to a dowry for what he has made lawful of her private parts. And if they dispute, then the ruler is the guardian of the one who has no guardian.' And on this topic there are other hadiths reported in Sunan al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, Mu'jam al-Tabarani, Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq and others, and in the chains of most of them there is weakness, but it is compensated by the multiplicity of paths. The response from our companions — setting aside the discussion about the chains — is that these hadiths do not indicate the non-conclusion of the marriage by the woman's own words. Yes, they indicate that the consent and permission of the guardian are necessary. For this reason, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad chose its stipulation, based on the fact that marriage is intended for its objectives, and delegating it to women is detrimental because they are deficient in intellect. However, Abu Yusuf adhered to the apparent meaning, so he ruled that it is not concluded except with a guardian, while Muhammad considered that the defect is removed by the guardian's ratification, so he ruled that it is concluded but suspended. As for Abu Hanifah, he adhered to the apparent meaning of the verses in which marriage is attributed to the woman. Allah said: {Then if he divorces her, she is not lawful to him thereafter until she marries another husband}, and He said: {Do not prevent them from marrying their husbands when they agree among themselves in a lawful manner} — even though marriage is an act of disposal over her own exclusive right, and she is qualified for it because she is a sane adult; for this reason, she is permitted to dispose of her wealth and choose husbands by consensus. So there is no meaning to its non-conclusion by her words, nor to stipulating the guardian for its validity. The upshot of the matter is that the guardian has the right to object if she falls short in a matter, such as if she marries someone not equal (kufu') or for less than the proper dowry. The aforementioned hadiths, in his view, are interpreted as referring to the marriage of one who is not legally responsible, or one who does not have the capacity to dispose, or as negating completeness and negating binding effect. This is supported by the fact that 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) — who is one of the narrators of the hadith stipulating the guardian — married Hafsah, the daughter of her brother 'Abd al-Rahman, while he was absent in Syria, as reported by Malik in al-Muwatta'.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the requirement of a guardian (wali) for a woman's marriage. The author is explaining the Hanafi position as stated in the matn: the marriage contract is not concluded by the woman's own words? No, the matn states 'لا ينعقد بعبارة النساء' which is the position of the opposing schools. The commentary clarifies the Hanafi view. The passage begins by presenting the hadith evidence used by those who require a wali: the famous hadith 'لا نكاح إلا بولي' and the longer hadith of 'A'isha stating that marriage without wali is invalid. The author notes that despite weakness in some chains, the hadiths are strengthened by multiple paths. Then comes the Hanafi response: these hadiths do not prove that the woman's own words cannot conclude a marriage, only that the wali's consent is necessary. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani differed: Abu Yusuf held the marriage is completely invalid without wali (following the apparent meaning of the hadith), while Muhammad held it is valid but suspended (mawquf) on the wali's ratification. Abu Hanifa, however, relied on Quranic verses (2:230, 2:232) where marriage is attributed to the woman, indicating her capacity to contract. He argued that since a woman can manage her own wealth, she can also marry herself, but the wali retains the right to object if she makes a harmful choice (e.g., marrying a non-kufu' or for too little dower). The hadiths are interpreted as applying to minors or as negating the ideal (kamal) or binding nature (luzum), not validity. This is supported by 'A'isha's own practice of marrying her niece without the brother's permission. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): The guardian responsible for giving consent in marriage; in this context, the wali has the right to object if the woman acts imprudently.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a contract that is valid but its binding effect is contingent upon ratification by another (e.g., the wali).
كفؤ (kufu'): Equal in status; a potential husband must be socially, religiously, and financially compatible with the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's position on a woman contracting marriage without a wali?
A) The marriage is completely invalid (batil).
B) The marriage is valid but suspended (mawquf) on the wali's ratification.
C) The marriage is valid and binding without any wali involvement.
D) The marriage is valid only if the wali is present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds a woman's marriage is valid without a wali, but the wali retains the right to object based on kafa'ah and mahr considerations.

Consent of a Virgin: Silence, Laughter, and Crying Without Sound
Defining valid indications of a virgin's consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a virgin's consent, distinguish it from the Shafi'i view on compulsion, and identify valid indications of consent using the commentary's conditions.

Core Idea
A virgin's consent is valid through silence, laughter, or silent crying; she cannot be compelled by any guardian.

In this passage, the commentator explains two interconnected rulings: first, that a mature virgin cannot be forced into marriage (ijbar)—a point of difference with Imam al-Shafi'i; second, that her consent can be given through non-verbal cues like silence, laughter, or crying without sound. Understanding these rules is essential for any practicing Muslim seeking to conduct a valid marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The passage first addresses whether a mature virgin (bikr baligha) can be compelled by her guardian. Note that minor virgins and minor previously-married women are compelled by agreement; the dispute is only about the mature virgin.
2
Contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i arguments
The Hanafi position relies on the explicit hadith 'لا ينكح البكر حتى تستأذن' which requires permission. The Shafi'i position uses mafhum al-mukhalafa from the hadith 'الثيب أحق بنفسها من وليها'. Observe how the commentator critiques the Shafi'i proof.
Mafhum al-mukhalafa is not accepted as a proof in Hanafi usul when it contradicts explicit text.
3
List the valid indications of consent
The matn states that silence, laughter, and crying without sound constitute consent. Work through each: silence (supported by multiple hadith), laughter (indicates satisfaction), crying without sound (grief at leaving family).
Each indication has conditions: silence must be voluntary, laughter not mocking, crying without sound.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on crying with sound
Abu Yusuf has two narrations; the preferred opinion is that crying with sound indicates refusal. However, al-Fath and the commentator note that custom should be considered, especially in regions where even slight sound signifies consent.
This illustrates the principle that 'al-urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative) in such matters.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Take a hypothetical: a virgin is asked, remains silent, but later cries silently. According to Hanafi, both silence and silent crying are consent. If she cries loudly, it is generally refusal unless custom dictates otherwise.
This step solidifies the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب الولي والكفؤ
وعند الشَّافِعِيله ثابتة على البكر دون الشَّيب، فالبكر الصغيرة تجبر اتفاقاً، لا الشَّيبُ البالغة اتفاقاً، والبكر البالغةُ لا تجبر عندنا، وتجبر عنده، والنَّيبُ الصَّغيرة تجبر عندنا لا عنده. ثُمَّ عندنا كل ولي فله ولاية الإجبار، وعند الشَّافِعِي له الولي المجبر ليس إلا الأب والجد.
وَصَمْتُها وضِحْكُها وبكاؤها بلا صوت إذن
[١]قوله دون الثيب؛ حجته حديث: «الثيب أحق بنفسها من وليها»، أخرجه مسلم وغيره، فإنّه يدلُّ على أن البكر ليست بأحقِّ، وهو بطريق مفهوم المخالفة الذي یستند به الشافعي، وهو ليس بحجة عند أصحابنا لا سيما إذا خالفه المنطوق الصريح، وهو حديث: «لا ينكح البكر حتى تستأذن» أخرجه الأئمة الستة فإن الاستيذان مناف للإجبار. [٢]قوله: إذن؛ بالرفع: خبر مبتدأ وهو قوله: «صمتُها» مع ما عطف عليه، أما كون الصمت إذناً؛ فلحديث: «لا تنكح الأيم حتى تستأمر، ولا تنكح البكر حتى تستأذن، قالوا يا رسول الله: وكيف إذنها؟ قال: أن تسكت»، أخرجه الأئمة الستة. وفي رواية للشيخين: قلت: فإن البكر تستأمرُ فتستحي فتسكت، قال رسول الله: سكوتها إذنها. وفي رواية لأصحاب السنن ومسلم: «إذنها صماتها». وقد دلت هذه الأحاديث على أن البكر لا تضطر إلى التكلم لكون الحياء غالباً.
ومعه رَدُّ حين استئذانه، أو بعد بلوغ الخبر بشرط تسمية الزوج لا المهر فيهما عليها طبعاً، بل يكتفى بما يدلُّ على رضاها؛ ولهذا كان الضحك والبكاء إذناً لدلالتهما عليه، ويشترط في السكوت أن يكون عن اختيار، فلو أخذها عطاس أو سعال حين الاستئذان فسكتت لم يعتبر ذلك. كذا في «البحر». ويشترط في الضحك أن يكون لا على سبيل الاستهزاء؛ لأنه إنما جعل إذناً لدلالته على الرضاء، فإذا لم يدلّ عليه لم يكن إذناً. كذا في «البحر» أيضاً. وإنما اشترط في البكاء أن يكون بلا صوت؛ لأنه يدلُّ على أنه لحزن على مفارقة أهلها، وإنما يكون ذلك عند الإجازة. كذا في «معراج الدراية». [1]قوله: ومعه؛ أي البكاء مع الصوتِ والاستئذان، ودال على عدم رضاها، وهذا في إحدى الروايتين عن أبي يوسف، وفي أخرى عنه أيضاً: رضى. وذكر في «الذخيرة»: إن المختار للفتوى هو الأول. وقال في «الفتح»: المعوَّلُ اعتبار قرائن الأحوال في البكاء والضحك، فإن تعارضت أو أشكل احتيط. انتهى. وهذا أعدلُ الأقوال، فإن الحكم في أمثال هذا الباب مختلف بحسب اختلاف العرف، ألا ترى إلى أن البكاء مع صوت خفيف أيضاً دال على الإذن في عرف بلاد الهند يعلمه كل من وقف على طرق أهل الهند. كاف.
[٢] قوله: حين استئذانه؛ إضافة إلى الولي: أي حين طلب الولي الإذن من المرأة. [٣] قوله: تسمية الزوج: أي تعيينه بحيث تعرفُها المرأةُ، فإن مجرد ذكرها غير كاف.[٤] قوله: فيهما؛ متعلق بما قبله والضمير إلى الاستئذان وبلوغ الخبر: أي تشترط تسمية الزوج في الصورتين ولا يشترط ذكر المهر فيهما.
The Book of Marriage, Chapter of the Guardian and Compatibility. And according to al-Shafi'i, compulsion is established for the virgin but not the previously married woman. So the minor virgin is compelled by agreement, not the adult previously married by agreement. The adult virgin is not compelled according to us, but is compelled according to him. The minor previously married is compelled according to us, not according to him. Then, according to us, every guardian has the authority of compulsion, and according to al-Shafi'i, the guardian with compulsion is only the father and grandfather. "And her silence, laughter, and crying without sound are consent." [1] His statement: "other than the previously married woman"—his proof is the hadith: "The previously married woman has more right to herself than her guardian," narrated by Muslim and others. It indicates that the virgin does not have more right, and this is by way of the opposite meaning (mafhum al-mukhalafa) which al-Shafi'i relies upon, but it is not a proof according to our companions, especially when contradicted by explicit text, which is the hadith: "The virgin should not be married until she is asked for permission," narrated by the six imams, because asking permission is contrary to compulsion. [2] His statement: "consent"—in the nominative, it is the predicate of a subject which is his statement: "her silence" and what is conjoined with it. As for silence being consent, it is due to the hadith: "The previously married woman should not be married until she is consulted, and the virgin should not be married until she is asked for permission." They said: O Messenger of Allah, how is her permission? He said: "That she remains silent," narrated by the six imams. In a narration by al-Bukhari and Muslim: They said: The virgin is consulted and she feels shy and remains silent, the Messenger of Allah said: "Her silence is her permission." In a narration by the authors of the Sunan and Muslim: "Her permission is her silence." These hadiths indicate that the virgin is not compelled to speak because shyness dominates. And with it, rejection at the time of seeking permission, or after the news reaches, with the condition of naming the husband, not the dowry, in both cases if she does not indicate satisfaction; rather, it suffices what indicates her satisfaction. Hence, laughter and crying are consent because they indicate it. It is required for silence to be voluntary; if a sneeze or cough overcomes her while seeking permission and she is silent, it is not considered. Similarly in al-Bahr. It is required for laughter not to be mockery, because it is made consent due to its indication of satisfaction; if it does not indicate, it is not consent. Similarly in al-Bahr. Crying is conditioned to be without sound because it indicates grief at parting from her family, and that occurs at the time of consent. Similarly in Mi'raj al-Diraya. [1] His statement: "with it"—i.e., crying with sound and seeking permission, and it indicates her dissatisfaction. This is in one of two narrations from Abu Yusuf; in another from him, it is also satisfaction. It is mentioned in al-Dhakhira that the chosen for fatwa is the first. And he said in al-Fath: The relied-upon is consideration of the circumstances regarding crying and laughter; if they conflict or are ambiguous, caution is exercised. End. This is the most just of opinions, because the ruling in such matters varies according to custom; do you not see that crying with a slight sound also indicates permission in the custom of the land of India, known to anyone who has studied the ways of the people of India. Sufficient. [2] His statement: "at the time of seeking his permission"—adding to the guardian: i.e., when the guardian seeks permission from the woman. [3] His statement: "naming the husband"—i.e., specifying him such that the woman knows him, for mere mention is insufficient. [4] His statement: "in both"—related to what precedes, the pronoun referring to seeking permission and the news reaching: i.e., naming the husband is required in both situations, and mentioning the dowry is not required in either.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah (the sharh) discusses the indications of a virgin's consent in marriage. The matn (core text) states that a virgin's silence, laughter, and crying without sound constitute valid consent (idhn). The commentary expands on this ruling. The passage begins by addressing a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i: the Hanafi school holds that a mature virgin (bikr baligha) cannot be compelled (ijbar) by her guardian, while al-Shafi'i holds she can. The Hanafi position is based on the hadith 'The virgin should not be married until she is asked for permission' which directly opposes compulsion. The Shafi'i argument uses the concept of mafhum al-mukhalafa from the hadith 'The previously married woman has more right to herself' implying the virgin does not, but Hanafis reject this as weak when contrasted with explicit text. Next, the commentary explains that silence is considered consent based on authentic hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said for a virgin: 'Her silence is her permission.' Laughter and crying without sound also indicate consent because they show satisfaction or natural shyness. Conditions are detailed: silence must be voluntary (not due to sneeze or cough), laughter must not be mocking, and crying must be without sound (sound indicates refusal). A difference from Abu Yusuf is noted regarding crying with sound: one narration says it indicates refusal, another says it may indicate acceptance. The preferred opinion for fatwa is the first, but the commentator in al-Fath advises relying on context and custom, noting that in India, even crying with a light sound is considered consent. Finally, the passage clarifies that when seeking permission, the husband must be specifically named, but mentioning the dowry is not required.

Key Terms
إجبار (ijbar): Legal compulsion by the guardian to marry without the woman's consent.
بكر (bikr): A woman who has never been married, regardless of virginity status.
إذن (idhn): Permission or consent given by the woman for the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a valid indication of a virgin's consent in the Hanafi school?
A) Voluntary silence
B) Laughter without mockery
C) Crying with a loud sound
D) Crying without sound

Key Takeaway
A virgin's silence, laughter, or silent crying are valid consent; the mature virgin cannot be compelled, as the Hanafi school holds against the Shafi'i.

Conditions of Consent: Silence, Laughter, and Crying
Explaining when silence, laughter, or crying indicate consent for a virgin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a virgin's silence, laughter, or crying constitute valid consent in marriage according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish between valid and invalid indications.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence or laughter indicates consent; crying indicates refusal only if it has sound.

In Hanafi marriage law, the consent of a virgin is often indicated by her reactions when asked. This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains how to interpret her silence, laughter, and crying. Understanding these conditions is crucial for ensuring the validity of the marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the signs of consent
Note that the passage discusses three signs: silence, laughter, and crying. Each has specific conditions.
Silence and laughter are generally consent; crying is ambiguous.
2
Examine the conditions for silence
Silence must be voluntary. If it results from a sneeze or cough, it is not considered consent.
This is from al-Bahr.
3
Examine the conditions for laughter
Laughter must be genuine, not mocking. Mocking laughter does not indicate consent.
Also from al-Bahr.
4
Analyze the crying distinction
Crying without sound indicates consent (grief at leaving family). Crying with sound generally indicates refusal, but there is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
Abu Yusuf has two narrations; the mufta bihi is that crying with sound indicates refusal.
5
Consider the role of custom
The passage notes that custom (urf) can affect the interpretation of crying. In some lands, even slight sound may indicate consent.
The commentator considers this the most balanced approach.

ومعه رَدُّ حين استئذانه ، أو بعد بلوغ الخبر بشرط تسمية الزوج لا المهر فيهما عليها طبعاً، بل يكتفى بما يدلُّ على رضاها ؛ ولهذا كان الضحك والبكاء إذناً لدلالتهما عليه، ويشترط في السكوت أن يكون عن اختيار ، فلو أخذها عطاس أو سعال حين الاستئذان فسكتت لم يعتبر ذلك. كذا في «البحر». ويشترط في الضحك أن يكون لا على سبيل الاستهزاء ؛ لأنه إنما جعل إذناً لدلالته على الرضاء ، فإذا لم يدلّ عليه لم يكن إذناً. كذا في «البحر» أيضاً. وإنما اشترط في البكاء أن يكون بلا صوت ؛ لأنه يدلُّ على أنه لحزن على مفارقة أهلها، وإنما يكون ذلك عند الإجازة. كذا في «معراج الدراية». قوله : ومعه ؛ أي البكاء مع الصوتِ والاستئذان، ودال على عدم رضاها، وهذا في إحدى الروايتين عن أبي يوسف الله ، وفي أخرى عنه له أيضاً : رضى. وذكر في «الذخيرة»: إن المختار للفتوى هو ا وقال في «الفتح» : المعوَّلُ اعتبار قرائن الأحوال في البكاء والضحك، فإن تعارضت أو أشكل احتيط. انتهى. وهذا أعدلُ الأقوال، فإن الحكم في أمثال هذا الباب مختلف بحسب اختلاف العرف، ألا ترى إلى أن البكاء مع صوت خفيف أيضاً دال على الإذن في عرف بلاد الهند يعلمه كل من وقف على طرق أهل الهند. قوله : حين استئذانه ؛ إضافة إلى الولي : أي حين طلب الولي الإذن من المرأة. قوله: تسمية الزوج : أي تعيينه بحيث تعرفُها المرأةُ، فإن مجرد ذكرها غير كاف. قوله : فيهما ؛ متعلق بما قبله والضمير إلى الاستئذان وبلوغ الخبر: أي تشترط تسمية الزوج في الصورتين ولا يشترط ذكر المهر فيهما.
And with it is the response at the time of his seeking permission, or after the news reaches her, on condition of naming the husband, not the dowry in both. Naturally, but whatever indicates her consent suffices; hence laughter and crying are permission because they indicate it. It is required for silence that it be by choice: if a sneeze or cough takes her while seeking permission and she remains silent, it is not considered. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. It is required for laughter that it not be in mockery; for it is made permission only because it indicates satisfaction, so if it does not indicate, it is not permission. Thus in 'al-Bahr' as well. He only required for crying that it be without sound because it indicates grief at parting from her family, and that only occurs at acceptance. Thus in 'Miraj al-Diraya'. His statement: 'and with it' meaning crying with sound and seeking permission, and it indicates her lack of consent. This is in one narration from Abu Yusuf, and in another from him also: consent. It was mentioned in 'al-Dhakhira': the chosen for fatwa is the first. And he said in 'al-Fath': the relied-upon is to consider the circumstances in crying and laughter; if they conflict or are unclear, one is cautious. End. This is the most balanced opinion, for the ruling in such matters varies according to custom. Do you not see that crying with a light sound also indicates permission in the custom of the land of India, known to anyone familiar with the ways of the people of India. His statement: 'at the time of his seeking permission' refers to the guardian: i.e., when the guardian asks permission from the woman. His statement: 'naming the husband' means identifying him so that the woman knows him; merely mentioning him is insufficient. His statement: 'in both' is related to what precedes, and the pronoun refers to seeking permission and the news reaching her: i.e., naming the husband is required in both situations, and mentioning the dowry is not required in either.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah. It explains the conditions under which a virgin's silence, laughter, or crying constitute consent in marriage. The core ruling is that when a guardian seeks permission from a virgin, her silence or laughter indicates consent, provided they are genuine. Silence must be voluntary, not due to a sneeze or cough. Laughter must not be mocking. Crying indicates refusal only if it is with sound; silent crying indicates consent due to grief at leaving family. The text also discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf has two narrations—one that crying with sound indicates refusal, another that it indicates consent. The preferred view for fatwa is the first, but the most balanced approach is to consider all circumstances and custom. The requirement to name the husband is emphasized: the woman must know who she is marrying. However, mentioning the dowry is not required for consent. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), contrary to some later scholars who required mentioning the dowry.

Key Terms
رضاء (rida): Consent of the woman to the marriage, which can be expressed verbally or through indications like silence, laughter, or crying without sound.
تسمية الزوج (tasmiyat al-zawj): Mentioning and identifying the prospective husband by name or description so the woman knows who he is.
مهر (mahr): Dowry given by the husband to the wife; its mention is not required for the validity of consent in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition is required for silence to indicate consent?
A) It must be after mentioning the dowry
B) It must be voluntary and not due to a sneeze or cough
C) It must be accompanied by laughter
D) It must be repeated three times

Key Takeaway
Consent of a virgin is indicated by voluntary silence, genuine laughter, or crying without sound; crying with sound generally indicates refusal, but custom and circumstances are considered.

Consent by Silence in Marriage: The Role of the Non-Closest Guardian
Ruling on consent by silence when the guardian is not the closest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a virgin's silence constitutes valid consent in marriage, and determine when mentioning the husband's name or the dowry is required according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Silence of an adult virgin counts as consent, but only if the husband is named; mentioning the dowry is not required by the correct Hanafi view.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the ruling when a guardian, especially one who is not the closest, seeks a woman's permission for marriage. The key issue is what constitutes valid consent—particularly by silence or laughter—and the conditions that affect its validity. Understanding this is crucial for correctly applying the rules of marriage consent in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the virgin's response (silence or laughter) when the guardian seeks her permission for marriage. The ruling is that it constitutes valid consent (sahih).
The passage states: 'فإذا استأذنها الولي فسكتت أو ضحكت كان رضاً' — if the guardian asks her and she is silent or laughs, it is consent.
2
Locate the condition for valid silence
The passage conditions this consent on the husband being named: 'لكن بشرط تسمية الزوج' — but on condition of naming the husband. If the husband is not named, silence is not consent.
This means the woman must know who she is marrying for her silence to be meaningful acceptance.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on mentioning the mahr
The passage addresses a dispute: some later scholars (mutakhkhirin) required mentioning the dowry (mahr) as well, but the correct view (as per al-Hidayah and the commentator) is that it is not required. The dispute is explained via a distinction: if the guardian is the father or grandfather, mentioning the husband suffices, but if it is someone else, al-Kafi required mentioning the mahr. However, the relied-upon position rejects that requirement.
The phrase 'هو الصحيح' marks the preferred view.
4
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
The operative ruling for fatwa is that silence or laughter is valid consent when the husband is named, and mentioning the dowry is not required. If the woman later rejects after learning the husband, her rejection is valid and the contract is not binding.
This is the position of the majority of Hanafi authorities as reflected in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو الصحيح ) الضمير في صمتها راجع إلى البكر البالغة ، فإذا استأذنها الولي فسكتت أو ضحكت كان رضاً ، وإذا بَلَغَ إليها خبر نكاحها فَسَكَتَتْ، فهو رضاء، لكن بشرط تسمية الزوج حتى لو لم يُذكر الزوج ، فسكونها لا يكون رضاء، ولا يشترط ذِكرُ الهر. (ولو استأذنها غيرُ ولى أقرب قوله : هو الصحيح ؛ احتراز عما قيل : إنه يشترط ذكر المهر أيضاً، وهو قول المتأخرين، وصحح صاحب «الهداية) وغيره عدم الاشتراط ، وذكر في «الكافي»: إنه إن كان الولي أباً وجداً فذِكْرُ الزوج يكفي ؛ لأن الأب لو نقص عن مهر المثل لا يكون إلا لأسلحة تزيد عليه، وإن كان غيرهما فلا بُدَّ من تسمية المهر، وفيه : إن ذلك في تزويجه الصغيرة بحكم الجبر، والكلام في الكبيرة التي وجب مشاورته لها، والأب في ذلك كالأجنبي. قوله : فإذا استأذنها ؛ سواء كان قبل العقد أو بعده ، فإنَّ كان الاستئذان قبلَهُ كان ما يدل على الإذن توكيلاً إن اتحد الوليُّ ، فلو تعدد المزوِّجُ لم يكن سكوتُها إذناً ، وإن كان الاستئذان بعد العقد كان إجازة. كذا في «البحر». قوله : الولي ؛ ليس بقيد احترازي، فإنه لو استأذن وكيل الولى أو رسوله ، فالحكم كذلك، بل هو بناء على أنه المسنون. قوله : بلغ إليها ؛ سواء كان المخبر رسول الولي أو فضولي، ويشترط فيه أن يكون عدلاً إذا كان واحداً. قوله : لا يكون رضاءً ؛ فإن ردّت بعدما علمت بالزوج بعد سكوتها سابقاً عند استئذانها مطلقاً كان ردُّها صحيحاً. قوله : أقرب ؛ صفةُ وَلِيّ : أي استأذنها من ليس بولي أقرب لها فيدخل فيه الأجنبي، وهو من ليس له ولاية كالأب إذا كان كافراً أو عبداً أو مكاتباً، لكن رسول
It is the correct view: the pronoun in 'her silence' refers to the virgin adult. So if the guardian seeks permission from her and she remains silent or laughs, it is consent. And if news of her marriage reaches her and she remains silent, it is consent, but on condition of naming the husband, so if the husband is not mentioned, her silence is not consent, and mentioning the dowry is not required. (And if someone other than the nearest guardian seeks permission from her... His statement: 'it is the correct view' is a precaution against what was said: that mentioning the dowry is also required, which is the statement of the later scholars. The author of al-Hidayah and others corrected that it is not required. It was mentioned in al-Kafi: if the guardian is father or grandfather, mentioning the husband is sufficient, because the father cannot fall short of the proper dowry except for a valid reason, but if it is someone else, then mentioning the dowry is necessary. In it: that is in marrying a minor by force, while the discussion is about an adult who must be consulted, and the father is like a stranger in that. His statement: 'so if he seeks permission from her' whether before the contract or after, if before, what indicates permission is an agency if there is a single guardian, but if there are multiple spouses, her silence is not permission; if after, it is ratification. Thus in al-Bahr. His statement: 'the guardian' is not a restrictive qualifier, for if the guardian's agent or messenger seeks permission, the ruling is the same; rather it is based on what is sunnah. His statement: 'reaches her' whether the informant is the guardian's messenger or an unauthorized person, and it is required that he be upright if he is alone. His statement: 'it is not consent' for if she rejects after knowing the husband following her previous silence at the time of seeking permission, her rejection is valid in any case. His statement: 'nearest' is an adjective of 'guardian': i.e., one who is not a closer guardian seeks permission from her, which includes a stranger, i.e., one who has no guardianship, like a father if he is an unbeliever, a slave, or a mukatib, but the messenger...
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage continues the ruling on consent. It confirms that the correct view is that silence or laughter of an adult virgin after being asked by the guardian constitutes consent. It also states that when news of the marriage reaches her, her silence is consent only if the husband is named; otherwise not. Mentioning the dowry is not required. The commentary then discusses a nuance: if the guardian is the father or grandfather, mentioning the husband suffices even without mentioning the dowry, because they are expected to act in her best interest. But if the guardian is someone else (like a brother), then mentioning the dowry is necessary according to al-Kafi, though the author of al-Hidayah disagrees. The commentator clarifies that this difference applies only when the father marries off a minor; for an adult, the father is like any other guardian. The passage also explains that seeking permission can be before or after the contract; before, it is an appointment of agency; after, it is ratification. If there are multiple spouses (polyandry is invalid in Islam? Actually here it means if there are multiple prospective husbands named), silence does not indicate permission. The guardian mentioned here is not exclusive; agents and messengers also apply. The informant (about the marriage) must be upright if alone. Importantly, if a woman initially remains silent when asked, then later when she knows the husband she rejects, her rejection is valid.

Key Terms
ولي أقرب (wali aqrab): The closest male guardian (e.g., father, grandfather) whose permission is typically sought; a non-closest guardian is anyone else, including a stranger.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification of a marriage contract after it has been conducted by someone without authority; the woman's consent after the fact validates it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Her initial silence is not consent, and her later silence is consent.
B) Her initial silence is consent, but her later silence is not consent because the husband was not named.
C) Both silences are valid consent because she did not object.
D) Neither silence is consent because she did not speak.

Key Takeaway
For a virgin's silence to be valid consent in marriage, the husband must be named; mentioning the dowry is not required by the correct Hanafi view.

Conditions for a Virgin's Silence as Consent in Marriage
Explains conditions for virgin's silence as consent and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a virgin's silence is considered consent in Hanafi law and distinguish the rulings when a non-guardian seeks permission.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence is consent only if the husband is named and permission is sought by her guardian.

In Hanafi marriage law, the consent of a woman is required for a valid marriage. For a virgin, natural shyness often prevents her from speaking openly, so the school accepts her silence as consent—but only under specific conditions. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details those conditions and clarifies when silence is insufficient, such as when a non-guardian seeks permission or when the husband is not named. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the Hanafi position on marriage consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its basis
The passage states that a mature virgin's silence (or laughter) when asked by her guardian constitutes consent (rida). This is based on istihsan due to the natural shyness of virgins.
The proof is the 'prevalence of shyness'—a consideration of custom and human nature.
2
List the conditions for silence to be valid consent
Two conditions are explicitly mentioned: (1) permission must be sought by the guardian (or his agent/ messenger), and (2) the husband must be named. Mentioning the dower is not required, contrary to later scholars.
If the husband is not named, silence is not consent; the woman must verbally accept or reject each suitor.
3
Examine the exception for non-guardians
When a non-guardian (distant relative or stranger) seeks permission, the woman must give verbal consent, like a non-virgin. Silence is insufficient because the natural shyness rationale does not apply to a stranger.
This preserves the woman's right to choose without pressure from someone without authority.
4
Apply the ruling to women whose hymen is lost by non-coital means
A woman whose virginity is removed by a jump, menstruation, injury, prolonged unmarried status (ta'nīs), or fornication is legally considered a virgin (bikr hukman) for consent purposes. Her silence is accepted because she retains shyness.
The rationale is that she is still shy to speak, especially if the loss was due to a forbidden act which she wishes to conceal.
5
Handle the intra-Hanafi dispute on mentioning the dower
Later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirun) required mentioning the dower for silence to be consent, but the relied-upon position (from Zahir al-Riwayah) rejects this requirement. The passage upholds the earlier view, citing al-Hidayah.
The practical effect: in fatwa, only naming the husband is necessary; the dower can be specified later or implied.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
