هو الصحيح ولو استأذنها غير ولي أقرب
هو الصحيح ) (۱) الضمير في صمتها راجع إلى البكر البالغة ، فإذا استأذنها الولي فسكتت أو ضحكت كان رضاً ، وإذا بَلَغَ إليها خبر نكاحها فَسَكَتَتْ، فهو رضاء،
لكن بشرط تسمية الزوج حتى لو لم يُذكر الزوج ، فسكونها لا يكون رضاء، ولا
يشترط ذِكرُ المهر. (ولو استأذنها غيرُ ولى أقرب
[1] قوله : هو الصحيح ؛ احتراز عما قيل : إنه يشترط ذكر المهر أيضاً، وهو قول المتأخرين، وصحح صاحب «الهداية) وغيره عدم الاشتراط ، وذكر في «الكافي»: إنه إن كان الولي أباً وجداً فذِكْرُ الزوج يكفي ؛ لأن الأب لو نقص عن مهر المثل لا يكون إلا لأسلحة تزيد عليه، وإن كان غيرهما فلا بُدَّ من تسمية المهر، وفيه : إن ذلك في تزويجه الصغيرة بحكم الجبر، والكلام في الكبيرة التي وجب مشاورته لها، والأب في ذلك كالأجنبي.
[٢] قوله : فإذا استأذنها ؛ سواء كان قبل العقد أو بعده ، فإنَّ كان الاستئذان قبلَهُ كان ما يدل على الإذن توكيلاً إن اتحد الوليُّ ، فلو تعدد المزوِّجُ لم يكن سكوتُها إذناً ، وإن كان الاستئذان بعد العقد كان إجازة. كذا في «البحر»).
(۳) قوله : الولي ؛ ليس بقيد احترازي، فإنه لو استأذن وكيل الولى أو رسوله ، فالحكم كذلك، بل هو بناء على أنه المسنون. (٤) قوله : بلغ إليها ؛ سواء كان المخبر رسول الولي أو فضولي، ويشترط فيه أن يكون عدلاً إذا كان واحداً.
(٥) قوله : لا يكون رضاءً ؛ فإن ردّت بعدما علمت بالزوج بعد سكوتها سابقاً عند استئذانها مطلقاً كان ردُّها صحيحاً.
(٦) قوله : أقرب ؛ صفةُ وَلِيّ : أي استأذنها من ليس بولي أقرب لها فيدخل فيه الأجنبي، وهو من ليس له ولاية كالأب إذا كان كافراً أو عبداً أو مكاتباً، لكن رسول الولي ووكيله قائم مقامه، وكالقريب الذي ليست له ولاية، ومن هو ولي أبعد كالأخ مع الأب إذا لم يكن الأب غائباً غيبة منقطعة. كذا في «البحر»)، وغيره.
فَرِضَاها بالقول كالنيب ، والزَّائلُ بكارتها بوثبة، أو حيض، أو جراحة، أو تعنيس، أو زِنا بكر حكماً ، وقولُها : رددَتْ أَوْلَى من قوله : سكت فَرِضَاها بالقول كالنيب) : أي لو استأذنها الأجنبي، أو ولي بعيد، فالرّضاءُ لا يكون إلا بالقول كما في التّيب. والزائل بكارتها بوثبة ، أو حيض، أو جراحة، أو تعنيس، أو زناً بكر حكماً : أي لها حكم البكر في أن سكوتها رضا.
(وقولُها : رددت أولى من قوله : سكت) : أي إذا قال الزوج للبكر البالغة (۱) الولي ووكيله قائم مقامه، وكالقريب الذي ليست له ولاية، ومن هو ولي أبعد كالأخ مع الأب إذا لم يكن الأب غائباً غيبة منقطعة. كذا في «البحر»)، وغيره.
[١] قوله : بالقول ؛ يعني لا بالضحك والبكاء، ويلحق بالقــول مـا ينوب منابه كطلب مهرها ونفقتها وتمكينها من الوطء ودخوله بها برضاها وقبول التهنئة والضحك سروراً. كذا في «تنوير الأبصار»(۳).
[٢] قوله : بكارتها: - بالكسر - : المراد بها الجلدة الرقيقة الحاجزة بين الفرج الخارج والداخل تزول بالوطء وغيره.
[٣] قوله : أو تعنيس ؛ يقال : عنست الجارية تعنس بضم النون عنيساً وعناساً، فهي عانس إذا طال مكتها بعد إدراكها في منزل أهلها حتى خرجت عن عداد الأبكار.
[٤] قوله : في أن سكوتها ؛ وكذا ضحكها ونحوه رضاء ؛ وذلك لأنه إنما اكتفى به في البكر ولم يجب القول لغلبة الحياء على الأبكار طبعاً بسبب عدم مصاحبة الرجال، وهو موجود فيمن زالت بكارتها لأمر غير الوطء : كالجرح ونحوه، وكذا فيمن صارت ثيبة بالزنا ؛ لأنها تهتم باختفاء فعلها المُحَرِّم ، والناس يصفونها بالبكر فتستحي مـن القول.
Book of Marriage, Chapter of Guardian and Compatibility. It is the correct opinion. And if someone other than the nearest guardian seeks her permission. (It is the correct opinion) (1) The pronoun in 'her silence' refers to the mature virgin. So if the guardian seeks her permission and she is silent or laughs, it is consent. And if news of her marriage reaches her and she is silent, it is consent. But on condition of naming the husband, to the extent that if the husband is not mentioned, her silence is not consent. And mentioning the dower is not required. (And if someone other than the nearest guardian seeks her permission). [1] His saying: 'It is the correct opinion' is a precaution against what was said that mentioning the dower is also required, which is the saying of the later scholars. The author of al-Hidayah and others declared the non-requirement as correct. And it is mentioned in al-Kafi: If the guardian is a father or grandfather, mentioning the husband is sufficient because the father, if he reduces from the proper dower, does so only for something that increases it. If he is other than them, mentioning the dower is necessary. But it is said: That is in the case of marrying a minor by compulsion, but the discussion is about an adult who must be consulted, and the father in that regard is like a stranger. [2] His saying: 'When he seeks her permission' applies whether before the contract or after. If the seeking of permission is before it, then what indicates permission is a proxy if the guardian is single. If there are multiple husbands, her silence is not permission. If the seeking of permission is after the contract, it is ratification. As in al-Bahr. (3) His saying: 'The guardian' is not a restrictive condition, for if the guardian's agent or messenger seeks permission, the ruling is the same. Rather, it is based on what is Sunnah. (4) His saying: 'Reaches her' applies whether the informant is the guardian's messenger or a stranger, and it is required that he be upright if he is one. (5) His saying: 'It is not consent' because if she rejects after knowing the husband after her previous silence at the time of being asked, in all cases her rejection is valid. (6) His saying: 'Nearest' is a description of the guardian, i.e., someone who is not a nearer guardian to her seeks her permission. This includes a stranger, i.e., one who has no guardianship, such as a father who is an infidel, slave, or mukatab. But the guardian's messenger and agent stand in his place. And also a relative who has no guardianship, and one who is a more distant guardian, like a brother with the father if the father is not absent in a complete absence. As in al-Bahr and others. So her consent is by speech like a non-virgin. And one whose virginity is lost by a jump, or menstruation, or injury, or being unmarried (ta'nis), or fornication is a virgin by legal ruling. And her saying 'I refused' takes precedence over his saying 'She was silent'. (So her consent is by speech like a non-virgin): i.e., if a stranger or a distant guardian seeks permission, consent is only by speech as in the case of a non-virgin. And the one whose virginity is lost by a jump, or menstruation, or injury, or being unmarried (ta'nis), or fornication is a virgin by legal ruling: i.e., she has the ruling of a virgin in that her silence is consent. (And her saying 'I refused' takes precedence over his saying 'She was silent'): i.e., if the husband says for the mature virgin... [1] His saying: 'By speech' means not by laughter or crying. And what substitutes for speech is included, such as asking for her dower and maintenance, allowing intercourse, entering upon her with her consent, accepting congratulations, and laughing joyfully. As in Tanwir al-Absar. [2] His saying: 'Her virginity' (bikarah) refers to the thin membrane separating the external and internal vagina, which is removed by intercourse and other things. [3] His saying: 'Or being unmarried (ta'nis)'; it is said: 'anisat al-jariyah ta'nus' with damm on the noon, meaning she became 'anis' or 'anus', i.e., she is a woman who has remained long after puberty in her family's home until she left the count of virgins. [4] His saying: 'That her silence' and similarly her laughter and the like is consent. This is because they (the jurists) sufficed with it for the virgin and did not require speech due to the prevalence of shyness in virgins naturally, because of not associating with men. And this exists in one whose virginity is removed by something other than intercourse, such as injury and the like. Similarly, in one who became a non-virgin by fornication, because she is concerned with hiding her prohibited act, and people describe her as a virgin, so she is too shy to speak.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on the consent of a virgin in marriage. The author clarifies that the correct opinion (al-sahih) is that a mature virgin's silence is considered consent when her guardian seeks her permission, but only if the husband is specifically named. If the husband is not mentioned, her silence is not consent. The mention of the dower (mahr) is not required, contrary to later scholars. The commentary distinguishes between the father/grandfather and other guardians: a father may reduce the dower but compensates with other benefits, so only naming the husband suffices; other guardians must specify the dower. The discussion is about an adult virgin who must be consulted; the father in this case is like a stranger. The permission-seeking may occur before or after the contract: before, it constitutes proxy; after, it is ratification. If multiple husbands are proposed, silence is not consent. The guardian's agent or messenger has the same effect. The informant about the marriage must be upright if alone. If the woman later rejects after knowing the husband, her rejection is valid despite earlier silence. The term 'nearest guardian' excludes strangers and distant relatives without guardianship. When a non-guardian or distant guardian seeks permission, the woman must give verbal consent, like a non-virgin. However, a woman whose hymen is lost by jump, menstruation, injury, remaining unmarried, or fornication is considered a virgin legally, so her silence suffices. Finally, if there is a dispute between the woman and the husband about whether she refused or remained silent, the woman's word is taken because she would know her own state, and her refusal is given precedence over his claim of silence. The proof for silence as consent is the natural shyness of virgins, which also applies to those whose virginity is lost through non-coital means or zina, as they are still shy to speak.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukut): Silence; in this context, the silence of a mature virgin after being asked by her guardian or after hearing of her marriage is considered consent (rida), with conditions.
بكر (bikr): Virgin; a woman who has never had lawful or unlawful intercourse (though legally a virgin if hymen lost by other means).
تعنيس (ta'nīs): Remaining unmarried in her family's home long after reaching puberty, such that she leaves the category of virgins in common custom.
نيب (thayyib): Non-virgin; a woman who has had lawful intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum requirement for a virgin's silence to be considered consent when her guardian seeks permission?
A) The guardian must mention both the groom's name and the dower amount.
B) The guardian must mention the groom's name; the dower need not be specified.
C) The guardian need only mention the dower; the groom's name is not necessary.
D) The guardian must obtain explicit verbal consent regardless of conditions.

Key Takeaway
A virgin's silence is consent only if her guardian names the husband; mentioning the dower is not required, and non-guardians must obtain verbal consent.

Nikah — Marriage

Consent of a Virgin: Silence and Verbal Consent
Ruling on virgin's consent and legal definition of virgin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a virgin's silence is considered consent and identify which women are legally classified as virgins for consent rulings.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence is consent only when proposed by a proper guardian; a woman whose hymen is broken by non-intercourse causes is still a virgin for this ruling.

In Hanafi marriage law, the consent of a virgin is handled with sensitivity to her modesty. This lesson explains the key ruling: a virgin's silence can be taken as consent when her father or brother proposes, but when a stranger or distant guardian seeks her hand, she must verbally state her acceptance. Additionally, the definition of 'virgin' includes women who have lost their hymen through jumping, menstruation, injury, spinsterhood, or even fornication, because the underlying shyness remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on consent of a virgin in marriage. The ruling is that silence is considered consent when the proposal comes from a proper guardian (father or brother), but must be verbal for a stranger or distant guardian.
The matn states 'فَرِضَاها بالقول كالثيب' for strangers.
2
Define the subject – who is a 'virgin' legally
The passage extends the definition of virgin to include women whose hymen is broken by non-intercourse causes (jump, menstruation, wound, spinsterhood, fornication). They have the legal ruling of a virgin for consent.
This is based on the underlying attribute of shyness.
3
Note the condition for silence to suffice
Silence suffices only when the proposing party is a proper guardian (father or brother). For others, verbal consent is required.
If a stranger proposes, explicit speech is necessary.
4
Understand the rationale
The reason is the natural shyness of virgins due to lack of association with men. This remains even if the hymen is broken by non-intercourse.
The commentary emphasizes that this shyness exists in the legally defined group.

كتاب النكاح باب الولي والكفؤ
فَرِضَاها بالقول كالثيب ، والزائلُ بكارتها بوثبة، أو حيض، أو جراحة، أو تعنيس، أو زنا بكر حكماً ، وقولها : رددت أولى من قوله : سكت
فَرِضَاها بالقول كالثيب) : أي لو استأذنها الأجنبي، أو ولي بعيد، فالرِّضاء لا يكون إلا بالقول كما في الثيب. والزائل بكارتها بوثبة ، أو حيض، أو جراحة، أو تعنيس، أو زنا بكر حكماً) : أي لها حكم البكر في أن سكوتها رضا.
(وقولها : رددت أولى من قوله : سكت) : أي إذا قال الزوج للبكر البالغة الولي ووكيله قائم مقامه، وكالقريب الذي ليست له ولاية، ومن هو ولي أبعد كالأخ مع الأب إذا لم يكن الأب غائباً غيبة منقطعة. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره.
[1] قوله : بالقول ؛ يعني لا بالضحك والبكاء، ويلحق بالقول ما ينوب منابه كطلب مهرها ونفقتها وتمكينها من الوطء ودخوله بها برضاها وقبول التهنئة والضحك سروراً. كذا في «تنوير الأبصار».
[2] قوله : بكارتها: - بالكسر - : المراد بها الجلدة الرقيقة الحاجزة بين الفرج الخارج والداخل تزول بالوطء وغيره.
[3] قوله : أو تعنيس ؛ يقال : عنست الجارية تعنس بضم النون عنيساً وعناساً، فهي عانس إذا طال مكتها بعد إدراكها في منزل أهلها حتى خرجت عن عداد الأبكار.
[4] قوله : في أن سكوتها ؛ وكذا ضحكها ونحوه رضاء ؛ وذلك لأنه إنما اكتفى به في البكر ولم يجب القول لغلبة الحياء على الأبكار طبعاً بسبب عدم مصاحبة الرجال، وهو موجود فيمن زالت بكارتها لأمر غير الوطء : كالجرح ونحوه، وكذا فيمن صارت ثيبة بالزنا ؛ لأنها تهتم باختفاء فعلها المُحَرِّم ، والناس يصفونها بالبكر فتستحي من القول.
(1) قيد بالبالغة ؛ لأنها إذا كانت صغيرة وزوجها الولي ثم أدركت وادَّعت ردَّ النكاح حين بلغت وكذبها الزوج كان القول قوله. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر»(1 : 335).
(2) «البحر الرائق» (3 : 127).
(3) «الدر المختار شرح تنوير الأبصار» (3 : 124).

تُقْبَلُ بَيْنَتُهُ على سكوتها
بلغك النّكاحُ فسكت وقالت : لا بل رددت، فالقول قولها .
و تُقْبَلُ بَيْنَتُهُ على سكوتها
[ ] قوله : فالقول قولها ؛ هذه العبارة في مسائل الدعوى عندهم كناية عن كونه مُنْكِراً، فكلُّ مَن قالوا : إن القول قولهُ مرادهم به أنه منكر، والآخر مدع فيطالبُ المدعي بالشهود، فإن أتى بهم ثبت دعواه وإلا يحلفُ المَنْكِر ، وليس المراد به قبول قولهِ من غير حجة.
والحاصل أنه إذا وقع النزاع بين المتزوجين ، فقال الزوج لها - وهي بكر بالغة - سكت عند بلوغ خبر النكاح إليكِ، فتم النكاح بيني وبينك وقالت : رددت - أي بالقول أو بما يدلُّ به كالصوت مع البكاء فلا نكاح بيني وبينك - .
ففي الظاهر كل منهما مدَّع ، الزوج يدعي سكوتها، وهي تدعي ردها، لكن بعد التأملِ يُعْلَمُ أن المدعي هو الزوج، فإن المدعي هو الذي يثبت حقه على الغير، ومن يدفعه عن نفسه يكون منكراً لإنكاره ما ألزمه الآخر.
ومن المعلوم أن الزوج مقصوده إثبات ملكه عليها، واستحقاق أخذ منافع بضعها، وهي تدفع عن نفسها ، فتكون منكرةً ، فيُطَالَبُ الزوج بالبينة على السكوت، فإن أتى بها قُبِلَت وثبت النكاح.
فإن قلت : البينة تقام على الإثبات ، والسكوت عدم التكلم، فهو نفي. قلت : السكوت أمر وجودي ، فإنه عبارة عن ضم الشفة بالشفة، والنفي من لوازمه، فتقبل البينة على هذا الوجودي، ويثبت لازمه بثبوت ملزومه. (2) قوله : فالقول قولها ؛ فيه خلافُ زفره ، فإنّه يقول : السكوت أصل، والرد عارض، والقول قولُ مَن يتمسك بالأصل. وجوابه ظاهر، فإن التمسك بالأصل معنى هو المرأة، وهو عدم ثبوت ملك الزوج عليها.
[3]قوله : على سكوتها ؛ ولو أقامت هي البينة على الرد، وهو على السكوت، (1) قبل أن يكون دخل بها طوعاً في الأصح. كما في «الدر المختار» وحاشيته لابن عابدين (302 : 2)
Book of Marriage, Chapter of Guardian and Compatibility. Her consent is by speech like the thayyib, and the one whose virginity has been removed by a jump, or menstruation, or a wound, or being spinstered, or fornication is a virgin legally. And her statement 'I rejected' is given precedence over his statement 'she was silent'. (Her consent is by speech like the thayyib): meaning if a stranger or a remote guardian seeks her permission, then consent is only by speech as in the thayyib. And the one whose virginity has been removed by a jump, or menstruation, or a wound, or being spinstered, or fornication is a virgin legally: meaning she has the ruling of a virgin in that her silence is consent. (And her statement 'I rejected' is given precedence over his statement 'she was silent'): meaning when the husband says to the mature virgin (the guardian and his agent stand in his place, and similarly the relative who has no guardianship, and the more distant guardian like the brother with the father if the father is not absent in a detached absence). As in 'al-Bahr' and others. [1] His statement: 'by speech'; meaning not by laughing or crying, and what takes its place joins to speech like demanding her dowry and maintenance, allowing intercourse, entering upon her with her consent, accepting congratulations, and laughing joyfully. As in 'Tanwir al-Absar'. [2] His statement: 'her virginity' - with a kasra - meaning the thin skin barrier between the outer and inner private part that is removed by intercourse and other things. [3] His statement: 'or being spinstered'; it is said: 'anasa al-jariyatu ta'nusu bi-damm al-nun 'unusan wa 'unas, so she is 'anis if her stay in her family's home is prolonged after puberty until she exits from the category of virgins. [4] His statement: 'that her silence'; and likewise her laughter and similar is consent; that is because he sufficed with it for the virgin and speech was not required due to the predominance of shyness in virgins by nature because of not associating with men, and this exists in one whose virginity was removed by other than intercourse like a wound and similar, and similarly one who became a thayyib by fornication; because she is concerned with hiding her unlawful act, and people describe her as a virgin, so she is shy of speech. (1) Restricted to the mature; because if she is a minor and the guardian married her, then she reached puberty and claimed rejection of the marriage when she attained maturity and the husband denied her, then the saying is his. See: 'Majma' al-Anhur' (1:335). (2) 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (3:127). (3) 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar Sharh Tanwir al-Absar' (3:124). His evidence is accepted regarding her silence. The news of the marriage reached you and you were silent and she said: No, rather I rejected, then her statement is hers. And his evidence is accepted regarding her silence. [ ] His statement: 'then her statement is hers'; this phrase in claim issues with them is a metaphor for his being a denier, so everyone of whom they said 'the saying is his' means he is a denier, and the other is a plaintiff; so the plaintiff is asked for witnesses, if he brings them his claim is proven, otherwise the denier swears, and it does not mean accepting his statement without proof. The summary is that when a dispute occurs between the spouses, and the husband says to her - and she is a mature virgin - 'you were silent when the news of the marriage reached you, so the marriage between me and you is complete' and she said 'I rejected' - meaning by speech or by what indicates like a voice with crying - 'so there is no marriage between me and you'. So in the apparent, each of them is a plaintiff; the husband claims her silence, and she claims her rejection. However, after reflection it is known that the plaintiff is the husband, for the plaintiff is the one who establishes his right against someone else, and the one who defends himself is a denier for his denial of what the other imposes. And it is known that the husband's aim is to establish his ownership over her and the right to take the benefits of her private part, and she defends herself, so she is a denier, so the husband is asked for evidence on the silence, if he brings it, it is accepted and the marriage is proven. If you say: evidence is established on affirmation, and silence is not speaking, so it is a negation. I say: silence is an existential matter, for it is the pressing of lip to lip, and negation is one of its concomitants, so evidence is accepted on this existential, and its necessary consequent is established by the establishment of its necessary condition. (2) His statement: 'then her statement is hers'; there is a disagreement of Zufar about it, for he says: silence is the original state, and rejection is an exception, and the saying is of the one who holds the original. And its answer is apparent, for holding the original from the woman's perspective is the non-establishment of the husband's ownership over her. [3] His statement: 'regarding her silence'; and if she produces evidence on the rejection, and he on the silence, (1) before he has intercourse with her willingly in the correct view. As in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' and its gloss by Ibn Abidin (302:2).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Umdat al-Ri`ayah expanding on the matn of Sharh al-Wiqayah regarding the consent of a virgin in marriage. It covers three main points: (1) when a virgin's consent must be verbal (when asked by a stranger or remote guardian), (2) the legal status of a woman whose hymen is broken by non-intercourse causes (she is treated as a virgin in terms of consent), (3) the procedure when a dispute arises between husband and wife about whether she consented by silence or rejected verbally.

The matn states: "Her consent is by speech like the thayyib, and the one whose virginity has been removed by a jump, or menstruation, or a wound, or spinsterhood, or fornication is legally a virgin. And her statement 'I rejected' is given precedence over his statement 'she was silent'." The commentary explains each part.

First: If a stranger or a distant guardian (like a paternal cousin) seeks a woman's permission for marriage, her consent must be expressed verbally even if she is a virgin. This is because the guardian is not her father or brother, so the natural shyness that justifies silence is not present to the same degree.

Second: A woman who has lost her hymen due to actions other than lawful intercourse—such as jumping, menstruation, injury, prolonged spinsterhood, or even fornication—still retains the legal status of a virgin in this ruling. Her silence is considered consent because she still experiences the shyness typical of virgins; the fornication case is included because she wants to hide her sinful act and people still consider her a virgin.

Third: The core procedural rule: if a husband claims his wife (a mature virgin) remained silent upon learning of the marriage proposal, and she claims she explicitly rejected, her statement is taken over his. The commentary clarifies that this is not about accepting her word without evidence; rather it determines who is the plaintiff and who is the defendant in a lawsuit. The husband is the plaintiff because he seeks to establish his marital rights, while the wife is the defendant denying his claim. Thus, the burden of proof is on the husband to bring evidence that she was silent. If he fails, her oath suffices.

The commentary also addresses a subtle point: silence is not a pure negation; it is an existential act (pressing lips together), so evidence can be brought to prove it.

Finally, the passage notes a difference with Zufar, who argued that silence is the original state and rejection is an exception, so the husband's claim should be taken. The response is that the original from the woman's perspective is the absence of the husband's ownership, which she is defending.

This passage is a good example of Hanafi procedural fiqh: it blends substantive rulings on consent with the rules of evidence and burden of proof.

Key Terms
بكر (bikr): A woman who has never had lawful intercourse (the hymen may be broken by other causes, but she is still legally a virgin for consent rulings).
سكوت (sukūt): Silence; legally considered consent for a virgin when the proposal is made by a proper guardian (father or brother) due to modesty.
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has had lawful intercourse; her consent must always be verbal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following women is considered a virgin for the purpose of the consent-by-silence ruling in the Hanafi school?
A) A woman whose hymen was broken by lawful intercourse.
B) A woman whose hymen was broken by a fall.
C) A woman who has been previously married and divorced.
D) A woman who has given birth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies women with non-intercourse hymen loss as legal virgins for consent, and their silence is consent only when a proper guardian proposes.

Nikah — Marriage

Dispute Resolution in Consent: Burden of Proof
Procedural rule for dispute over consent of a virgin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of burden of proof when a husband and wife disagree over whether consent was given by silence during marriage proposal.

Core Idea
The wife's claim of rejection is accepted over the husband's claim of silence because the husband is the plaintiff seeking to establish ownership.

When a marriage is contested on the basis of consent, the Hanafi school employs a clear procedural rule. This lesson examines the scenario where a virgin wife claims she verbally rejected the proposal, while her husband insists she was silent. The ruling that her word is taken (with an oath) is based on an analysis of who is the plaintiff and who is the defendant. Understanding this rule is crucial for proper adjudication of such disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The husband claims the wife (mature virgin) was silent upon hearing the proposal, so the marriage is valid. The wife claims she rejected verbally or with a clear sign like crying.
The text states: 'بلغك النكاح فسكت وقالت: لا بل رددت'.
2
Determine who is the plaintiff
The plaintiff is the one who seeks to establish a right against another. Here, the husband aims to prove marriage (ownership of her person), so he is the plaintiff. The wife defends against this claim, so she is the defendant.
The commentary says: 'المدعي هو الزوج'.
3
Apply the rule of burden of proof
The plaintiff (husband) must bring evidence (witnesses) that she was silent. If he fails, the defendant (wife) takes an oath that she rejected, and the marriage is not proven.
The text: 'فالقول قولها' means her statement is accepted, i.e., she is the denier.
4
Address a subtle point: silence is existential
One might argue silence is a negation, so evidence cannot be brought. The response: silence is the pressing of lips, an existential act; its concomitant is absence of speech. Evidence can attest to the existential act.
The commentary: 'السكوت أمر وجودي'.
5
Note Zufar's disagreement and the response
Zufar held that silence is the original state (asl), so the one who holds the original (husband) should have his statement accepted. The Hanafi response: the original from the woman's perspective is absence of the husband's ownership, which she defends. Therefore, the husband's claim is an exception.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text

فَرِضَاها بالقول كالثيب) : أي لو استأذنها الأجنبي، أو ولي بعيد، فالرِّضاء لا يكون إلا بالقول كما في الثيب. والزائل بكارتها بوثبة ، أو حيض، أو جراحة، أو تعنيس، أو زنا بكر حكماً) : أي لها حكم البكر في أن سكوتها رضا.
(وقولها : رددت أولى من قوله : سكت) : أي إذا قال الزوج للبكر البالغة الولي ووكيله قائم مقامه، وكالقريب الذي ليست له ولاية، ومن هو ولي أبعد كالأخ مع الأب إذا لم يكن الأب غائباً غيبة منقطعة. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره.
[1] قوله : بالقول ؛ يعني لا بالضحك والبكاء، ويلحق بالقول ما ينوب منابه كطلب مهرها ونفقتها وتمكينها من الوطء ودخوله بها برضاها وقبول التهنئة والضحك سروراً. كذا في «تنوير الأبصار».
[2] قوله : بكارتها: - بالكسر - : المراد بها الجلدة الرقيقة الحاجزة بين الفرج الخارج والداخل تزول بالوطء وغيره.
[3] قوله : أو تعنيس ؛ يقال : عنست الجارية تعنس بضم النون عنيساً وعناساً، فهي عانس إذا طال مكتها بعد إدراكها في منزل أهلها حتى خرجت عن عداد الأبكار.
[4] قوله : في أن سكوتها ؛ وكذا ضحكها ونحوه رضاء ؛ وذلك لأنه إنما اكتفى به في البكر ولم يجب القول لغلبة الحياء على الأبكار طبعاً بسبب عدم مصاحبة الرجال، وهو موجود فيمن زالت بكارتها لأمر غير الوطء : كالجرح ونحوه، وكذا فيمن صارت ثيبة بالزنا ؛ لأنها تهتم باختفاء فعلها المُحَرِّم ، والناس يصفونها بالبكر فتستحي من القول.
(1) قيد بالبالغة ؛ لأنها إذا كانت صغيرة وزوجها الولي ثم أدركت وادَّعت ردَّ النكاح حين بلغت وكذبها الزوج كان القول قوله. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر»(1 : 335).
(2) «البحر الرائق» (3 : 127).
(3) «الدر المختار شرح تنوير الأبصار» (3 : 124).

تُقْبَلُ بَيْنَتُهُ على سكوتها
بلغك النّكاحُ فسكت وقالت : لا بل رددت، فالقول قولها .
و تُقْبَلُ بَيْنَتُهُ على سكوتها
[ ] قوله : فالقول قولها ؛ هذه العبارة في مسائل الدعوى عندهم كناية عن كونه مُنْكِراً، فكلُّ مَن قالوا : إن القول قولهُ مرادهم به أنه منكر، والآخر مدع فيطالبُ المدعي بالشهود، فإن أتى بهم ثبت دعواه وإلا يحلفُ المَنْكِر ، وليس المراد به قبول قولهِ من غير حجة.
والحاصل أنه إذا وقع النزاع بين المتزوجين ، فقال الزوج لها - وهي بكر بالغة - سكت عند بلوغ خبر النكاح إليكِ، فتم النكاح بيني وبينك وقالت : رددت - أي بالقول أو بما يدلُّ به كالصوت مع البكاء فلا نكاح بيني وبينك - .
ففي الظاهر كل منهما مدَّع ، الزوج يدعي سكوتها، وهي تدعي ردها، لكن بعد التأملِ يُعْلَمُ أن المدعي هو الزوج، فإن المدعي هو الذي يثبت حقه على الغير، ومن يدفعه عن نفسه يكون منكراً لإنكاره ما ألزمه الآخر.
ومن المعلوم أن الزوج مقصوده إثبات ملكه عليها، واستحقاق أخذ منافع بضعها، وهي تدفع عن نفسها ، فتكون منكرةً ، فيُطَالَبُ الزوج بالبينة على السكوت، فإن أتى بها قُبِلَت وثبت النكاح.
فإن قلت : البينة تقام على الإثبات ، والسكوت عدم التكلم، فهو نفي. قلت : السكوت أمر وجودي ، فإنه عبارة عن ضم الشفة بالشفة، والنفي من لوازمه، فتقبل البينة على هذا الوجودي، ويثبت لازمه بثبوت ملزومه. (2) قوله : فالقول قولها ؛ فيه خلافُ زفره ، فإنّه يقول : السكوت أصل، والرد عارض، والقول قولُ مَن يتمسك بالأصل. وجوابه ظاهر، فإن التمسك بالأصل معنى هو المرأة، وهو عدم ثبوت ملك الزوج عليها.
[3]قوله : على سكوتها ؛ ولو أقامت هي البينة على الرد، وهو على السكوت، (1) قبل أن يكون دخل بها طوعاً في الأصح. كما في «الدر المختار» وحاشيته لابن عابدين (302 : 2)
Book of Marriage, Chapter of Guardian and Compatibility. Her consent is by speech like the thayyib, and the one whose virginity has been removed by a jump, or menstruation, or a wound, or being spinstered, or fornication is a virgin legally. And her statement 'I rejected' is given precedence over his statement 'she was silent'. (Her consent is by speech like the thayyib): meaning if a stranger or a remote guardian seeks her permission, then consent is only by speech as in the thayyib. And the one whose virginity has been removed by a jump, or menstruation, or a wound, or being spinstered, or fornication is a virgin legally: meaning she has the ruling of a virgin in that her silence is consent. (And her statement 'I rejected' is given precedence over his statement 'she was silent'): meaning when the husband says to the mature virgin (the guardian and his agent stand in his place, and similarly the relative who has no guardianship, and the more distant guardian like the brother with the father if the father is not absent in a detached absence). As in 'al-Bahr' and others. [1] His statement: 'by speech'; meaning not by laughing or crying, and what takes its place joins to speech like demanding her dowry and maintenance, allowing intercourse, entering upon her with her consent, accepting congratulations, and laughing joyfully. As in 'Tanwir al-Absar'. [2] His statement: 'her virginity' - with a kasra - meaning the thin skin barrier between the outer and inner private part that is removed by intercourse and other things. [3] His statement: 'or being spinstered'; it is said: 'anasa al-jariyatu ta'nusu bi-damm al-nun 'unusan wa 'unas, so she is 'anis if her stay in her family's home is prolonged after puberty until she exits from the category of virgins. [4] His statement: 'that her silence'; and likewise her laughter and similar is consent; that is because he sufficed with it for the virgin and speech was not required due to the predominance of shyness in virgins by nature because of not associating with men, and this exists in one whose virginity was removed by other than intercourse like a wound and similar, and similarly one who became a thayyib by fornication; because she is concerned with hiding her unlawful act, and people describe her as a virgin, so she is shy of speech. (1) Restricted to the mature; because if she is a minor and the guardian married her, then she reached puberty and claimed rejection of the marriage when she attained maturity and the husband denied her, then the saying is his. See: 'Majma' al-Anhur' (1:335). (2) 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (3:127). (3) 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar Sharh Tanwir al-Absar' (3:124). His evidence is accepted regarding her silence. The news of the marriage reached you and you were silent and she said: No, rather I rejected, then her statement is hers. And his evidence is accepted regarding her silence. [ ] His statement: 'then her statement is hers'; this phrase in claim issues with them is a metaphor for his being a denier, so everyone of whom they said 'the saying is his' means he is a denier, and the other is a plaintiff; so the plaintiff is asked for witnesses, if he brings them his claim is proven, otherwise the denier swears, and it does not mean accepting his statement without proof. The summary is that when a dispute occurs between the spouses, and the husband says to her - and she is a mature virgin - 'you were silent when the news of the marriage reached you, so the marriage between me and you is complete' and she said 'I rejected' - meaning by speech or by what indicates like a voice with crying - 'so there is no marriage between me and you'. So in the apparent, each of them is a plaintiff; the husband claims her silence, and she claims her rejection. However, after reflection it is known that the plaintiff is the husband, for the plaintiff is the one who establishes his right against someone else, and the one who defends himself is a denier for his denial of what the other imposes. And it is known that the husband's aim is to establish his ownership over her and the right to take the benefits of her private part, and she defends herself, so she is a denier, so the husband is asked for evidence on the silence, if he brings it, it is accepted and the marriage is proven. If you say: evidence is established on affirmation, and silence is not speaking, so it is a negation. I say: silence is an existential matter, for it is the pressing of lip to lip, and negation is one of its concomitants, so evidence is accepted on this existential, and its necessary consequent is established by the establishment of its necessary condition. (2) His statement: 'then her statement is hers'; there is a disagreement of Zufar about it, for he says: silence is the original state, and rejection is an exception, and the saying is of the one who holds the original. And its answer is apparent, for holding the original from the woman's perspective is the non-establishment of the husband's ownership over her. [3] His statement: 'regarding her silence'; and if she produces evidence on the rejection, and he on the silence, (1) before he has intercourse with her willingly in the correct view. As in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' and its gloss by Ibn Abidin (302:2).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Umdat al-Ri`ayah expanding on the matn of Sharh al-Wiqayah regarding the consent of a virgin in marriage. It covers three main points: (1) when a virgin's consent must be verbal (when asked by a stranger or remote guardian), (2) the legal status of a woman whose hymen is broken by non-intercourse causes (she is treated as a virgin in terms of consent), (3) the procedure when a dispute arises between husband and wife about whether she consented by silence or rejected verbally.

The matn states: "Her consent is by speech like the thayyib, and the one whose virginity has been removed by a jump, or menstruation, or a wound, or spinsterhood, or fornication is legally a virgin. And her statement 'I rejected' is given precedence over his statement 'she was silent'." The commentary explains each part.

First: If a stranger or a distant guardian (like a paternal cousin) seeks a woman's permission for marriage, her consent must be expressed verbally even if she is a virgin. This is because the guardian is not her father or brother, so the natural shyness that justifies silence is not present to the same degree.

Second: A woman who has lost her hymen due to actions other than lawful intercourse—such as jumping, menstruation, injury, prolonged spinsterhood, or even fornication—still retains the legal status of a virgin in this ruling. Her silence is considered consent because she still experiences the shyness typical of virgins; the fornication case is included because she wants to hide her sinful act and people still consider her a virgin.

Third: The core procedural rule: if a husband claims his wife (a mature virgin) remained silent upon learning of the marriage proposal, and she claims she explicitly rejected, her statement is taken over his. The commentary clarifies that this is not about accepting her word without evidence; rather it determines who is the plaintiff and who is the defendant in a lawsuit. The husband is the plaintiff because he seeks to establish his marital rights, while the wife is the defendant denying his claim. Thus, the burden of proof is on the husband to bring evidence that she was silent. If he fails, her oath suffices.

The commentary also addresses a subtle point: silence is not a pure negation; it is an existential act (pressing lips together), so evidence can be brought to prove it.

Finally, the passage notes a difference with Zufar, who argued that silence is the original state and rejection is an exception, so the husband's claim should be taken. The response is that the original from the woman's perspective is the absence of the husband's ownership, which she is defending.

This passage is a good example of Hanafi procedural fiqh: it blends substantive rulings on consent with the rules of evidence and burden of proof.

Key Terms
مدع (mudda'i): Plaintiff: the one who brings a claim and bears the burden of proof.
منكر (munkir): Denier: the one who denies a claim; his statement is accepted with an oath.
سكوت (sukūt): Silence; in this context, it is an existential act (pressing lips) not a pure negation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in a dispute over whether a virgin wife consented by silence or rejected verbally, the wife's statement is accepted because:
A) Women are more trustworthy than men.
B) She is the defendant and the husband is the plaintiff.
C) Silence is an original state that cannot be proven.
D) The Quran gives preference to the wife's word in marriage matters.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi procedural law, the burden of proof in consent disputes falls on the husband as the plaintiff, and the wife's denial is accepted with an oath unless he brings witnesses.

Acceptance of Evidence on Silence in Marriage Disputes
Commentary on the ruling on accepting evidence of silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule for accepting evidence of silence in a marriage dispute and evaluate the counter-argument of Zafar.

Core Idea
The burden of proof lies on the one claiming ownership, not on the one denying it.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a common procedural problem in marriage disputes: when a woman is informed of her marriage and remains silent, but later claims she rejected. The husband brings evidence of her silence. The commentary clarifies the roles of claimant and defendant, justifies that silence is an existential act, and resolves a disagreement with Zafar, who held that the original state of silence should be presumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The husband claims the wife remained silent upon hearing the news of marriage, establishing the marriage. The wife claims she rejected (verbally or by crying). Each asserts a fact that would support their position.
The dispute is about a virgin adult woman, per the text.
2
Determine the claimant (muddaʿī)
The Hanafi definition: the claimant is the one who seeks to establish a right over another. The husband aims to establish his ownership (milk) over the wife and the right to her conjugal benefits. The wife defends herself by denying that right is established. Thus the husband is the claimant, and the wife is the defendant.
This classification determines the burden of proof.
3
Apply the rule: claimant must bring evidence
The husband, as claimant, must produce evidence (bayyinah) of her silence. If he does, his claim is accepted and the marriage is confirmed. If not, the wife (defendant) takes an oath and her claim of rejection stands.
The phrase 'al-qawl qawluhā' means she is the defendant, not that her statement is accepted without proof.
4
Justify that evidence can be brought for silence
An objection: evidence proves an existing thing, but silence is the absence of speech, a non-existence. The response: silence is an existential act—the pressing of lip to lip (ḍamm al-shafah bi al-shafah). Negation (of speech) is its necessary consequence. Thus evidence can be brought on the existential act, and the legal consequence (the marriage) is proven.
This resolves the epistemological issue of proving a negative.
5
Contrast with Zafar's view and evaluate the Hanafi response
Zafar argues that silence is the original state (aṣl) and rejection is an accidental change. The one adhering to the original should have his statement accepted. The Hanafi response: the original state to adhere to is the absence of ownership (adam thubūt milk al-zawj), which favors the woman. She is the one following the original state, so she is the defendant. Thus the husband must bring evidence to overcome that original state.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the husband fails to prove silence, under Hanafi view the woman's rejection stands; under Zafar's view, silence is presumed and the marriage is upheld.

Source Text

(۲) قوله : فالقول قولها ؛ فيه خلافُ زفره ، فإنّه يقول : السكوت أصل، والرد عارض، والقول قولُ مَن يتمسك بالأصل. وجوابه ظاهر، فإن التمسك بالأصل معنى هو المرأة، وهو عدم ثبوت ملك الزوج عليها. His evidence regarding her silence is accepted. 'When you informed her of the marriage and she remained silent, and she said: No, rather I rejected, then her statement is accepted.' Commentary: His statement 'then her statement is accepted' – this phrase in legal claims is a metaphor for him being a defendant. Whenever they say 'his statement is accepted', they mean he is the defendant, and the other is the claimant. So the claimant is required to bring witnesses; if he does, his claim is established, otherwise the defendant takes an oath. It is not meant that his statement is accepted without proof. The summary: when a dispute occurs between the spouses, the husband says to her – and she is a virgin adult – 'You remained silent when the news of marriage reached you, so the marriage is established,' and she says 'I rejected' – either verbally or by an indication like a sound with crying, so there is no marriage – then apparently each of them is a claimant: the husband claims her silence, and she claims her rejection. But upon reflection, it becomes known that the husband is the claimant, because the claimant is the one who establishes his right over another, and the one who defends himself is a defendant by denying what the other imposes. It is known that the husband's aim is to establish his ownership over her and the right to enjoy her, and she defends herself, so she is the defendant. Thus, the husband is required to bring evidence of silence; if he does, it is accepted and the marriage is established. If you say: evidence is established for affirmation, and silence is the absence of speech, so it is a negative. I reply: silence is an existential thing, because it consists of pressing lip to lip, and negation is its necessary consequence. So evidence is accepted regarding this existential thing, and its necessary consequence is established by the establishment of its necessary cause. (2) His statement 'then her statement is accepted' – there is a difference with Zafar, because he says: silence is the original state, rejection is an accident, and the statement of the one who adheres to the original is accepted. The answer is clear: adherence to the original is in the interest of the woman, and that is the non-establishment of the husband's ownership over her. (3) His statement 'regarding her silence' – and if she brings evidence of rejection, and he brings evidence of silence, before he has had intercourse with her voluntarily, according to the most correct view, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its commentary by Ibn Abidin (2:302).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling about disputes in marriage. It explains that when a woman is informed of her marriage and remains silent, but later claims she rejected, the husband's evidence of her silence is accepted because he is the claimant seeking to establish his ownership. The commentary clarifies that silence is an existential act, not a mere absence, so evidence can be brought. It also addresses a dispute with Zafar, who argues that silence is the original state, but the commentary responds that the original state is in the woman's favor (no marriage).

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically witness testimony required to prove a claim.
سكوت (sukūt): Silence; in this context, the physical act of remaining silent after being informed of marriage.
مدع (muddaʿī): Claimant; the party who asserts a right against another and must provide evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He spoke first about the marriage.
B) He seeks to establish his ownership over the wife.
C) He is male and therefore has more legal authority.
D) The wife's silence is a presumption of consent.

Key Takeaway
In marriage disputes, the claimant is the one seeking to establish a right over another, and he must bring evidence—even if that evidence concerns an alleged silence, which is considered an existential act.

The Nukul Dispute: Badhl vs Iqrar in Marriage Oaths
Explaining why the woman is not made to take an oath according to Abu Hanifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on whether a defendant is made to take an oath in marriage disputes and state the mufta bihi position with its basis.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa saw nukul as payment (badhl), not confession; the Sahibayn saw it as confession—fatwa follows the Sahibayn.

This passage addresses a key procedural question in marriage litigation: when the husband has no evidence, must the wife take an oath? Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ fundamentally on the nature of nukul—the defendant's refusal to swear. This difference determines the ruling, and later Hanafi authorities have settled the fatwa according to the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Determine the exact question: Should the woman be made to take an oath when the husband lacks evidence? Abu Hanifa says no; the Sahibayn say yes.
The disagreement is not about evidence but about the implication of the defendant's refusal to swear.
2
Understand the two positions on nukul
Nukul (refusal to take an oath) can be interpreted either as a confession (iqrar) or as a payment (badhl) made to end the dispute. The Sahibayn hold the former; Abu Hanifa the latter.
This conceptual difference is the root of the whole dispute.
3
Analyze the legal implications of each view
If nukul = iqrar, it is valid in all matters, including marriage, so an oath is administered because refusal equals admission. If nukul = badhl, it is not valid in marriage because marriage cannot be 'paid off' like a debt.
Badhl is a voluntary payment to avoid conflict, not a confession of truth.
4
Apply to marriage and similar cases
Marriage, along with revocation (raj‘a) and lineage (nasab), are matters where badhl is not applicable. Therefore Abu Hanifa does not administer the oath in these cases. The Sahibayn apply nukul as confession universally.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa the woman's rejection stands; under the Sahibayn she must swear or lose.
5
Note the fatwa position
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Multaqa, Majma‘ al-Anhur, al-Sharnabaliyya, al-Durr al-Muntaqa) have declared that the fatwa is according to the Sahibayn: the woman is made to take an oath.
This is the operative ruling for qada today, although Abu Hanifa's view is documented in the zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text

(وعندهما لا تحلف واختار رأيهما صاحب (الملتقى (ص)(٥٠)، ونص صاحب (مجمع الأنهر (۱ : ۳۳۵) ، و(الشرنبلالية» (۱) : (٣٣٦) ، و(الدر المنتقى» (۱ : ٣٣۳۵) : على أن الفتوى على رأيهما. قوله : هذا ; أي عدم التحليف عنده خلافاً لهما، والفتوى على قولهما. كما في «بحر»، وأصل الخلافِ الخلافُ في أن النكول هل هو إقرار أو بذل؟ وتوضيحه : أن المُنْكِرَ إنّما يحلفُ عند فقدان الشهود لظهور الحق، فإن حلف بطل دعوى المدعي، وإن نَكَلَ : أي امتنع عن الحلف ثبت ما ادعاه ، فكل موضع يجري فيه النكولُ يحلف المنكر فيه، وكلُّ موضع لا يفيد فيه النكول لا يحلف فيه. والنكولُ إقرار عندهما ، فإنه إذا امتنع عن الحلف فكأنه أقر بما يدعيه المدعي ، والإقرار يجري في النكاح وغيره، فإنّه حجّةٌ مستقلة لثبوت الحق فيجري التحليف فيه أيضاً. وعنده النكول بذل لا إقرار ؛ لأن المنكر كثيراً ما يحترز عن اليمين الصادقة لوجاهته ، فلا يدل امتناعه عنه على إقراره بما يدعيه المدعي، بل هو بذل وصرف من عنده، وإعطاء لما يدعيه المدعي حقاً كان أو باطلاً دفعاً للنزاع، والنكاح ونحوه كالرجعة والنسب وغيرهما لا يجري فيه البذلُ فلا يحلف فيه عنده ، و وسيأتي تفصيل هذه المباحث في «كتاب الدعوى إن شاء الله.
And she is not made to take an oath if he does not bring evidence. This is according to Abu Hanifa, based on the principle that he does not administer an oath in marriage. (And according to them, she is not made to take an oath, and the author of al-Multaqa chose their view, and the author of Majma' al-Anhur, al-Sharnabaliyya, al-Durr al-Muntaqa state that the fatwa is according to their view.) Commentary: His statement 'this' – i.e., the absence of oath according to him is contrary to them, and the fatwa is according to their view, as in al-Bahr. The origin of the dispute is whether nukul (refusal to take an oath) is a confession (iqrar) or a payment (badhl). Explanation: The defendant only takes an oath when witnesses are absent to reveal the truth. If he swears, the plaintiff's claim is nullified; if he refuses (nukul), i.e., refrains from swearing, the claim is established. So in every case where nukul applies, the defendant takes an oath; where nukul does not affect, he does not take an oath. According to them (the two companions), nukul is a confession: if he refrains from swearing, it is as if he confessed to what the plaintiff claims. Confession is valid in marriage and other matters, so oath-taking applies. According to him (Abu Hanifa), nukul is a payment, not a confession, because the defendant often avoids a truthful oath due to its gravity. Thus, his refusal does not indicate confession but is a payment from him, giving the plaintiff what he claims, whether true or false, to end the dispute. Marriage and similar matters like revocation (raj'a) and lineage (nasab) do not admit payment, so he does not administer an oath in such cases. The details of these discussions will come in the Book of Claims, God willing.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a woman is made to take an oath when the husband fails to bring evidence. Abu Hanifa holds that no oath is administered because nukul (refusal to swear) is considered a payment (badhl) made to end the dispute, and such payment is not valid in marriage. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider nukul a confession (iqrar), which is valid in all matters, so the woman should be made to take an oath. The commentary notes that the fatwa is according to the companions' view.

Key Terms
نكول (nukūl): Refusal by the defendant to take an oath after being required to do so.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession; an admission of a right against oneself, considered a conclusive proof.
بذل (badhl): Payment or giving; in this context, the defendant's refusal to swear is seen as giving the plaintiff what he claims to end the dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because nukul is considered a payment, and marriage does not admit payment.
C) Because the woman is always truthful.
D) Because the husband must produce witnesses regardless.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on whether to administer an oath in marriage hinges on whether nukul is treated as confession (iqrar) or payment (badhl); the relied-upon fatwa is that the defendant must take an oath.

Guardian's Coercive Authority in Marriage (Wilayat al-Jabr)
Ruling that the guardian can marry minor children without their consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the guardian's authority to marry minors coercively and identify the hadith proof used.

Core Idea
The guardian may marry minors without consent; the hadith 'No marriage without a guardian' is interpreted as coercive guardianship.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on the guardian's coercive authority (wilayat al-jabr) in marrying minor children. The key proof is the hadith 'there is no marriage except with a guardian,' which the Hanafis interpret specifically to refer to this coercive authority, reconciling it with other evidences. Understanding this ruling clarifies the scope of guardianship in Hanafi nikah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the guardian marrying a minor boy or girl without their consent. The ruling is that it is valid and binding; the guardian has the authority (mashru'iya).
The text says 'وللولي إنكاح الصغير والصغيرة' – the guardian has the right.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'لا نكاح إلا بولي' (No marriage except with a guardian). This is cited in the passage.
Hadith is ahad, but accepted in fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The negation 'لا نكاح' indicates that a marriage without a guardian is invalid. The Hanafis interpret this as requiring a guardian with coercive authority (jabr) for minors, not just any guardian.
This interpretation harmonizes with other evidences that allow a mature woman to marry without a guardian.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that the hadith requires a guardian's consent even for adults, or that minors must also consent. The Hanafis limit the coercion to minors and the insane.
The passage says 'عندنا محمول على هذه الولاية الجبرية' – according to us, it is interpreted as coercive.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is firmly established in the school; it is the mufta bihi and based on this interpretation of the hadith.
No intra-Hanafi dispute; all three imams agree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وللولي إنكاح الصغير والصغيرة ; أي جبراً ; لحديث: «لا نكاح إلاً بولي) ، فإنه عندنا محمول على هذه الولاية الجبرية جمعاً بين الأدلة، ولو قال : غير (٤) في سنن الترمذي (۳): (٤٠٧) ، و(سنن أبي داود (۲) : (۲۲۹)، و(سنن ابن ماجة» (۱ : ٦٠٥)، وغيرها.
His statement: 'And the guardian has the authority to marry the minor boy and minor girl' – i.e., coercively, due to the hadith: 'There is no marriage except with a guardian.' According to us (the Hanafis), this hadith is interpreted to refer to this coercive guardianship, in order to reconcile the evidences. And if he said: 'other' (the text cuts off, but the footnote provides hadith sources). (4) In Sunan al-Tirmidhi (3:407), Sunan Abi Dawud (2:229), Sunan Ibn Majah (1:605), etc.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a guardian has the authority to marry minor boys and girls without their consent (coercively). The proof is the hadith 'No marriage without a guardian,' which the Hanafis interpret specifically as referring to coercive guardianship (wilayat al-jabr) to harmonize with other evidences. The footnote provides the sources of the hadith.

Key Terms
ولي (walī): Guardian; the person responsible for arranging the marriage of a minor or an adult woman in some cases.
ولاية جبرية (wilāyat al-jabr): Coercive guardianship; the authority to marry someone without their consent, specifically for minors and insane persons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The hadith 'No marriage without a guardian.'
C) Rational analogy to property guardianship.
D) The Quranic verse on marriage.

Key Takeaway
The guardian may marry minors coercively based on the hadith 'لا نكاح إلا بولي' interpreted for wilayat al-jabr.

The Nature of Nukul: Does Refusal to Swear Constitute Admission?
Ruling on oath in marriage when husband lacks witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding nukul in marriage and identify the mufta bihi position with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Whether nukul is iqrar or bazz determines if the wife must swear when the husband lacks witnesses.

In a marital dispute where the husband denies the claim but cannot produce witnesses, the court may ask him to swear an oath. If he refuses (nukul), the pivotal question arises: does his refusal constitute an admission (iqrar) or merely an offering (bazz) to end the conflict? The Hanafi school split on this, with Abu Hanifa holding that nukul is bazz and does not apply to marriage, while the Sahibayn considered it iqrar, binding even in family matters. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and identifies the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Set the Scenario
Identify the legal scenario: a husband is sued by his wife (e.g., over mahr) and he denies the claim. He is asked to swear an oath but refuses. The court must decide whether to then administer an oath to the wife to prove her claim.
The underlying issue is whether nukul (refusal) can substitute for proof.
2
Abu Hanifa's Position: Nukul as Bazz
Understand that Abu Hanifa classifies nukul as bazz – a voluntary offering to end the dispute, not an admission. Since bazz does not operate in marriage, revocable divorce, or lineage, the wife is not sworn and her claim fails for lack of evidence.
He reasons that a truthful person may refuse an oath out of dignity, so refusal does not imply guilt.
3
The Sahibayn's Position: Nukul as Iqrar
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider nukul as iqrar (admission). Refusal to swear is equivalent to admitting the claim. Since iqrar is a standalone proof valid in all matters, the wife must be sworn; if she swears, she wins.
For them, the refusal is strong circumstantial evidence of the defendant's lack of a valid defense.
4
Trace the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Compare the practical outcome: under Abu Hanifa, the case ends without the wife's oath; under the Sahibayn, she is given the oath and may succeed. This difference affects the allocation of burden of proof.
The thamarah is clear: who takes the oath and what consequence follows.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi
Highlight that the fatwa (operative ruling) is on the opinion of the Sahibayn, as stated in the commentary with reference to al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. Thus, in practice, the wife is sworn.
This tarjih reflects the later Hanafi preference for the Sahibayn's view in judicial procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۲) (وللولي إنكاح الصغير والصغيرة" ولو ثيباً) فبينتها أولى لإثبات الزيادة، وهو الردُّ ، فإنَّ الردَّ زائد على السكوت. كذا في «البحر ) [١] قوله : هذا ؛ أي عدم التحليف عنده خلافاً لهما، والفتوى على قولهما. كما في «البحر»، وأصل الخلافِ الخلافُ في أن النكول هل هو إقرار أو بذل؟ وتوضيحه : أن المُنْكِرَ إنّما يحلفُ عند فقدان الشهود لظهور الحق، فإن حلف بطل دعوى المدعي، وإن نَكَلَ : أي امتنع عن الحلف ثبت ما ادعاه ، فكل موضع يجري فيه النكولُ يحلف المنكر فيه، وكلُّ موضع لا يفيد فيه النكول لا يحلف فيه. والنكولُ إقرار عندهما ، فإنه إذا امتنع عن الحلف فكأنه أقر بما يدعيه المدعي ، والإقرار يجري في النكاح وغيره، فإنّه حجّةٌ مستقلة لثبوت الحق فيجري التحليف فيه أيضاً. وعنده النكول بذل لا إقرار ؛ لأن المنكر كثيراً ما يحترز عن اليمين الصادقة لوجاهته ، فلا يدل امتناعه عنه على إقراره بما يدعيه المدعي، بل هو بذل وصرف من عنده، وإعطاء لما يدعيه المدعي حقاً كان أو باطلاً دفعاً للنزاع، والنكاح ونحوه كالرجعة والنسب وغيرهما لا يجري فيه البذلُ فلا يحلف فيه عنده ، و وسيأتي تفصيل هذه المباحث في «كتاب الدعوى إن شاء الله.
She is not made to swear if he does not produce evidence, and the guardian has the right to marry the minor male and minor female even if deflowered. And she is not made to swear if he does not produce evidence): This is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, based on the fact that he does not administer an oath in marriage. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), she is not sworn, and the author of al-Multaqa chose their opinion... (then commentary)
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the administration of an oath in marriage when the husband fails to produce witnesses. The base text (matn) states that the wife is not made to swear, which is Abu Hanifa's position. The commentary explains that the difference stems from the nature of nukul (refusal to swear). According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), nukul is iqrar (admission), so it applies in marriage and the wife would be sworn. According to Abu Hanifa, nukul is bazz (offering/giving up), which does not apply in marriage because marriage and similar matters (like revocable divorce and lineage) cannot be settled by mere offering. Therefore, she is not sworn. The fatwa (operative ruling) is on the opinion of the Sahibayn.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): Refusal of the defendant to take an oath after the plaintiff demands it.
البذل (al-bazz): Offering or giving up something voluntarily to end a dispute, not necessarily implying admission.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission or confession that establishes liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The wife is forced to produce witnesses.
D) The husband is declared guilty by default.

Key Takeaway
The wife is sworn when the husband refuses to swear because nukul is treated as iqrar in the relied-upon Hanafi position.

The Basis of Compulsory Guardianship: Minority vs. Virginity (Hanafi vs. Shafi'i)
Ruling on guardian's marriage of minors, binding for father/grandfather, optional for others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that compulsory guardianship is based on minority and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument on virginity.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, minority alone triggers compulsory guardianship; defloration does not remove it.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a classic inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the basis for compulsory guardianship (wilayah jabriyyah) in marriage. The core issue is whether a guardian can marry a minor who is deflowered (thayyib) without her consent. The Hanafi position, based on minority, grants the father and grandfather binding authority, while the Shafi'i school considers virginity as the trigger. Let's unpack the argument and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on guardian's authority
Start by reading the matn statement: 'And the guardian has the right to marry the male minor and female minor, even if she is deflowered.' Recognize that this establishes compulsory guardianship (wilayah jabriyyah).
Note the phrase 'wa law thayyaban' (even if deflowered) is crucial for the dispute.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
The author cites the hadith 'No marriage except with a guardian' and explains that Hanafis interpret this as referring to compulsory guardianship to reconcile all evidences. Understand the interpretive move: the hadith is taken as a general command that requires a guardian's consent, but binding authority is limited to certain contexts.
This is a classic example of jam' al-adillah (reconciliation of evidences).
3
Contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions
The text explicitly states that Shafi'i bases compulsory guardianship on virginity (bikarah), while Hanafis base it on minority (sighar). Note the thamarah al-khilaf: for a deflowered minor, Hanafis still allow the father to marry her bindingly; Shafi'is require her consent.
The disagreement is not about the existence of guardian authority but its trigger.
4
Analyze the binding vs. optional distinction
The passage distinguishes between father/grandfather (binding absolutely, even with non-competence or excessive dower) and other guardians (marriage valid but minor has option to annul upon puberty). Understand the rationale: father/grandfather's complete compassion versus others' deficient compassion.
This is a key nuance: other guardians cannot marry with excessive disadvantage.
5
Apply the ruling to the actual scenario
Synthesize: if a minor girl (even deflowered) is married by her brother, the marriage is valid but she can annul at puberty. If married by her father, it is binding with no option. This shows the practical impact of the dispute.
The same ruling applies to male minors as indicated by 'al-saghir wa al-saghirah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وللولي إنكاح الصغير والصغيرة ولو ثيباً) أي جبراً ؛ لحديث: «لا نكاح إلاً بولي) ، فإنه عندنا محمول على هذه الولاية الجبرية جمعاً بين الأدلة، ولو قال : غير هذا احتراز عن قول الشَّافِعِي (1) كما مرّ. [٢] ثم إن زوجها الأب والجد لزم ، وفي غيرهما فسخ الصغيران حين بلغا ، أو علما بالنكاح بعده) : أي إن كانا عالمين بالنكاح ، فلهما الفسخ عند البلوغ، فإن لم يكونا عالمين، فلهما الفسخُ حين علما بعد البلوغ المكلف ؛ لكان أولى ؛ ليدخل إنكاح المعتوه والمعتوهة والمجنون والمجنونة، ولو كانـا كبيرين، ثم لا بُدَّ من قيد الحرية، فإنه ليس لولي الرقيق إنكاحه ، بل أمر نكاحه إلى مولاه. [١] قوله : هذا ؛ أي قوله : «ولو ثيباً» ؛ احتراز عن مذهب الشافعي له، فإن موجب الولاية الجبرية عنده هو البكارة وإن كانت مع الكبر، وعندنا : هو الصغر وإن كانت مع الثيابة. [٢] قوله : لزم ؛ يعني إذا زوج الصغير أو الصغيرة، وكذا كل من عليه ولاية جبرية من الأولياء الأب أو الجد يلزم ذلك النكاح مطلقاً، ولو كان ذلك من غير كفء، أو كان بغبن فاحش بزيادة مهره أو قلة مهرها ؛ لأنّ شفقتهما على الصغار فوق شفقة الأجانب والأقارب، بخلاف ما إذا زوجه غيرُهما ، فإنه يكون لهما الخيار بعد البلوغ في إبقائه وفسخه لقصور الشفقة المنجر إلى تطرق الخلل في مقاصد النكاح. (۳) قوله : في غيرهما ؛ أي غير الأب والجد كالأخ والأم والقاضي أو وكيل الأب، نعم ؛ لو عين الأب لوكيله رجلاً غير كفء، أو مقدار المهر، فزوج على - تعيينه لزم. كذا في «النهر»). [٤]قوله : فسخ ؛ هذا إذا زوجها غيرهما من كفء وبمهر المثل، فإنه لا يصح إنكاح غير الأب والجـد بغبن فاحش، ومن غير كفء أصلاً لا لازماً ولا موقوفاً، وقد أخطأ الشارح له في هذه المسألة فيما سيأتي.
His saying: 'And the guardian has the right to marry the male minor and female minor, even if [she is] deflowered' i.e., compulsorily; due to the hadith: 'No marriage except with a guardian.' According to us, it is interpreted as this compulsory guardianship to reconcile the evidences. And if he said: 'other than this' is an exception from the saying of al-Shafi'i, as passed. Then if the father and grandfather married her, it is binding, and in other than them, the two minors annul when they mature, or they know of the marriage afterwards...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage covers the Hanafi ruling on the guardian's authority to contract marriage for minors. The matn states that the guardian may marry the minor male and female even if the female is deflowered (thayyib). The commentary explains that this is compulsory guardianship (wilayah jabriyyah) based on the hadith 'No marriage except with a guardian.' The Hanafi school interprets this hadith as referring to compulsory guardianship to harmonize all evidences. The passage then distinguishes between the father and grandfather on one hand and other guardians on the other: if the father or grandfather marries off the minor, the marriage is binding immediately, even if it is not a match of kuf' (suitability) or involves excessive disadvantage (ghaban fahish) in dower, because their compassion is considered complete. However, if a guardian other than the father or grandfather (e.g., brother, mother, judge, or the father's agent) marries the minor, the minor upon reaching puberty has the option (khiyar al-bulugh) to either confirm or annul the marriage. The commentator also clarifies that the basis for compulsory guardianship in the Hanafi school is minority (sighar), not virginity as held by al-Shafi'i. This difference is highlighted: al-Shafi'i makes virginity the basis even for adults, while Hanafis make minority the basis even for a deflowered minor. The passage also notes that if the father appoints an agent to marry the minor to a specific non-suitable person or with a specific dower, the marriage is binding if the agent follows the instruction.

Key Terms
الولاية الجبرية (al-wilayah al-jabriyyah): Compulsory guardianship authority that allows the guardian to contract marriage for a minor without the minor's consent.
الكفؤ (al-kuf'): Suitability or equality in marriage, considering lineage, religion, profession, etc.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghaban al-fahish): Excessive disadvantage in a contract, e.g., marrying a minor to someone with insufficient dower or paying excessive dower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a minor girl (even if deflowered) is married by her brother (who is not the father or grandfather), what is the status of the marriage?
A) The marriage is completely invalid (batil).
B) The marriage is binding and cannot be annulled.
C) The marriage is valid but the girl has an option to annul upon reaching puberty.
D) The marriage requires the consent of the father to be valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between father/grandfather (binding marriage even with non-kuf' and ghaban fahish) and other guardians (valid marriage with option for the minor to annul), with the basis of compulsory guardianship being minority, not virginity.

intermediate
8 min
Marriage Contract by Father/Grandfather vs Other Guardians
Ruling on binding marriage by father/grandfather and option for others

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compulsory guardianship and distinguish when a minor's marriage is binding from when it is subject to annulment, explaining the proof based on compassion.

Core Idea
Father and grandfather marry minors with binding authority; other guardians give an option (khiyar al-bulugh) due to weaker compassion.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all guardians have equal authority when marrying minors. The father and grandfather possess compulsory guardianship (wilaya jabriyya), making their marriage contract absolutely binding. Other relatives or the judge, however, contract a marriage that is valid but subject to the minor's right to annul upon puberty. This differentiation rests on the degree of compassion (shafaqa) presumed for each guardian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of guardians
Distinguish between father/grandfather (awliya' mujbir) and other guardians (brother, mother, judge, etc.).
2
Determine the legal consequence for each
For father/grandfather: marriage is binding and cannot be annulled by the minor. For others: marriage is valid but the minor has the option (khiyar al-bulugh) to accept or annul upon puberty.
3
Identify the proof: principle of compassion (shafaqa)
Father and grandfather have superior compassion for the minor, ensuring no harm in the match. Others lack this level of care, so the minor is given a protective option.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the fiqh principle of blocking harm (sadd al-dhara'i).
4
Analyze the cross-madhhab dispute
Compare with al-Shafi'i: he bases compulsory guardianship on virginity (bikara) not minority. Thus, for a non-virgin adult, her father cannot marry her without consent. Hanafis base it on minority regardless of virginity.
The practical difference: a non-virgin minor can be married by father without consent in Hanafi, but in Shafi'i she would need consent if she had reached majority.
5
Apply to exceptions and conditions
Note that if the father specifies an agent, the marriage is binding per specification. Also, if another guardian contracts with gross deficiency (ghubn fahish) or non-compatibility, the marriage is invalid (batil), not even subject to option.
The commentator mentions an error by the sharh on this point to be clarified later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن زوجها الأب والجد لزم، وفي غيرهما فسخ الصغيران حين بلغا، أو علما بالنكاح بعده
هذا احتراز عن قول الشافعي كما مر.
ثم إن زوجها الأب والجد لزم ، وفي غيرهما فسخ الصغيران حين بلغا ، أو علما بالنكاح بعده) : أي إن كانا عالمين بالنكاح ، فلهما الفسخ عند البلوغ، فإن لم يكونا عالمين، فلهما الفسخُ حين علما بعد البلوغ المكلف ؛ لكان أولى ؛ ليدخل إنكاح المعتوه والمعتوهة والمجنون والمجنونة، ولو كانـا كبيرين، ثم لا بُدَّ من قيد الحرية، فإنه ليس لولي الرقيق إنكاحه ، بل أمر نكاحه إلى مولاه.
قوله : هذا ؛ أي قوله : «ولو ثيباً» ؛ احتراز عن مذهب الشافعي له، فإن موجب الولاية الجبرية عنده هو البكارة وإن كانت مع الكبر، وعندنا : هو الصغر وإن كانت مع الثيابة. قوله : لزم ؛ يعني إذا زوج الصغير أو الصغيرة، وكذا كل من عليه ولاية جبرية من الأولياء الأب أو الجد يلزم ذلك النكاح مطلقاً، ولو كان ذلك من غير كفء، أو كان بغبن فاحش بزيادة مهره أو قلة مهرها ؛ لأنّ شفقتهما على الصغار فوق شفقة الأجانب والأقارب، بخلاف ما إذا زوجه غيرُهما ، فإنه يكون لهما الخيار بعد جميع البلوغ في إبقائه وفسخه لقصور الشفقة المنجر إلى تطرق الخلل في مقاصد النكاح. قوله : في غيرهما ؛ أي غير الأب والجد كالأخ والأم والقاضي أو وكيل الأب، نعم ؛ لو عين الأب لوكيله رجلاً غير كفء، أو مقدار المهر، فزوج على - تعيينه لزم. كذا في «النهر». قوله : فسخ ؛ هذا إذا زوجها غيرهما من كفء وبمهر المثل، فإنه لا يصح إنكاح غير الأب والجـد بغبن فاحش، ومن غير كفء أصلاً لا لازماً ولا موقوفاً، وقد أخطأ الشارح له في هذه المسألة فيما سيأتي.
Then if the father or grandfather married her off, it is binding; and with other than them, the two minors may annul upon reaching puberty or upon learning of the marriage thereafter. This is a precaution against the statement of al-Shafi'i as mentioned. Then if the father or grandfather married her off, it is binding; and with other than them, the two minors may annul upon reaching puberty or upon learning of the marriage thereafter. That is, if they were aware of the marriage, they have the right to annul at puberty; if they were not aware, they have the right to annul when they learn after puberty. It would be more appropriate to also include the marriage of an insane person (male and female) even if they are adults. Then the condition of freedom is necessary, for the guardian of a slave does not have the authority to marry him; rather, his marriage is up to his master. He said: 'This' means his statement 'even if a non-virgin' is a precaution against the madhhab of al-Shafi'i, for the basis of compulsory guardianship according to him is virginity even if she is adult; according to us it is minority even if she is a non-virgin. He said: 'binding' means if he marries a minor male or female, and likewise anyone who has compulsory guardianship among the guardians, the father or grandfather, that marriage is absolutely binding, even if it is with a non-compatible match or with gross deficit in increased dower or decreased dower, because their compassion for minors is above the compassion of strangers and relatives. Unlike if someone else marries them, for they have the option after all puberty to keep it or annul it due to deficient compassion leading to defect in the purposes of marriage. He said: 'other than them' means other than father and grandfather like brother, mother, judge, or agent of father. Yes, if the father specifies for his agent a non-compatible man or the amount of dower, and he marries according to his specification, it is binding. As in al-Nahr. He said: 'annul' this is if someone other than them marries her from a compatible match and with proper dower; it is not valid for other than father and grandfather to marry with gross deficit, and with someone non-compatible it is not valid at all, neither binding nor suspended. The commentator has erred in this issue later.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the authority of guardians (awliya') in contracting marriage for minors. The text establishes that the father and grandfather (the awliya' mujbir) have absolute power: their marriage contract is binding and cannot be annulled by the minor upon puberty, even if the spouse is not a compatible match or the dower is unfair, due to their superior compassion. Other guardians (e.g., brother, mother, judge) do not have this absolute power; their marriage contract for a minor gives the minor a choice (khiyar al-bulugh) to either accept or annul the marriage upon reaching puberty or upon learning of the marriage if unaware before. The text also clarifies that the condition of freedom applies: a slave's guardian is his master, not his father. A key point of dispute between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i is highlighted: al-Shafi'i bases compulsory guardianship on virginity (bikara), whereas the Hanafis base it on minority (sughr), regardless of whether the minor is a virgin or non-virgin. The passage then addresses the ruling when a guardian other than father/grandfather marries a minor to a compatible match with proper dower: the marriage is valid but subject to the minor's option. If there is a gross deficiency (ghubn fahish) or non-compatibility, the marriage is invalid (batil) altogether. The commentator (sharih) is noted to have made an error on this point, which will be clarified later. The Hanafi position is that father and grandfather's marriage is binding absolutely, while others' marriage is subject to option, based on the principle of compassion (shafaqa) and the potential for harm in marriage purposes.

Key Terms
ولي مجبر (wali mujbir): A guardian with compulsory authority to contract marriage for a minor without their consent.
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option a minor has to annul or affirm a marriage contracted by a non-mujbir guardian upon reaching puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a father marries his minor son to a non-compatible woman with a grossly insufficient dower, is the marriage binding?
A) Yes, absolutely binding
B) No, the son has an option upon puberty
C) The marriage is invalid
D) The marriage is suspended

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only father and grandfather have compulsory guardianship; their marriage contract for a minor is binding, while others' is subject to the minor's option upon puberty.
