Silence of a Virgin as Consent for Marriage
Ruling that a virgin's silence indicates consent and option is immediate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a virgin's silence at puberty constitutes consent and apply the immediate option rule, distinguishing it from al-Khassaf's and al-Shafi'i's positions.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence, due to shyness, is consent — and her option to annul is fawri.

In Hanafi marriage law, a virgin's silence speaks louder than words. When a minor married by a non-mujbir guardian reaches puberty, her silence upon learning of the marriage is taken as acceptance, extinguishing her right to annul. This rule hinges on the natural shyness of a virgin. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the reasoning, the immediate nature of the option (fawri), and contrasts with divergent views within and outside the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Understand the scenario: a virgin minor married by a guardian other than father/grandfather reaches puberty. The ruling is that her silence is consent, making the marriage binding without option.
The act is khiyar al-bulugh; silence nullifies it.
2
Locate the Dalil
The proof is analogy (qiyas) to the initial marriage contract: a virgin's silence there indicates consent due to shyness; same here.
Dalil is aqli (reasoning based on commonality of shyness).
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is the natural shyness of a virgin, which prevents her from speaking. Silence in both scenarios is evidence of consent.
The passage explicitly mentions 'al-haya' 'an al-takallum' (shyness from speaking).
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Two disputes: (1) Intra-Hanafi: al-Khassaf says option extends to end of session; al-Mabsut says fawri. (2) Inter-madhhab: al-Shafi'i holds marriage by non-mujbir invalid entirely.
The text sides with al-Mabsut (zahir al-riwayah).
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the option is immediate (fawri) per al-Mabsut. Silence at the moment of puberty (if aware) constitutes consent.
Rejection of arbitrary nullification by asking name etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسكوتُ البكر رضاء هنا ، ولا يمتد خيارها إلى آخر المجلس، وإن جهلت به وفيه خلافُ الشَّافِعِي له فإن تزويج غير الأب والجد قبل البلوغ لا يصح عنده لما ذكرنا أن الولي المجبر عنده ليس إلا الأب والجد.
وسكوت البكر رضاء هنا ) : أي عند البلوغ، أو العلم بالنكاح بـعـد البلوغ، ولا يمتد خيارها إلى آخر المجلس ، وإن جهلت به
قوله : وسكوت البكر ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا بلغت التي زوجها غير أبيها وجدها ، وقد علمت بالنكاح قبل ذلك فسكتت عند البلوغ، أو بلغ خبر النكاح فيما إذا لم تَعْلَمْ به قبل ذلك فسكتت ولم ترد ، ولم تفسخ، كان ذلك دليلاً على رضائها فيبطل خيارها ؛ لأن هذه الحالة كحالة ابتداء النكاح، فكما اعتبر سكوت البكر هناك دليلاً على الرضاء يجعل دليلاً هاهنا أيضاً ؛ لوجود الجامع بينهما، وهو الحياء عن التكلم. قوله : إلى آخر المجلس ؛ قال في «فتح القدير»: «المراد بالمجلس: مجلس بلوغها بأن حاضت في مجلس، وقد كان بلغها النكاح ، أو مجلس بلوغ خبر النكاح إذا كانت بكراً بالغةً ، وجَعَلَ الخَصَّافُ خيار البلوغ ممتداً إلى آخر المجلس، وهو خلاف رواية «المبسوط»، فإن فيه ثبوت الخيار لها في الساعة التي تكون بالغة إذا كانت عالمة بالنكاح. وعلى هذا، قالوا : ينبغي أن تطلب مع رؤية الدم، فإن رأته ليلاً تطلب بلسانها ، فتقول : فسخت نكاحي وتشهد إذا أصبحت وتقول : رأيت الدم الآن. قيل لمحمد : كيف يصح هذا وهو كذب، فقال : لا تصدق في الإشهاد فجاز لها أن تكذب كيلا يبطل حقها. ثم إذا اختارت وأشهدت ولم تتقدم إلى القاضي شهراً أو شهرين، فهي على خيارها، وما قيل : لو سألت عن اسم الزوج أو عن المهر، أو سلمت على الشهود بطل خيارها تعسف لا دليل عليه، وغاية الأمر كون هذه الحالة كحالة ابتداء النكاح، ولو سألت البكر عن اسم الزوج لا ينفد عليها.
And the silence of a virgin is consent here, and her option does not extend to the end of the session, even if she was unaware of it. And therein is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, for the marriage by other than father and grandfather before puberty is not valid according to him, as we mentioned that the compulsory guardian according to him is only father and grandfather. And the silence of a virgin is consent here, meaning at puberty, or knowledge of the marriage after puberty, and her option does not extend to the end of the session, even if she was unaware of it. He said: 'And the silence of a virgin...' meaning if she reaches puberty, she who was married by other than her father and grandfather, and she knew of the marriage before that and remained silent at puberty, or the news of the marriage reached her in the case where she did not know it before, and she remained silent and did not reject and did not annul, that is evidence of her consent, and her option is void. Because this situation is like the situation of the initial marriage contract, so just as the silence of a virgin is considered evidence of consent there, it is considered evidence here as well, because of the common link between them: shyness from speaking. He said: 'to the end of the session' – al-Khassaf made the option of puberty extend to the end of the session, which is contrary to the narration of al-Mabsut, for in it the option is established for her in the hour that she reaches puberty if she is aware of the marriage. According to this, they said: she should seek (annulment) upon seeing the blood; if she sees it at night, she should speak with her tongue saying 'I have annulled my marriage' and bear witness in the morning saying 'I saw the blood now.' It was said to Muhammad: How can this be valid when it is a lie? He said: She is not believed in bearing witness, so it is permissible for her to lie so that her right is not lost. Then if she chooses and bears witness and does not go to the judge for a month or two, she is still on her option. And what is said: if she asks about the name of the husband or the dower, or greets the witnesses, her option is nullified – that is arbitrary with no evidence. The most that can be said is that this situation is like the situation of the initial marriage contract; if the virgin asks about the name of the husband, it does not cause her (option) to lapse.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage addresses the role of a virgin bride's silence as an indication of consent when she has the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh). According to the Hanafi school, if a virgin who was married by a guardian other than father/grandfather (so she has the option) reaches puberty and remains silent upon learning of the marriage, that silence is taken as consent (rida), thereby nullifying her option. The rationale is that a virgin is naturally shy to speak explicitly about marriage matters, so silence is a sign of acceptance. This is analogous to her silence in the initial marriage contract being considered consent. The option is immediate (fawri), not extending to the end of the session, contrary to the view of al-Khassaf who allowed extension to the end of the session. The text from 'Fath al-Qadir' clarifies that 'end of the session' refers to the session of puberty or the session of learning about the marriage. The preferred Hanafi position (from al-Mabsut) is that the option is only in the very moment of attaining puberty if aware; hence she must immediately declare her annulment, even if by a permissible statement that might be considered a lie for the sake of preserving her right. The passage also discusses that if she delays going to the judge for a month or two after choosing, her option remains. It rejects the notion that mere inquiries about the husband's name or dower nullify the option, calling that arbitrary. The inter-madhhab dispute is also noted: al-Shafi'i holds that marriage by non-father/grandfather guardians is invalid entirely before puberty, so the option issue does not arise; instead, he requires the woman's consent. The Hanafi position is that such marriages are valid but subject to option, and a virgin's silence is consent.

Key Terms
سكوت البكر (sukut al-bikr): The silence of a virgin bride, considered as consent to marriage or to its continuation.
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The right of a minor to annul a marriage contracted by a non-mujbir guardian upon reaching puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a virgin minor married by her brother reaches puberty and remains silent for an hour, then seeks to annul, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Her silence is considered consent and her option is void
B) She can still annul within the session
C) The marriage is invalid
D) She must pay compensation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a virgin's silence upon reaching puberty and learning of her marriage is legally deemed consent, extinguishing her option to annul immediately.

Nikah — Marriage

Silence of the Virgin as Consent in Khiyar al-Bulugh
Commentary expanding ruling on khiyar al-bulugh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers a virgin's silence at puberty as consent and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument, and state the mufta bihi position on the duration of the option.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence at puberty is immediate consent; ignorance of the option does not excuse her.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the commentator expands the ruling on khiyar al-bulugh for a virgin. The key question is what effect her silence has upon reaching puberty or learning of the marriage. The Hanafi school holds that her silence is consent, even if she is ignorant of her right to annul, and the option is lost immediately. The passage also contrasts this with Imam al-Shafi'i's position that such marriages are invalid from the outset, and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the option extends to the end of the session.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A virgin's silence upon reaching puberty or knowledge of marriage. The ruling: her silence is consent and the option of puberty is invalidated immediately.
The option is lost at the moment of silence, not extended.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi proof is qiyas (analogy) to the initial marriage contract, where a virgin's silence is consent due to modesty (haya'). The same 'illa (modesty) applies here.
The source explicitly mentions the analogy.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a marriage contracted by a non-ajbar guardian (other than father/grandfather) before puberty is invalid, so no option arises. This is because in Shafi'i usul, only the father can be a mujbir.
This is a fundamental difference in the validity of the marriage itself.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Khassaf holds that the option extends to the end of the session (the session of puberty/knowledge). The relied-upon view (from al-Mabsut, supported by Fath al-Qadir) restricts it to the immediate moment.
The mufta bihi is the immediate loss of option.
5
Apply to a practical scenario
If a virgin sees blood (sign of puberty) at night, she must verbally annul and may lie in witnessing to protect her right. Her silence otherwise would be consent.
Ignorance of the option is not an excuse for a free woman.

وسكوت البكر رضاء هنا ) : أي عند البلوغ، أو العلم بالنكاح بـعـد البلوغ، ولا يمتد خيارها إلى آخر المجلس (۲) (۲) ،
وإن جهلت به

على الرضاء يجعل دليلاً هاهنا أيضاً ؛ لوجود الجامع بينهما، وهو الحياء عن التكلم. فتقول : فسخت نكاحي وتشهد إذا أصبحت وتقول : رأيت الدم الآن. قيل لمحمد : كيف يصح هذا وهو كذب، فقال : لا تصدق في الإشهاد
فجاز لها أن تكذب كيلا يبطل حقها. ثم إذا اختارت وأشهدت ولم تتقدم إلى القاضي شهراً أو شهرين، فهي على خيارها، وما قيل : لو سألت عن اسم الزوج أو عن المهر، أو سلمت على الشهود بطل خيارها تعسف لا دليل عليه، وغاية الأمر كون هذه الحالة كحالة ابتداء النكاح، ولو سألت البكر عن اسم الزوج لا ينفد عليها (۳).»
(۳) انتهى من «فتح القدير»(۳ : ۲۸۲).

أي بالخيار"، فإن البكر إذا سكتت بعد البلوغ أو العلم بناءً على أنها لم تعلم أنَّ لها الخيار يبطل خيارها ، فإن سكوتها رضا ولا تُعذر بالجهل، والجهل ليس بعذر في حقها .
And the silence of the virgin is consent here, and her option does not extend to the end of the session, even if she is ignorant of it, and therein is a disagreement of al-Shafi'i; for him, the marriage by other than the father and grandfather before puberty is not valid according to him because, as we mentioned, the compelling guardian according to him is only the father and grandfather. And the silence of the virgin is consent here: i.e., at puberty, or upon knowledge of the marriage after puberty, and her option does not extend to the end of the session. And if she is ignorant of it. [1] His statement: 'And the silence of the virgin...' means that if she reaches puberty who was married by other than her father and grandfather, and she knew of the marriage before that and was silent at puberty, or the news of the marriage reached her when she did not know it before and she was silent and did not reject and did not annul, that is evidence of her consent, so her option is invalidated; because this state is like the state of the beginning of the marriage, just as the silence of the virgin there is considered evidence of consent, it is considered evidence here as well; due to the common factor between them, which is modesty from speaking. [2] His statement: 'to the end of the session'; he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'The meaning of session is the session of her puberty such that she menstruates in a session and the marriage news had reached her, or the session of the arrival of the news of the marriage if she is a virgin adult. And al-Khassaf made the option of puberty extend to the end of the session, contrary to the narration of al-Mabsut, for in it the option is established for her in the hour in which she reaches puberty if she is aware of the marriage. And upon this, they said: She should seek with the sight of blood; if she sees it at night, she should seek with her tongue saying: I annul my marriage, and she gives witness when morning comes and says: I saw the blood now. It was said to Muhammad: How can this be valid and it is a lie? He said: She is not truthful in the witnessing, so it is permissible for her to lie so that her right is not invalidated. Then if she chooses and gives witness and does not approach the qadi for a month or two, she remains upon her option. And what was said: If she asks about the name of the husband or about the dowry, or greets the witnesses, her option is invalidated, that is arbitrary with no evidence; the utmost is that this state is like the state of the beginning of the marriage, and if the virgin asks about the name of the husband, it is not binding upon her.' (End of quote from Fath al-Qadir 3:282). 'i.e., about the option': then the virgin if she remains silent after puberty or knowledge on the basis that she did not know that she has an option, her option is invalidated; for her silence is consent and she is not excused by ignorance, and ignorance is not an excuse in her case.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses the ruling on khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) for a virgin girl. The matn states that her silence upon reaching puberty or upon learning of the marriage is considered consent (rida'), and her option to annul the marriage does not extend to the end of the session—it is immediate. The commentary explains that even if she is ignorant of having the option, her silence still invalidates the option. There is a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that a marriage conducted by a guardian other than the father or grandfather before puberty is invalid, so no option arises. The commentary then cites Fath al-Qadir to elaborate on the meaning of 'session' and an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Khassaf extends the option to the end of the session, while the relied-upon position (from al-Mabsut) restricts it to the immediate moment. Practical guidance is given: a virgin who menstruates at night should verbally annul and later testify that she saw blood, with a dispensation to lie in witnessing to protect her right. The passage concludes by reaffirming that silence indicates consent and ignorance is not an excuse for a free woman.

Key Terms
بكر (bikr): A virgin girl who has never been married.
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option of a minor who was married by a guardian other than father/grandfather to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.
الولي المجبر (al-wali al-mujbir): The guardian with legal authority to compel a minor into marriage; in Hanafi fiqh, only the father and grandfather have this power.
سكوت (sukut): Silence; in the context of marriage, a virgin's silence is legally interpreted as consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a virgin, married by her brother (not father/grandfather), reaches puberty and remains silent without knowing she has an option to annul the marriage?
A) Her silence is not consent because she was ignorant, so her option remains.
B) Her silence is considered consent and her option is invalidated; ignorance is not an excuse.
C) The marriage is invalid because the guardian had no authority.
D) She must be questioned by the qadi before any ruling.

Key Takeaway
A virgin's silence upon reaching puberty or learning of her marriage is legally consent, and ignorance of the option does not excuse her.

Khiyar al-‘Itq vs. Khiyar al-Bulugh: The Effect of Ignorance
Distinguishing khiyar al-‘itq from khiyar al-bulugh on the excuse of ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rules of khiyar al-‘itq for an emancipated slave woman from those of khiyar al-bulugh for a free woman, specifically regarding the effect of ignorance, and list at least four of the six differences between the two options.

Core Idea
Ignorance of the option excuses the emancipated slave woman but not the free woman because of her service burden.

In Hanafi marriage law, a woman who gains her freedom while married is given an option: stay or leave. This khiyar al-‘itq has rules distinct from the khiyar al-bulugh that a minor receives upon reaching puberty. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains that a free woman's ignorance of her option does not excuse her delay, while a newly freed slave woman is excused because she was occupied with service. The commentary then lists six differences between the two options, making this a key reference for understanding how the law adapts to circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Options
First, identify that the passage contrasts two legal options: khiyar al-‘itq (option upon emancipation) and khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty). The free woman's option is khiyar al-bulugh; the emancipated slave woman's is khiyar al-‘itq.
Khiyar al-‘itq applies only to females, while khiyar al-bulugh applies to both males and females.
2
State the Ruling on Ignorance
The matn states: for the free woman (who is silent), ignorance is not an excuse. For the emancipated slave woman (mu‘taqa), ignorance is an excuse. Note the commentary clarifies that the free woman is not excused because she can spare time to learn the law, whereas the slave woman cannot due to service.
3
Analyze the Rationale (Illah)
The illah (effective cause) for the difference is the woman's occupation: a slave woman is busy serving her master and cannot find time to learn the legal ruling. Therefore, her ignorance is excused. A free woman has the opportunity to learn, so her ignorance is not excused.
This is a rational (dalil aqli) argument based on practicality and fairness.
4
List the Six Differences
The commentary enumerates six points where khiyar al-‘itq differs from khiyar al-bulugh: (1) applies only to females; (2) not invalidated by silence; (3) no qadi's ruling required; (4) extends to the end of the session; (5) ignorance is an excuse; (6) invalidated by any indication of aversion (contrary to khiyar al-bulugh for virgin or non-virgin). Commit these to memory for application.
Differences 1-5 are mentioned explicitly; difference 6 is also given.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage distinguishes khiyar al-‘itq (option upon emancipation) from khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty). The matn states that the rule for the emancipated slave woman (mu‘taqa) is different from the free woman: if she is ignorant of her option, she is excused, whereas the free woman is not. The commentary explains the rationale: an emancipated slave woman is occupied with serving her master and cannot spare time to learn the law. The commentary then lists six differences between the two options: (1) khiyar al-‘itq applies only to women, while khiyar al-bulugh applies to both males and females; (2) silence does not invalidate khiyar al-‘itq; (3) no qadi is needed for khiyar al-‘itq; (4) khiyar al-‘itq extends to the end of the session; (5) ignorance is an excuse in khiyar al-‘itq; (6) khiyar al-‘itq is invalidated by any indication of aversion, unlike the option of puberty for a virgin or thayyib. The commentator also notes that the disagreement (between the commentator and others) involves both ignorance and extension, not just ignorance alone.

Key Terms
معتقة (mu‘taqa): A female slave who has been emancipated (freed) by her master.
خيار العتق (khiyar al-‘itq): The option of a slave woman who was married during slavery to either keep or annul the marriage upon being freed.
جهل (jahl): Ignorance of the legal right (option); in the context of the free woman, it is no excuse; for the emancipated slave, it is an excuse.

D) Khiyar al-‘itq is established for both males and females, while khiyar al-bulugh is only for females.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance of the option excuses the emancipated slave woman in khiyar al-‘itq but not the free woman in khiyar al-bulugh, due to the slave's inability to learn the law while occupied with service.

Ignorance in Khiyar al-Bulugh vs Khiyar al-'Itq
Contrasting the effect of ignorance on two spousal options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ignorance in khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itq, apply the distinction to a given scenario, and explain the underlying rationale using the hadith on seeking knowledge.

Core Idea
A free woman is not excused for ignorance of her spousal option; a slave woman is excused because she is not obligated to seek knowledge.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah contrasts the effect of ignorance on two types of spousal options in Hanafi law: the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) and the option of emancipation (khiyar al-'itq). While a free virgin's silence after puberty invalidates her option even if she is ignorant, an emancipated slave woman's ignorance excuses her silence. The commentator uses the hadith 'Seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim and Muslimah' to justify the distinction, and enumerates six differences between the two options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
Recognize that khiyar al-bulugh is the option for a minor upon reaching puberty, and khiyar al-'itq is for a female slave upon emancipation. Note that the passage focuses on the effect of ignorance.
The option of puberty applies to both males and females; the option of emancipation only to females.
2
Contrast the ruling on ignorance
For khiyar al-bulugh: silence after knowledge (or after puberty) invalidates the option; ignorance is not an excuse. For khiyar al-'itq: ignorance is an excuse; silence does not invalidate the option.
The text explicitly says 'الجهل ليس بعذر في حقها' for the free woman and 'فجهلها عذر' for the slave woman.
3
Analyze the proof
The commentator cites the hadith 'طلب العلم فريضة على كل مسلم ومسلمة'. Explain how this hadith implies that free individuals must know their rights, so ignorance due to negligence is not excused. For slaves, service prevents learning, so ignorance is excused.
The hadith is marfu' with some weakness, but considered hasan. The 'necessary amount' of knowledge is intended.
4
List the six differences
The passage lists six differences between khiyar al-'itq and khiyar al-bulugh. Memorizing them helps apply the correct ruling in practice.
Differences: (1) Only for females, (2) not invalidated by silence, (3) no judge needed, (4) lasts till end of session, (5) ignorance is excuse, (6) voided only by clear aversion.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a scenario, determine whether ignorance is a valid excuse. If the person is free and has capacity to learn, she is not excused; if she is a slave or similarly occupied, she may be excused.
The illa (effective cause) is the obligation to seek knowledge; if that obligation is lifted due to circumstances, ignorance becomes an excuse.

Most of its chains are defective, and some imams authenticated some of its chains. Al-Mizzi said: its chains raise it to the rank of hasan, and Ibn al-Qattan, the companion of Ibn Majah, also declared it hasan. Al-Zayn al-'Iraqi elaborated on it in his تخريج أحاديث إحياء العلوم and others. The meaning of 'knowledge' in this hadith is the necessary amount.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the text of al-Wiqayah, specifically addressing the ruling on ignorance in two types of spousal options: the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) and the option of emancipation (khiyar al-'itq). The author begins by stating the main ruling: if a free virgin woman remains silent after reaching puberty or after learning that she has the right to choose, her silence is considered consent and her ignorance is not an excuse because she is not busy like a slave. This is contrasted with an emancipated slave woman (mu'taqah) who is given the option after emancipation; if she is ignorant of this right, her ignorance is excused because she is occupied with serving her master and cannot easily learn. The commentary then enumerates six differences between the two options: (1) the option of emancipation is only for females, not male slaves; (2) it is not invalidated by silence; (3) it does not require a judge's ruling; (4) it lasts only until the end of the session; (5) ignorance is an excuse; (6) it is voided only by clear aversion. The commentator notes that the difference applies to both ignorance and duration. Finally, a proof is cited: the hadith 'Seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim and Muslimah' is used to argue that free individuals are not excused for ignorance because they have the capacity to learn, whereas slaves are excused due to their circumstances. The authenticity of the hadith is discussed, noting that while its chains are weak, some scholars have graded it hasan, and the intended knowledge is the necessary amount for practice.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option given to a minor, upon reaching puberty, to annul or continue the marriage contract made by the guardian during minority.
خيار العتق (khiyar al-'itq): The option given to a female slave upon emancipation, if she was married by the master before emancipation, to choose between continuing the marriage or annulling it.
المعتقة (al-mu'taqah): The emancipated female slave who was married before emancipation and upon emancipation receives the option of khiyar al-'itq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the free woman is more intelligent than the slave woman.
B) Because seeking knowledge is obligatory upon every free Muslim, and the free woman has the capacity to learn, while the slave woman is occupied with service.
C) Because the emancipated slave woman has no husband.
D) Because the option of emancipation is weaker than the option of puberty.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a free woman's ignorance of her spousal option does not excuse her silence, but a slave woman's ignorance does, due to the differential obligation to seek knowledge.

Excuse of Ignorance for Minors and Obligation of Teaching
Objection and answer regarding the virgin's excuse by ignorance before puberty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a virgin reaching puberty is not excused for ignorance of khiyar al-bulugh, and explain the evidence from the hadith commanding guardians to teach children.

Core Idea
Guardians must teach children before puberty; ignorance afterward is not excused.

This lesson addresses a key objection in marriage law: if a virgin is not obligated before puberty, should she be excused for ignorance of her option to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty? The Hanafi response centers on the obligation of guardians to teach children approaching puberty, supported by the well-known hadith on commanding children to pray at seven.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector argues that since a pre-pubescent girl is not obligated by shar'i rules, her ignorance of the option of puberty should be a valid excuse.
The objection relies on the principle 'رفع القلم' (the pen is lifted) for minors.
2
Pinpoint the Hanafi Response
The commentator introduces the concept of 'irahaq' (approaching puberty) and argues that either the child or the guardian must ensure learning. Since guardians are commanded to teach, the child's ignorance after puberty is due to the guardian's negligence, not an excuse for the child.
The child is not obligated before puberty, but the guardian's duty creates an expectation of knowledge.
3
Examine the Hadith Evidence
The hadith 'مروا صبيانكم بالصلاة إذا بلغوا سبعاً' is cited. The imperative 'مروا' indicates a command on guardians to train children. This shows that children are not left without religious instruction; thus after puberty, ignorance of basics is not excused.
The hadith is from Sunan Abu Dawud and is used to prove the obligation of teaching.
4
Address the Counter-Objection on Slaves
A secondary objection about slaves is answered: they are also obligated, but are excused for negligence due to service to their master, unlike free persons.
This clarifies that the rule about excuse applies only to those with valid impediments.
5
Apply to the Case of Khiyar al-Bulugh
In Hanafi fiqh, a virgin's silence at the time of her marriage contract agreed by a guardian (other than father/grandfather) is not considered consent if she was not consulted. But once she reaches puberty, she is expected to know her option (khiyar al-bulugh). Ignorance is not accepted because she should have been taught.
The practical effect: the option is only valid if she was not taught and had no way to know.

[3] His saying: 'Either it is obligatory...' etc.; this alternative is not correct, for one who has not reached puberty is obligated to nothing, neither knowledge nor action. He only mentioned it as a detail and clarification of the answer. [4] His saying: 'Or it is obligatory...' etc.; its summary: When the child approaches puberty, it is obligatory upon the child's guardian to teach him what he will need after puberty, and he should not be left neglected, knowing nothing of the rulings of the religion. Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof commentary addressing an objection about whether a virgin (al-bikr) who has just reached puberty is excused for ignorance, specifically regarding the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh). The objector argues that before puberty she is not obligated, so ignorance should excuse her. The commentator responds by introducing the concept of 'irahaq' (approaching puberty) and argues that guardians must teach children before they reach puberty, citing the hadith commanding guardians to order children to pray at seven and beat them at ten. This shows that the child is not entirely without religious obligation in terms of learning. The commentator then addresses another objection about slaves: they are also obligated to learn the necessary amount, but are excused for negligence due to service. The commentary clarifies that the correct position is that minors are not obligated at all, but the guardian's duty to teach prevents the minor from having a valid excuse after puberty. The hadith 'رفع القلم' is cited to support that pre-pubescent children are not accountable. However, the practical effect is that once a child reaches puberty, ignorance of basic matters is not excusable because guardians are presumed to have taught them.

Key Terms
راهق (rahaqa): To approach or nearly reach puberty; the stage before actual bulugh.
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option of the girl upon reaching puberty to annul a marriage contracted by her guardian before puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a virgin who has just reached puberty not excused for ignorance of khiyar al-bulugh?
A) Because she is already obligated before puberty
B) Because guardians are obliged to teach her before puberty and she is presumed to have learned
C) Because the hadith 'رفع القلم' applies to all ages
D) Because the option of puberty is invalid in Hanafi fiqh

Key Takeaway
Ignorance of essential marital rights after puberty is not excused; guardians must teach children before they reach puberty.

Ignorance and the Obligation to Teach Religious Duties
Commentary on whether ignorance excuses the option of puberty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether ignorance excuses a minor who has reached puberty from exercising the option of puberty, and identify the hadith used to support the obligation of teaching religious duties.

Core Idea
Guardians must teach minors the essentials of faith; thus ignorance of the option of puberty is not excused.

When a minor married before puberty reaches puberty, they have the option to annul or confirm the marriage. But what if they were not taught about this option? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that a guardian's duty to instruct prevents the excuse of ignorance, supported by the hadith about commanding children to pray.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that since a child before puberty is not obligated to learn religious rulings, ignorance should excuse them from exercising the option. Understand the logic: if no duty to learn, then no blame for not knowing.
2
Locate the Hanafi response
The commentator counters that when a child approaches puberty, the duty to learn or the guardian's duty to teach arises. The child cannot be left neglected (suda).
The response establishes a positive obligation.
3
Examine the dalil
The hadith 'Command your children to pray when they are seven, and beat them when they are ten' is cited. This is a command to guardians, indicating wujub (obligation) of teaching prayer and similar essentials.
The hadith is from Abu Dawood, Tirmidhi, etc.
4
Apply to the case at hand
Since guardians must teach religious duties, a child who reaches puberty is presumed to have learned the essentials, including the option of puberty. Therefore, ignorance is not a valid excuse; the child is expected to know and act.

If it is said: Our discussion is about the virgin at the time of her reaching puberty, and before puberty she is not obligated by the religious duties. We say: When the boy and girl approach puberty, either they must learn faith and its rulings, or their guardian must teach them, and they should not be left neglected. The Prophet said: 'Command your children to pray when they are seven, and beat them when they are ten.' If you say: The generality of every Muslim male and female requires it to be obligatory upon the slave and slave woman also. [1] His saying: 'If it is said...' etc.; its summary: Just as the freed slave woman is excused due to ignorance, likewise the virgin who has reached puberty and the one who has reached puberty should be excused due to ignorance, for seeking knowledge is only obligatory upon the legally responsible person, and responsibility is after puberty, so it is not obligatory upon the boy and girl, so her lack of knowledge of the issue of the option of puberty is not considered negligence. [2] His saying: 'not obligated by the religious duties'; i.e., by knowledge of religious rulings and acting upon them, due to the hadith: 'The pen is lifted' i.e., the pen of responsibility from the boy, reported by Tirmidhi and others. The hadith mentions prayer specifically, but the intention is it and what is similar from the necessities. And the option of the young boy and the non-virgin woman is not invalidated without explicit consent or its indication. The explicit is that he says: I am pleased, and the indication is that he does something that indicates consent, like kissing, touching, the boy giving the dowry, and the non-virgin woman accepting the dowry. [1] His saying: 'the young boy'; i.e., the small child. [2] His saying: 'the non-virgin woman'; whether she was a non-virgin originally or was a virgin at marriage and her husband had intercourse with her, then she reached puberty. [3] His saying: 'is not invalidated'; the principle in this chapter is consideration of the state at the beginning of the marriage. For the small virgin girl, when she reaches puberty and is asked for consent to the marriage, if she is silent at the beginning of the marriage, her silence is consent. Likewise, if she has the option and reaches puberty and is silent, her silence is consent. As for the young boy and the non-virgin woman, if they are asked for consent at the beginning of the marriage, their silence is not consent; rather, consent must be by word or an action indicating it. Likewise, at the time of the option of puberty, their silence is not consent. Thus in al-Binayah. [4] His saying: 'like kissing'; with dammah: If he kisses or touches or has intercourse after puberty, that becomes evidence of his approval of the previous marriage. And consent from her side is indicated by her allowing her husband to have intercourse and demanding the obligatory maintenance. [0] His saying: 'the boy giving'; this is only evidence of consent if it was before consummation. But if he had consummated with her before his puberty, his payment of the dowry should not be [considered consent], and likewise her acceptance of the dowry.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) in marriage. The author discusses two main issues: (1) whether ignorance excuses a person who has just reached puberty from exercising the option, and (2) how the option is waived (i.e., by explicit or implicit consent).

The passage begins with an objection: Someone argues that since a virgin girl before puberty is not obligated to learn religious rulings, she should be excused if she is ignorant of the option of puberty. The commentator responds by explaining that when a child approaches puberty (murahiq), it is either obligatory upon them to learn the essentials of faith and its rulings, or upon their guardian to teach them. Neglecting them is not permissible. This is supported by the hadith commanding guardians to instruct children in prayer from age seven and discipline them from age ten. Thus, ignorance is not a valid excuse because guardians are expected to teach the child what is needed after puberty.

The commentator then extends this to slaves: Even slaves are obligated to learn the necessary religious knowledge, but they may be excused if occupied with their master's service.

Next, the text addresses the option itself: The option of a young boy (ghulam) and a non-virgin woman (thayyib) is not invalidated without either explicit consent (saying 'I am pleased') or implicit consent (an action indicating pleasure). Examples of implicit consent for the boy include giving the dowry; for the non-virgin, accepting the dowry. Additionally, kissing, touching, or intercourse after puberty are signs of consent.

For a virgin girl (bikr) who was married as a minor, her silence upon reaching puberty and being asked about the marriage is considered consent, just as her silence at the time of the initial marriage contract was consent. This is because the default for a virgin is that silence indicates consent. However, for a boy or non-virgin, silence does not indicate consent; they must explicitly express it.

The commentary clarifies that the ruling on consent at the time of the original marriage is analogous to the situation at the time of the option of puberty. This is based on the principle that the same standard applies at both moments.

Overall, the passage emphasizes that the option of puberty is a right that can be waived, but only through clear consent. The burden of teaching religious duties falls on guardians, so ignorance does not excuse the failure to exercise the option.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The right of a minor married before puberty to annul or confirm the marriage upon reaching puberty.
مُرُوا (muru): Command (imperative form), used in the hadith to indicate obligation on guardians.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the minor is obligated to know all rulings from birth.
B) Because the guardian has a duty to teach the minor religious essentials before puberty.
C) Because the option of puberty is automatically annulled at puberty.
D) Because the minor can always sue the guardian for negligence.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance of the option of puberty is not a valid excuse because guardians have a religious duty to teach children essential rulings, as supported by the hadith on commanding children to pray.

How the Option of Puberty is Waived: Explicit and Implicit Consent
Ruling on how the option of puberty is waived for a boy and a non-virgin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the types of consent (explicit and implicit) that waive the option of puberty for a boy and a non-virgin woman, and state the difference with a virgin's silence.

Core Idea
A boy or non-virgin must give explicit verbal consent or perform a clear action to waive the option; silence suffices only for a virgin.

The option of puberty is a right that can be waived, but how? The Hanafi school distinguishes between types of individuals: for a virgin girl, silence upon being asked is considered consent, but for a boy or a non-virgin woman, silence is not enough. This lesson details the required explicit or implicit expressions of consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on who cannot waive by silence
The text states: 'The option of the young boy and the non-virgin woman is not invalidated without explicit consent or its indication.' Understand that silence does not work for them.
Non-virgin includes a woman who was married and had intercourse, even if she was a virgin at marriage.
2
Define explicit consent (rida sarih)
Explicit consent is verbal, such as saying 'I am pleased' (raditu). This is straightforward.
3
Define implicit consent (rida dalalah)
Implicit consent is shown by actions: kissing, touching, giving the dowry (by the boy), or accepting the dowry (by the non-virgin). These actions clearly indicate satisfaction with the marriage.
The commentary also mentions intercourse after puberty as a sign of consent.
4
Apply the analogy from the initial marriage
The principle is the same as at the time of the marriage contract: for a virgin, silence is consent; for a boy or non-virgin, it is not. The same applies to the option of puberty by analogy.
This is based on the rule in al-Binayah.

[٢] قوله : غير مكلفة بالشرائع ؛ أي بعلم الأحكام الشرعية وعملها لحديث : (۲) رفع القلم ) : أي قلم التكليف عن الصبي، أخرجه الترمذي، وغيره. (٤) قوله : أو يجب... الخ ؛ حاصله : أن الصبي إذا قارب البلوغ يجب على ولي الصبي أن يعلمه ما يحتاج إليه بعد البلوغ، ولا ينبغي أن يترك مهملاً لا يعلم شيئاً من أحكام الدين، والغالب أن أولياءه لا يتركونه جاهلاً مطلقاً، فلا يعذرُ الصبي بالجهل. [0]قوله : مروا ... الخ ؛ بصيغة الأمر من الأمر، خطاب إلى الأولياء، وهو حديث أخرجه أبو داود والحاكم والترمذي والدارقطني وغيرهــم مرفوعاً، وفي رواية البزار عن أبي رافع مرفوعاً: «اضربوا أبناءكم على الصلاة إذا بلغوا – أظنه تسعاً – »، وأخرجه أبو نعيم في كتاب «المعرفة»، والطبراني كما بسطه السخاوي في «المقاصد الحسنة في الأحاديث المشتهرة على الألسنة ) ، والحديث وإن ذكـرت فـيه الصلاة خاصة، لكنَّ المراد هي وما يماثلها من الضروريات.
وخيارُ الغُلامِ والنيب لا يبطل بلا رضاء صريح، أو دلالته. الصريح أن يقول : رضيت، والدلالة أن يفعل ما يدلُّ على الرضا، كالقبلة، واللمس، واعطاء الغلام المهر ، وقبول التّيب المهر. [1] قوله : الغلام ؛ أي الطفل الصغير. [٢]قوله : والثيب ؛ سواء كانت ثيباً في الأصل أو كانت بكراً عند النكاح فوطئها زوجها، ثم بلغت. [٣] قوله : لا يبطل ؛ الأصل في هذا الباب الاعتبار بحالة ابتداء النكاح، فإن الصغيرة البكر إذا أدركت واستؤمرت للنكاح فسكتت عند ابتداء النكاح كان سكوتها رضاء ، فكذلك إذا كان لها الخيار وأدركت وسكتت كان سكوتها رضاء. والغلام والثيب إذا استؤمروا عند ابتداء النكاح لا يكون سكوتهما رضاء، بل لا بُدَّ من الرضاء قولاً أو فعل ما يدل عليه ، فكذلك عند خيار البلوغ لا يكون سكوتهما رضاء. كذا في «البناية (٢). [٤] قوله : كالقبلة ؛ - بالضم - : فإنه إذا قَبلَ أو لَمِسَ أو وَطِئ بعد البلوغ صَارَ ذلك دليلاً على ارتضائه بالنكاح السابق ، ومن الرضاء من جانبها دلالة تمكينها زوجها للوطء، وطلب الواجب من النفقة. [0]قوله : وإعطاء الغلام ؛ هذا إنما يكون دليلاً على الرضى إذا كان قبل الدخول، أما لو دخل بها قبل بلوغه ينبغي أن لا يكون دفعه المهر، وكذا قبولها المهر
If it is said: Our discussion is about the virgin at the time of her reaching puberty, and before puberty she is not obligated by the religious duties. We say: When the boy and girl approach puberty, either they must learn faith and its rulings, or their guardian must teach them, and they should not be left neglected. The Prophet said: 'Command your children to pray when they are seven, and beat them when they are ten.' If you say: The generality of every Muslim male and female requires it to be obligatory upon the slave and slave woman also. I say: Yes; the necessary amount is also obligatory upon them, but if they fall short in it, they are excused because they are occupied with the service of the master, while the free man and free woman are not excused. [1] His saying: 'If it is said...' etc.; its summary: Just as the freed slave woman is excused due to ignorance, likewise the virgin who has reached puberty and the one who has reached puberty should be excused due to ignorance, for seeking knowledge is only obligatory upon the legally responsible person, and responsibility is after puberty, so it is not obligatory upon the boy and girl, so her lack of knowledge of the issue of the option of puberty is not considered negligence. [2] His saying: 'not obligated by the religious duties'; i.e., by knowledge of religious rulings and acting upon them, due to the hadith: 'The pen is lifted' i.e., the pen of responsibility from the boy, reported by Tirmidhi and others. [3] His saying: 'either they must...' etc.; this part is not correct, for before puberty nothing is obligatory upon him, neither knowledge nor action; he only mentioned it as a detailed clarification for the answer. [4] His saying: 'or their guardian...' etc.; its summary: When the boy approaches puberty, the guardian of the boy must teach him what he needs after puberty, and he should not be left neglected knowing nothing of the rulings of religion, and usually his guardians do not leave him entirely ignorant, so the boy is not excused due to ignorance. [0] His saying: 'Command...' etc.; in the imperative form of command, addressed to guardians, and it is a hadith reported by Abu Dawood, al-Hakim, al-Tirmidhi, al-Daraqutni and others as marfu', and in a narration of al-Bazzar from Abu Rafi' marfu': 'Beat your children for prayer when they reach – I think it is nine –', and reported by Abu Nu'aym in Kitab al-Ma'rifah, and al-Tabarani as elaborated by al-Sakhawi in al-Maqasid al-Hasanah. The hadith mentions prayer specifically, but the intention is it and what is similar from the necessities. And the option of the young boy and the non-virgin woman is not invalidated without explicit consent or its indication. The explicit is that he says: I am pleased, and the indication is that he does something that indicates consent, like kissing, touching, the boy giving the dowry, and the non-virgin woman accepting the dowry. [1] His saying: 'the young boy'; i.e., the small child. [2] His saying: 'the non-virgin woman'; whether she was a non-virgin originally or was a virgin at marriage and her husband had intercourse with her, then she reached puberty. [3] His saying: 'is not invalidated'; the principle in this chapter is consideration of the state at the beginning of the marriage. For the small virgin girl, when she reaches puberty and is asked for consent to the marriage, if she is silent at the beginning of the marriage, her silence is consent. Likewise, if she has the option and reaches puberty and is silent, her silence is consent. As for the young boy and the non-virgin woman, if they are asked for consent at the beginning of the marriage, their silence is not consent; rather, consent must be by word or an action indicating it. Likewise, at the time of the option of puberty, their silence is not consent. Thus in al-Binayah. [4] His saying: 'like kissing'; with dammah: If he kisses or touches or has intercourse after puberty, that becomes evidence of his approval of the previous marriage. And consent from her side is indicated by her allowing her husband to have intercourse and demanding the obligatory maintenance. [0] His saying: 'the boy giving'; this is only evidence of consent if it was before consummation. But if he had consummated with her before his puberty, his payment of the dowry should not be [considered consent], and likewise her acceptance of the dowry.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) in marriage. The author discusses two main issues: (1) whether ignorance excuses a person who has just reached puberty from exercising the option, and (2) how the option is waived (i.e., by explicit or implicit consent).

The passage begins with an objection: Someone argues that since a virgin girl before puberty is not obligated to learn religious rulings, she should be excused if she is ignorant of the option of puberty. The commentator responds by explaining that when a child approaches puberty (murahiq), it is either obligatory upon them to learn the essentials of faith and its rulings, or upon their guardian to teach them. Neglecting them is not permissible. This is supported by the hadith commanding guardians to instruct children in prayer from age seven and discipline them from age ten. Thus, ignorance is not a valid excuse because guardians are expected to teach the child what is needed after puberty.

The commentator then extends this to slaves: Even slaves are obligated to learn the necessary religious knowledge, but they may be excused if occupied with their master's service.

Next, the text addresses the option itself: The option of a young boy (ghulam) and a non-virgin woman (thayyib) is not invalidated without either explicit consent (saying 'I am pleased') or implicit consent (an action indicating pleasure). Examples of implicit consent for the boy include giving the dowry; for the non-virgin, accepting the dowry. Additionally, kissing, touching, or intercourse after puberty are signs of consent.

For a virgin girl (bikr) who was married as a minor, her silence upon reaching puberty and being asked about the marriage is considered consent, just as her silence at the time of the initial marriage contract was consent. This is because the default for a virgin is that silence indicates consent. However, for a boy or non-virgin, silence does not indicate consent; they must explicitly express it.

The commentary clarifies that the ruling on consent at the time of the original marriage is analogous to the situation at the time of the option of puberty. This is based on the principle that the same standard applies at both moments.

Overall, the passage emphasizes that the option of puberty is a right that can be waived, but only through clear consent. The burden of teaching religious duties falls on guardians, so ignorance does not excuse the failure to exercise the option.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The right of a minor married before puberty to annul or confirm the marriage upon reaching puberty.
رضاء صريح (rida sarih): Explicit verbal consent, such as saying 'I am pleased' or 'I agree'.
رضاء دلالة (rida dalalah): Consent indicated by actions that clearly show pleasure, such as kissing, touching, or paying/receiving the dowry.
ثيب (thayyib): A non-virgin woman; in this context, she is treated differently from a virgin in consent rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What action by a non-virgin woman is considered implicit consent (rida dalalah) to waive the option of puberty?
A) Remaining silent when asked.
B) Accepting the maintenance (nafaqah) from her husband.
C) Leaving the marital home.
D) Denying the marriage.

Key Takeaway
A boy or non-virgin must explicitly state consent or perform a clear action to waive the option of puberty; silence is only sufficient for a virgin.

Consent for Boy and Non-Virgin: Explicit and Indicative
Explains types of consent for khiyar al-bulugh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit and indicative consent for khiyar al-bulugh for a boy and non-virgin, and apply the rule that silence does not constitute consent in these cases.

Core Idea
For a boy and non-virgin, silence never equals consent — only explicit words or clear actions suffice.

The option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) allows a minor or non-virgin to accept or reject a marriage contracted during their minority. But how is consent validly expressed? This passage clarifies that silence—enough for a virgin—is insufficient for a boy or non-virgin. Instead, they must provide explicit verbal consent or perform an action that clearly indicates acceptance, such as giving or receiving the dowry. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the validity of a marriage under the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the persons and their default consent rules
Note that the passage distinguishes three categories: virgin girl (bikr), boy (ghulam), and non-virgin (thayyib). Recall that for a virgin, silence at the marriage contract is consent. This is the baseline rule.
The principle is 'consideration of the state at the beginning of marriage'.
2
Apply the same logic to the option of puberty
The passage uses qiyas: just as at the initial contract, the boy/non-virgin cannot consent by silence, so at the option of puberty, silence is also invalid. Therefore, the option remains intact until proper consent is given.
The ruling is derived by analogy from the initial marriage contract.
3
Differentiate explicit vs. indicative consent
Explicit consent is the verbal statement 'radītu' (I am pleased). Indicative consent is an action that unequivocally implies acceptance, such as kissing, touching, the boy paying the mahr, or the non-virgin accepting the mahr.
The act must be clearly indicative; ambiguous actions are insufficient.
4
Apply the timing nuance for mahr
For the boy, giving the mahr is only indicative of consent if done after puberty and before consummation. If consummation occurred before puberty, subsequent payment of mahr does not indicate consent, because consummation itself may imply coercion or different circumstances.
This nuance prevents false inference of consent from post-consummation behavior.

Source Text

And the option of the boy and the non-virgin is not invalidated without explicit consent or its indication. Explicit is that he says: 'I am pleased,' and indication is that he does something that indicates consent, like kissing, touching, the boy giving the dowry, and the non-virgin accepting the dowry. He said: 'the boy' means the young child. He said: 'the non-virgin' whether she was originally a non-virgin or was a virgin at marriage but her husband had intercourse with her and then she reached puberty. He said: 'is not invalidated' the principle in this chapter is consideration of the state at the beginning of marriage. For the young virgin girl, when she reaches maturity and is asked for consent in marriage, her silence at the beginning of marriage is consent. Similarly, when she has the option and reaches maturity and is silent, her silence is consent. The boy and the non-virgin, when asked for consent at the beginning of marriage, their silence is not consent; rather, consent must be expressed or by an act indicating it. Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's ruling on the option of puberty for a boy or a non-virgin. It first states that the option is not invalidated without explicit or indicative consent. Explicit consent is the verbal statement 'I am pleased.' Indicative consent is an action that clearly implies acceptance, such as kissing, touching, the boy paying the mahr, or the non-virgin woman accepting the mahr. The commentator then explains the terms: 'boy' refers to a minor male; 'non-virgin' includes a woman who was deflowered either before marriage or after marriage by her husband even if she was a virgin at the time of marriage. The core legal principle here is that the standard for consent at the time of the option is the same as at the time of the initial marriage contract. For a virgin girl, silence at the time of the contract is consent, and similarly at the time of option, her silence is consent. For a boy and a non-virgin, silence is not consent at the contract, so likewise it is not consent at the option. The commentator then details the indicative acts: for the boy, paying the mahr after puberty is indicative of acceptance, but only if it occurs before consummation; if consummation occurred before puberty, then subsequent payment of mahr does not indicate consent. For the non-virgin, allowing her husband to have intercourse and demanding maintenance indicate consent.

Key Terms
غلام (ghulam): A minor male child who has not reached puberty.
ثيب (thayyib): A non-virgin woman, whether deflowered before marriage or by her husband after marriage.
رضاء صريح (rida' sarīḥ): Explicit verbal consent, e.g., saying 'radītu' (I am pleased).
رضاء دلالة (rida' dalālah): Consent indicated by actions that clearly show acceptance, like giving or receiving mahr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The boy says 'I am pleased'.
C) The boy pays the mahr to his wife after puberty, before consummation.
D) The boy leaves the gathering after the contract.

Key Takeaway
For a boy and non-virgin, the option of puberty is only invalidated by explicit verbal consent or a clear indicative action, never by silence.

Standing Up Does Not Invalidate the Option of Puberty
Distinguishes khiyar al-bulugh from khiyar al-‘itq regarding standing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-‘itq regarding the effect of standing up and state the Hanafi ruling that standing up does not invalidate the option of puberty.

Core Idea
Standing up from the session does not end the option of puberty; it lasts until consent is given.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqaya addresses a common point of confusion: does a child or non-virgin lose their option to annul a marriage simply by standing up from the gathering where they learned of it? The text clarifies that unlike the option of a freed slave (khiyar al-'itq), the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) is not interrupted by such actions, because it is a lifetime right that only ends with explicit or indicative consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
The passage discusses the option for the child (boy) and the non-virgin (thayyib) after reaching puberty or hearing news of marriage.
Both have khiyar al-bulugh.
2
State the ruling for these parties
Standing up does not invalidate their option. The text says 'لا يبطل خيار الطفل والثيب بقيامهما'.
3
Contrast with the freed slave
The option of the freed slave is invalidated by actions like standing that show aversion (الإعراض).
The passage explicitly contrasts: 'بخلاف خيار المعتقة'.
4
Understand the rationale
The option of puberty lasts for life ('وقته العمر'); it only ends with explicit or indicative consent.
Standing does not necessarily indicate final rejection.
5
Apply the distinction
In practice, the child and non-virgin retain the option even after leaving the session, while the freed slave does not.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بقيامهما عن المجلس قوله : ولا بقيامهما؛ أي لا يبطل خيار الطفل والثيب بقيامهما عن مجلس البلوغ، أو بلوغ خبر النكاح، بخلافِ خيار المعتقة، فإنه يبطل بما يدلُّ على الإعراض كالقيام من المجلس، وهذا الخيارُ وقتُهُ العمر، فيبقى حتى يوجد الرضاء صريحاً أو دلالة.
And not by their standing up from the session. He said: 'And not by their standing up' means the option of the child and the non-virgin is not invalidated by their standing up from the session of puberty or the arrival of the news of marriage, unlike the option of the freed slave, for it is invalidated by what indicates aversion, like standing up from the session. And this option's time is life, so it remains until consent is found explicitly or by indication.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage clarifies that for the boy (child) and the non-virgin woman, their option of puberty is not nullified simply by their standing up from the gathering where they learned of the marriage or reached puberty. This is different from the option of the freed slave ('itq), where any action showing disinterest, such as leaving the session, invalidates the option. The reason is that the option of puberty lasts for the entire lifetime of the person; it only ends when explicit or indicative consent is given. Standing up does not necessarily indicate rejection for a child or non-virgin; their silence could be due to other reasons. Therefore, the option remains until actual consent is established.

Key Terms
قيام من المجلس (qiyām min al-majlis): Standing up from the gathering where the marriage was announced or where puberty occurred.
خيار البلوغ (khiyār al-bulūgh): The option of a minor who reaches puberty to affirm or annul their marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) His option is transferred to his guardian.

Key Takeaway
The option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) for a child or non-virgin is not invalidated by standing up from the session; it continues until consent is given.

Requirement of Judge's Ruling for Annulment of Puberty Option
Explains procedure for annulment under puberty option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement of a judge's ruling for annulment under khiyar al-bulugh and distinguish it from khiyar al-'itq by explaining the underlying rationale of hidden harm.

Core Idea
Annulment under puberty option requires a judge because it imposes hidden harm on the husband; the freed slave's option does not.

In this passage, al-Haskafi explains a key procedural distinction between two marriage options: khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) and khiyar al-'itq (option of emancipation). While both allow the spouse to annul the marriage, the Hanafi school requires a judge's ruling for the former but not the latter. The reason lies in the nature of the harm involved — hidden versus apparent. Understanding this distinction clarifies when judicial authority is necessary to impose annulment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options and their rulings
First, recognize that khiyar al-bulugh applies to a minor who reaches puberty and wishes to annul the marriage contracted by a guardian. Khiyar al-'itq applies to a slave woman who is freed and finds that her husband now has greater power over her (e.g., more divorces). For the Hanafi school, a judge's ruling is required for the first but not the second.
The annulment in khiyar al-bulugh is called faskh and requires a qadi's decree.
2
Locate the rationale: hidden harm vs. prevention of increase
The text states that annulment in khiyar al-bulugh is 'to repel hidden harm' (دفع ضرر خفي). This harm is the possibility of defect in the guardian's affection, which is not apparent. In contrast, khiyar al-'itq is 'prevention of increase of ownership' on the husband's part; the harm is clear – the husband now has more rights than before emancipation.
Harm is hidden when it cannot be observed externally; judicial investigation is needed.
3
Understand why hidden harm requires judicial imposition
Since the harm is hidden, the annulment becomes an imposition (إلزام) on the husband – it ends the marriage against his will without clear fault. Imposing such a change requires the authority of a judge to ensure fairness and avoid injustice.
This is a rational (aqli) argument: imposition of hidden harm needs a qualified decision-maker.
4
Contrast with the freed slave's case: no harm imposed
For a freed slave woman, the annulment merely prevents the husband from gaining increased rights over her (e.g., more divorces). This only benefits her and does not impose any harm on the husband. Hence, she can annul without a judge; her own decision suffices.
The husband's position is not worsened by her annulment; he merely loses a potential gain.
5
Note the practical consequence
In practice, a Hanafi who reaches puberty and wishes to annul must seek a judge's decree; mere mutual agreement is ineffective. A freed slave woman can simply declare annulment. This difference affects how cases are handled in court.
Other schools may allow mutual annulment in both cases, but the Hanafi view is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط القضاء لفسخ مَن بَلَغَ لَا مَن عتقت فإن في الأَوَّل إلزام الضَّرَرِ على الزوج بخلافِ فسخ المعتقة، فإنَّه منع زيادة الملكِ للزوج عليها رضاً ؛ لأنه لا بد منه أقام أو فسخ كذا في «الفتح». قوله : وشرط؛ بصيغة المجهول: يعني يشترط لفسخ نكاح من بلغ ذكراً كان أو أنثى إن اختار الفسخ قضاء القاضي، فلا ينفسخ بمجرد التراضي، بل لا بد فيه من حكم، ولا كذلك خيار المعتقة فإنها مستقلة في فسخ نكاحها لا يحتاج فيه إلى قضاء القاضي. قوله : فإن في الأول؛ أي خيار من بلغ، وحاصله: أن الفسخ في خيار البلوغ لدفع ضرر خفي، وهو تمكن الخلل لقصور شفقة المنكح؛ ولهذا يشتمل الذكر والأنثى، وإذا كان الضرر خفياً لا يطلع عليه، فيكون الفسخُ إلزاماً في حق الزوج، والإلزام يفتقرُ فيه إلى قضاء القاضي. قوله : فإنه منع ... الخ؛ توضيحه: أن اعتبار الطلاق عندنا على ما سيأتي في موضعه بالنساء، فيملك الزوج على الحرّة ثلاث تطليقات، ولا تبين من زوجها بالبينونة التي تحتاج إلى تحليل زوج آخر إلا بالطلاق الثالث سواء كان الزوج حراً أو عبداً. ويملك الزوج حراً كان أو عبداً على زوجته الرقيقة ثنتين، فتبين منه بالطلقتين. إذا تمهد هذا، فاعلم أن خيار المعتقة إنّما شُرِعَ دَفْعاً لضرر نفسها بملك زوجها عليها أزيـد مـا ملـكـه قـبل عتقها، فتخيّر لذلك إن شاءت قرت تحته، وإن شاءت فسخت، ففي فسخها بهذا الخيار ليس إلا دفع الضرر عن نفسها، ولا إلزام فيه على الزوج.
And the condition of the judge's ruling for annulment of one who has reached puberty, not for one who has been freed. Because in the first, there is imposition of harm on the husband, contrary to the annulment of the freed slave, for it is prevention of increase of ownership of the husband over her, not imposition, because it is necessary whether he stays or annuls – as in al-Fath. He said: 'and the condition' – in passive form – means the annulment of the marriage of one who has reached puberty, male or female, if he chooses annulment, requires the judge's ruling. So it is not annulled by mere mutual agreement; rather, a ruling is necessary. Not so the option of the freed slave, for she is independent in annulling her marriage; she does not need the judge's ruling. He said: 'because in the first' – i.e., the option of one who has reached puberty. Its summary is that annulment in the option of puberty is to repel hidden harm, which is the possibility of defect due to deficiency of affection of the marriage guardian. For this reason, it includes both male and female. When the harm is hidden and cannot be observed, then the annulment becomes an imposition on the husband, and imposition requires the judge's ruling. He said: 'because it is prevention' – its clarification: The consideration of divorce according to us will come in its place regarding women. The husband owns three divorces over a free woman, and she does not separate from her husband with the separation that requires a second husband except by the third divorce, whether the husband is free or slave. The husband, whether free or slave, owns two divorces over his slave wife, so she separates from him with two divorces. Now that this is clear, know that the option of the freed slave was legislated to repel harm to herself from her husband owning more over her than he owned before her emancipation. So she has a choice: if she wishes she remains under him, and if she wishes she annuls. So in her annulment through this option, there is only repelling harm from herself, and there is no imposition on the husband.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage discusses the procedural difference between two types of options: khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) and khiyar al-'itq (option of emancipation). For a person who reaches puberty and wishes to annul the marriage, the annulment requires a judge's ruling. It does not take effect by mere mutual agreement between the spouses. The reason is that the annulment in khiyar al-bulugh is based on a hidden harm – the possibility that the person who contracted the marriage (usually a guardian) may have had deficient affection, leading to a flawed contract. This harm is not apparent, so the annulment is imposed on the husband, and imposition of such a significant change requires judicial authority. In contrast, for a freed slave woman, her option is intended to prevent harm to herself: if her husband now owns more rights over her (e.g., more divorces) than before, she can annul to avoid that increase. This annulment is purely for her benefit and does not impose any harm on the husband, so she can do it without a judge. The passage also explains the background of divorce: a husband has three divorces over a free wife, but only two over a slave wife. After emancipation, the husband's increased power over her (since she is now free) is the harm being prevented. Therefore, the freed slave can annul independently.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of the marriage contract by the option of puberty or emancipation.
إلزام الضرر (ilzām al-ḍarar): Imposition of harm; in this context, the annulment is seen as imposing harm on the husband because the marriage ends against his will.
خيار البلوغ (khiyār al-bulūgh): Option of puberty: the right of a minor who was married by a guardian to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.
خيار العتق (khiyār al-'itq): Option of emancipation: the right of a slave woman who is freed to annul the marriage if her husband gains increased power over her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the annulment of a marriage under the option of puberty require a judge's ruling, while the option of a freed slave does not?
A) Because the female cannot act without a judge.
B) Because the annulment in puberty imposes harm on the husband due to hidden defect, whereas the freed slave's annulment only prevents harm to herself.
C) Because the guardian must approve the annulment.
D) Because the husband must consent.

Key Takeaway
The requirement of a judge's ruling for annulment under khiyar al-bulugh is based on the hidden nature of the harm, whereas khiyar al-'itq involves no imposition on the husband and requires no judge.
