Inheritance Before Exercising the Option of Puberty
Ruling on inheritance before option of puberty exercised

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance when a spouse dies before the option of puberty is exercised and explain why the marriage's validity is the basis.

Core Idea
A valid marriage at inception creates inheritance rights; the option of puberty does not retroactively void it.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses a critical scenario in marriage contracts of minors. When one spouse dies before the other exercises the option to annul upon reaching puberty, the living spouse inherits. The key insight is that the marriage was originally valid (sahih), and the option to annul does not negate that validity unless actually exercised.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage considers a marriage contracted during the minority of both spouses, where one dies before the option of puberty is exercised.
2
State the ruling
The surviving spouse inherits from the deceased, regardless of whether the death occurred before or after puberty.
This includes the case where the deceased was still a minor.
3
Understand the legal basis
The marriage was valid when contracted. The option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) does not invalidate the marriage retroactively; it merely gives a right to annul going forward.
The existence of the option itself indicates the marriage was valid, as an option to annul only exists for a valid contract.
4
Contrast with the alternative
If the spouses actually separated (exercised the option) before death, then inheritance is cut off because the marriage is no longer valid at death.
The passage explicitly states: 'if one of them separates after separation, neither inherits'.
5
Apply to a concrete case
Consider a 15-year-old boy married to a 14-year-old girl by a guardian other than father. The boy dies at 16 before the girl exercises her option. She inherits from him because the marriage was valid from its inception.
This shows the practical consequence of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مات أحدهما قبل التفريق بلغ أو لا وَرِثَهُ الآخر فإن اعتبار الطلاق عندنا بالنِّساء ، فإذا أعتقت صار الملك عليها بثلاث تطليقات بعدما كان بتطليقتين، ويكون الفسخ امتناعاً عن هذا، فلا يحتاج إلى قضاء القاضي. (وإن مات أحدهما قبل التفريق بلغ أو لا وَرِثَهُ الآخر) الزوج، ومثله لا يحتاج الى القضاء. [١] قوله : عندنا ؛ احتراز عن قول الشافعي الله وغيره من أن عدد الطلاق معتبر بحال الزوج، والعدة بالنساء، قال الإمام محمد الله في «الموطأ» بعد أن أخرج عن عثمان وزيد بن ثابت وابن عمر الله ما يدل على أن الطلاق بالرجال : قد اختلف الناس في هذا ، فأما ما عليه فقهاؤنا ، فإنّهم يقولون الطلاق بالنساء والعدة بهنَّ؛ لأنَّ الله قال : فَطَلِقُوهُنَّ لِعِدَّتِهِنَّ (١) ، فإنما الطلاق للعدة ، فإذا كانت حرّة وزوجها عبد فعدَّتُها ثلاثة قروء، وطلاقها ثلاث تطليقات للعدة كما قال الله ، وإذا كانت الحرّة شملت الأمة فعدَّتُها حيضتان وطلاقهما للعدة تطليقتان كما قال الله جلالة». انتهى. (وقد أوضحت المسألة مع دلائلها في التعليق المسجد على موطأ محمد) [٢] قوله : وإن مات ؛ يعني إن مات أحد الزوجين اللذين زُوِّجا في حال صباهما أحدهما قبل أن يفسخا العقد بخيار البلوغ ورثه الآخر، وهو الحي منهما سواء كان موته قبل البلوغ أو بعده ؛ لأن النكاح السابق كان صحيحاً فيتحقق منشأ الوراثة، فإن خيار الفسخ ليس عين الفسخ ، بل هو منبئ عن صحة النكاح إذ لولاه لما كان الخيار. [٣] قوله : قبل التفريق ؛ فإن كان أحدهما بعد التفريق لم يرث أحدهما من الآخر ؛ لفوات موجب الوراثة وفقده عند الموت.
If one of them dies before separation, whether [the surviving spouse] has reached puberty or not, the other inherits from him. For verily the consideration of divorce with us is based on women. So if she becomes free, her ownership becomes three divorces after it was two divorces, and the annulment is an abstention from this, so it does not require a judge's ruling. (And if one of them dies before separation, whether [the surviving spouse] has reached puberty or not, the other inherits from him) the husband, and likewise it does not require a judge's ruling. [1] His statement: 'with us' is a precaution against the saying of al-Shafi'i and others that the number of divorces is considered according to the state of the husband, and the waiting period is based on women. Imam Muhammad said in al-Muwatta after narrating from Uthman, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Ibn Umar what indicates that divorce is by men: 'People have differed in this. As for what our jurists hold, they say: divorce is by women and the waiting period is by them because Allah said: "Divorce them for their waiting period." So divorce is for the waiting period. So if a free woman has a slave husband, her waiting period is three menstrual cycles, and her divorce is three divorces for the waiting period, as Allah said. And if the free woman includes the slave woman, her waiting period is two menstruations, and her divorce for the waiting period is two divorces, as Allah said.' End. (I have explained the issue with its proofs in al-Ta'liq al-Masjid on Muwatta Muhammad) [2] His statement: 'And if one of them dies' means if one of the two spouses who were married in their childhood dies before annulling the contract by the option of puberty, the other inherits from him, whether the death was before puberty or after, because the previous marriage was valid, thereby establishing the origin of inheritance. For the option of annulment is not the annulment itself, rather it indicates the validity of the marriage, since otherwise there would be no option. [3] His statement: 'before separation' so if one of them separates after separation, neither inherits from the other because the cause of inheritance has been lost and is absent at the time of death.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from al-Wiqayah, presenting a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. The ruling: if one spouse dies before the other exercises the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh), the living spouse inherits from the deceased, regardless of whether the death occurred before or after puberty. The proof is that the original marriage was valid (sahih), and the option to annul does not retroactively invalidate it; rather, the existence of the option itself confirms the marriage's validity. The commentary then addresses a related issue: the number of divorces (three for a free woman, two for a slave woman) is based on the woman's status, not the man's. This is the Hanafi position, supported by the Quranic verse 'Divorce them for their waiting period' (65:1), indicating that divorce is tied to the 'iddah, which depends on the woman's condition. The passage contrasts this with al-Shafi'i's view that the number of divorces follows the husband's status. The Hanafi response, attributed to Imam Muhammad in al-Muwatta', argues that since the Quran connects divorce to the 'iddah, and the 'iddah varies by the woman's status (free slave), the number of divorces must also vary accordingly. Finally, the commentary clarifies that if the spouses separate before death (i.e., actually annul the marriage), then inheritance is cut off because the cause of inheritance is lost.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option given to a minor upon reaching puberty to annul a marriage contracted during their minority by a guardian other than the father or grandfather.
الطلاق بالنساء (al-talaq bi al-nisa'): The principle that the number of divorces is determined by the status of the woman (free or slave), not the man.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a marriage contracted during minority, if one spouse dies before the option of puberty is exercised, does the surviving spouse inherit?
A) Yes, because the marriage was valid in origin.
B) No, because the marriage is voidable.
C) Only if the deceased died after puberty.
D) Only if the marriage was consummated.

Key Takeaway
A valid marriage at inception creates inheritance rights; the option to annul does not retroactively void it, so the surviving spouse inherits even if the option has not been exercised.

Divorce Count Based on the Woman’s Status
Inter-madhhab dispute on divorce count by woman's status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that the number of divorces is determined by the woman's status and contrast it with the Shafi'i position, citing the Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
The number of divorces follows the woman's waiting period because the Quran ties divorce to the 'iddah.

This passage also delves into a significant inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school holds that the number of divorces a woman can receive depends on her own status (free or slave), not her husband's. They derive this from the Quranic verse 'Divorce them for their waiting period' (65:1), arguing that divorce is legislated in relation to the 'iddah, which varies by the woman's condition. Al-Shafi'i, however, maintains that the number of divorces follows the husband's status, since divorce is the husband's act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim
The number of divorces is based on the woman's status: free woman gets three, slave woman gets two.
The passage explicitly states: 'the consideration of divorce with us is based on women'.
2
Locate the proof
The Hanafi proof is Quran 65:1: 'Divorce them for their waiting period.' The verse connects divorce to the waiting period ('iddah), which depends on the woman's status.
Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Muwatta' is cited, which uses this verse.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Al-Shafi'i argues that the number of divorces follows the husband's status because divorce is an act of the husband. The 'iddah is separate based on the woman.
The passage underscores: 'with us' is a precaution against al-Shafi'i's saying.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the Quran directly ties divorce to the 'iddah, so the number of divorces must correspond to the 'iddah, which is by women. Therefore, divorces are by women.
Imam Muhammad's statement provides a clear reasoning.
5
Apply to scenarios
If a free woman marries a slave, she has three divorces (Hanafi) vs. two (Shafi'i). If a slave woman marries a free man, she has two divorces (Hanafi) vs. three (Shafi'i). This is the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
This shows the real-world impact of the dispute.

Definition of Wali and Asaba in Marriage
Definition of wali and elaboration on asaba categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of wali in marriage as an asaba and distinguish it from the wali in financial matters.

Core Idea
The wali in marriage is any asaba by himself — not limited to father or grandfather.

This passage from the matn defines the wali (guardian) in marriage as the asaba (agnate). It clarifies that the wali's authority is based on being an agnate relative, and it distinguishes this from guardianship in financial matters, which is far more restricted. Understanding this definition is essential for identifying who may validly contract a marriage according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term being defined
The passage defines 'al-wali' in the context of marriage. Note that it says 'al-wali huwa al-asaba' — the guardian is the asaba.
The definition is for the chapter of marriage, not for financial transactions.
2
Understand the definition: wali = asaba
The wali is defined as the asaba for the validity of the marriage. This means that any male agnate (asaba by himself) has the right to act as wali, not just the father or grandfather.
The marriage is valid (sahih) even if it is not binding (lazim), which is different from a fuduli contract.
3
Distinguish marriage guardianship from financial guardianship
The passage explicitly contrasts the wali in marriage with the wali in financial matters. For marriage, all asaba are eligible; for finances, only the father, his executor, the grandfather, his executor, the judge, and his deputy.
This distinction is crucial to avoid conflating two different types of guardianship.
4
Classify the types of asaba
The passage explains that asaba can be: (1) by himself (male agnate), (2) by another (female with fixed share who becomes asaba through a male), or (3) with another (female with fixed share who becomes asaba with another female). Only the first category acts as wali.
The wali must be an asaba by himself, which limits guardianship to male agnates.

[٤] قوله : العصبة بنفسه ؛ قال في «الوجيز»: هو إما عصبة بنفسه : وهو كلُّ ذكر يدلي : أي ينتسب إلى الميت لا بالأنثى، أو عصبة بغيره : وهو أُنثى ذات سهم تصير عصبة بالذكر، أو عصبة مع غيره : وهو كل أنثى ذات سهم تصير عصبة مع الأنثى كالأخت مع البنت.
The Book of Marriage, Chapter of the Guardian and Compatibility. And the guardian is the asaba for the validity of the marriage between them. (And the guardian) is the asaba: meaning intended is the asaba by himself. [1] His statement: 'for the validity of the marriage between them' means the validity of its contract even if it is not binding, contrary to the contract of a fuduli (unauthorized agent), for if the fuduli marries by contracting marriage between a man and a woman without their permission, and one of them dies before ratification, the other does not inherit from him, because the marriage of a fuduli is suspended and becomes void by death. As in al-Hidayah. (2) His statement: 'And the guardian' means in the chapter of marriage, and he is the one who has the authority to marry minors, whether compulsory or non-compulsory. As for the guardian in financial transactions, he is the father and his executor, and the grandfather and his executor, and the judge and his deputy only. [3] His statement: 'the asaba' - with two fatha - is one of three types of heirs. For the heir is either one whose share is fixed by law: like the husband, wife, mother, uterine brother, etc., so he is a dhul-fard (fixed-share heir). Or one who takes what remains of the fixed-share heirs, and when alone takes all the property, so he is an asaba like the son and son's son. Or one who is neither this nor that, so he is from the dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives) like the sister's son. And the description of asaba may combine with fard for an heir depending on different circumstances, like the father: he is a dhul-fard with the son, for his share is one-sixth, and he is an asaba with the daughter. So the guardian in this chapter is the one who is characterized by asaba, whether it is mere asaba without fard like the son or not like the father. [4] His statement: 'the asaba by himself'; he said in al-Wajiz: it is either asaba by himself: which is every male who traces lineage to the deceased without a female; or asaba by another: which is a female with a fixed share who becomes asaba through a male; or asaba with another: which is every female with a fixed share who becomes asaba with another female, like the sister with the daughter.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines the wali (guardian) in marriage as the asaba (agnate). It begins by stating that the wali is the asaba for the validity of the marriage. The commentary explains that 'validity' here means the contract is valid (sahih) even if not binding (lazim), unlike the marriage contracted by a fuduli (unauthorized agent), which is suspended (mawquf) and becomes void upon the death of a party before ratification. The passage then distinguishes between two types of guardianship: guardianship in marriage (wilayat al-nikah) and guardianship in financial matters (wilayat al-tasarruf al-mali). The wali in marriage is defined as anyone who has the authority to marry minors, whether compulsory (jabri) or non-compulsory; this includes all asaba. The wali in financial matters is limited to the father, his executor, the grandfather, his executor, the judge, and his deputy only. Next, the passage explains the term 'asaba' as a category of heirs. Heirs are of three types: (1) dhul-fard (fixed-share heirs) like husband, wife, mother; (2) asaba (agnates) who take the residue, like son and son's son; and (3) dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives) like sister's son. The description of asaba may combine with fard in some heirs like the father, who is a fixed-share heir (1/6) when there is a son, but becomes an asaba when with a daughter. Thus, the wali in marriage is anyone who has the characteristic of asaba, whether pure asaba (like son) or combined with fard (like father). Finally, the passage elaborates on the three types of asaba: (a) asaba by himself (every male who traces lineage to the deceased without a female), (b) asaba by another (a female with a fixed share who becomes asaba through a male, e.g., daughter with son), and (c) asaba with another (a female with a fixed share who becomes asaba with another female, e.g., sister with daughter).

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): In this chapter, the guardian who has authority to contract marriage, specifically defined as an asaba (agnate) by himself.
العصبة (al-asaba): A category of heir who takes the residue after fixed-share heirs; in marriage, the asaba are the ones who can act as guardians.
ذو فرض (dhu fard): A fixed-share heir whose share is prescribed by the Quran, e.g., husband, wife, mother.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized agent who contracts a marriage without permission from the parties; such a marriage is suspended (mawquf) until ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who can be a wali (guardian) in marriage?
A) Only the father and grandfather.
B) Any asaba by himself (male agnate).
C) Any male relative, including cousins.
D) The judge only.

Key Takeaway
The wali in marriage is any asaba by himself, and this is the Hanafi position, distinguishing it from other schools and from financial guardianship.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
The Wali Must Be an Asabah: Types of Residuary Guardians in Hanafi Nikah
Defining marriage guardian as agnate and classifying asabah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that the marriage guardian (wali) must be an asabah, and identify the three types of asabah (bi-nafsihi, bi-ghayrihi, ma'a ghayrihi) with examples.

Core Idea
Only agnates (asabah) can be marriage guardians in Hanafi law.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a marriage contract depends on the presence of a guardian (wali) who is an 'asabah' – an agnate relative. This lesson unpacks the definition of 'asabah' inherited from the science of fara'id (inheritance law) and applies it to the marriage context. You will learn the three subcategories of asabah and understand why certain relatives qualify as wali while others do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on wali
The passage states: 'The guardian is the asabah.' This establishes a condition for marriage validity: the wali must be an agnate relative. Note that this is a shart (condition), not a rukn (pillar).
Contrast with a fuduli marriage (third party without authorization) which is suspended and voids on death.
2
Define asabah in inheritance terms
Asabah is one of three categories of heirs: prescribed share holders (ashab al-furud), residuaries (asabah), and uterine relatives (dhawi al-arham). The wali is an asabah, meaning he takes the residual inheritance or the entire estate when alone.
Some heirs, like the father, can combine both prescribed share and residuary status.
3
Distinguish the three types of asabah
The passage classifies asabah into: (1) asabah bi-nafsihi – a male agnate related without a female intermediary (e.g., son, brother, paternal uncle); (2) asabah bi-ghayrihi – a female who becomes residuary through a male (e.g., daughter with son); (3) asabah ma'a ghayrihi – a female who becomes residuary with another female (e.g., sister with daughter).
The 'other' in bi-ghayrihi is a male asabah; in ma'a ghayrihi, both are female prescribed share holders.
4
Apply the types to marriage guardianship
Only asabah bi-nafsihi (males) and potentially asabah bi-ghayrihi? The passage notes that females who are asabah (bi-ghayrihi or ma'a ghayrihi) do NOT have guardianship over their insane relatives. This implies that in marriage, the wali is typically a male asabah bi-nafsihi, though the text says the wali is 'the one characterized by asabah' – this requires careful interpretation.
The passage later states: 'as for the asabah by another, like the daughter when she becomes an asabah through the son, she has no guardianship over her insane mother.' So female asabah are not wali.
5
Identify the cross-madhhab dispute
The Hanafi school is stricter: only asabah can be wali. Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) allow any male relative, including non-agnates like maternal uncles. This difference arises from varying definitions of 'asabah' and the proofs used.
The practical consequence: in Hanafi, a marriage contracted by a maternal uncle is invalid; in Shafi'i it is valid.

Book of Marriage, Chapter of Guardian and Compatibility. And the guardian: the asabah (agnates). For the validity of marriage between them. (And the guardian): the asabah: meaning the asabah by himself. [1] His saying: for the validity of marriage between them: meaning the validity of its contract even if not binding, contrary to the contract of a intermediary (al-fuduli), for if the intermediary marries (contracts) between a man and a woman without their permission, then one of them dies before ratification, the other does not inherit from him, because the marriage of the intermediary is suspended and becomes void by death. Thus in al-Hidayah. (2) His saying: and the guardian: i.e., in the chapter of marriage, and he is the one who has the authority to marry off minors, compulsory or non-compulsory. As for the guardian in financial transactions, he is only the father and his executor, the grandfather and his executor, and the judge and his deputy. [3] His saying: the asabah: it is (with two fathas) one of the three divisions of heirs. For the heir is either one whose share is prescribed in the law: like the husband, wife, mother, uterine brother, etc., he is a prescribed share holder. Or one who takes what is left by the prescribed share holders, and when alone takes all the property: he is an asabah like the son and son's son. Or one who is neither this nor that: he is from the uterine relatives like the daughter's son. And the quality of asabah may combine with prescribed share in an heir depending on conditions like the father, for he is a prescribed share holder with the son (his share is one-sixth) and an asabah with the daughter. So the guardian in this chapter is the one who is characterized by asabah, whether it is asabah free from prescribed share like the son, or not like the father. [4] His saying: asabah by himself: He said in al-Wajiz: It is either asabah by himself: which is every male who relates (i.e., is related to the deceased) not through a female, or asabah by another: which is a female who has a prescribed share and becomes an asabah through a male, or asabah with another: which is every female who has a prescribed share and becomes an asabah with a female like the sister with the daughter. (1) (al-Hidayah 3:284). [PAGE_084] Umda al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqaya. On the arrangement of inheritance and preclusion (hajb). That is, a male who is connected without the mediation of a female. As for the asabah by another, like the daughter when she becomes an asabah through the son, she has no guardianship over her insane mother, and likewise the asabah with another, like the sister with the daughter, she has no guardianship over her insane sister. [0] (On the arrangement of inheritance and preclusion): meaning the part is given priority even if lower. [1] His saying: without the mediation of a female: for the one in whose lineage the female enters is not an asabah, like the children of the mother, for they are from the prescribed share holders, and like the maternal grandfather and the daughter's son, for they are from the uterine relatives. If you say: the brother from father and mother is an asabah by himself although the mother enters into his relation to the deceased? I say: The paternal kinship is the basis for entitlement to asabah, for when it is alone it suffices to establish asabah, unlike the maternal kinship, for it alone is not suitable as a cause for establishing it, so it is disregarded in entitlement to asabah. Thus in al-Sharifiyya Sharh al-Fara'id al-Sirajiyya. [2] His saying: as for the asabah by another; the difference between asabah by another and asabah with another: that the 'other' in asabah by another is an asabah who makes a prescribed share holder an asabah, like the daughter becomes asabah with the son, and he is an asabah; while in asabah with another, the 'other' does not become an asabah: like the sister becomes asabah with the daughter, and both are prescribed share holders. [3] His saying: like the sister; for her share is half if she is one, and for two sisters two-thirds. If she is with a daughter, she is an asabah. The sister here means the full or consanguine sister; as for the uterine sister, she does not become an asabah with the daughter.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of Marriage, specifically on the condition of the guardian (wali) and the definition of asabah. The author begins by stating that the wali must be an asabah (agnate relative). This is a ruling: the validity of marriage depends on having a guardian who is an asabah. The commentary then distinguishes this from a marriage contract made by a third party without authorization (fuduli), which is suspended and becomes void upon the death of either party before ratification. Next, the commentary explains that the wali in marriage is the one with authority to marry off minors, whether compulsory or not. This is contrasted with the guardian in financial matters, who is limited to the father, his executor, grandfather, his executor, and the judge or his deputy. The passage then defines asabah as one of three categories of heirs: prescribed share holders, asabah (residuaries), and uterine relatives. It notes that some heirs, like the father, can combine both a prescribed share and residuary status depending on the presence of other heirs. The wali in marriage is anyone characterized by asabah, whether purely residuary or not. The commentary further breaks down asabah into three types: asabah by himself (male agnate without female mediation), asabah by another (female who becomes residuary through a male), and asabah with another (female who becomes residuary with another female). Examples are given: the daughter becomes asabah by the son, and the sister becomes asabah with the daughter. The passage then explains that asabah by himself must be a male related without an intervening female; those related through a female, like uterine children or maternal grandfather, are not asabah. A potential objection regarding the full brother (who is an asabah despite having a mother) is answered by stating that paternal kinship is the basis for asabah, and maternal kinship is irrelevant. The commentary also clarifies the difference between asabah by another and asabah with another: in the former, the 'other' (the male) is an asabah who makes the female an asabah; in the latter, both are prescribed share holders. Finally, it specifies that the sister who becomes asabah with the daughter is the full or consanguine sister, not the uterine sister.

Key Terms
ولي (waliy): The guardian in marriage, who must be an asabah (agnate relative).
عصبة (asabah): A category of heirs who take the residual inheritance; in marriage, the one who has the right to give in marriage.
عصبة بنفسه (asabah bi-nafsihi): A male agnate who is a residuary by himself, i.e., related without an intervening female.
عصبة بالغير (asabah bi-ghayrihi): A female who becomes a residuary through a male, e.g., daughter with son.
عصبة مع الغير (asabah ma'a ghayrihi): A female who becomes a residuary with another female, e.g., sister with daughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following individuals CANNOT serve as a wali in marriage in the Hanafi school?
A) The bride's full brother
B) The bride's paternal uncle
C) The bride's maternal uncle
D) The bride's son (if she has one)

Key Takeaway
The wali in marriage must be an asabah; the three types of asabah are: by himself (male agnate), by another (female through male), and with another (female with another female as co-residuary). Females who are asabah do not have guardianship over their relatives.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner

Conditions of the Wali in Marriage
Stating the conditions for a valid wali in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for a valid wali in Hanafi marriage and identify which condition is proven by Quranic verse.

Core Idea
The wali must be free, sane, adult, and Muslim — without these, the marriage contract is invalid.

This lesson examines the essential qualities of a marriage guardian (wali) as stipulated in the Hanafi school. The text from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the conditions derived from the matn. Understanding these conditions is crucial because a marriage contracted through an unqualified wali may be invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conditions
The text states that the wali must be free (harr), sane (aqil), adult (baligh), and Muslim. List them in order.
2
Understand the rationale for slaves, minors, and insane
The text argues that these individuals have no guardianship over themselves, so transitive guardianship is impossible. Analyze the qiyas: transitive guardianship is a branch of intransitive.
This is a qiyas jali (clear analogy) based on the principle that one who cannot manage his own affairs cannot manage another's.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof for Islam
The verse 4:141 is cited: 'And Allah will never make for the disbelievers over the believers a way.' Explain how this denies non-Muslim authority over Muslims, including marriage guardianship.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalala, providing definitive proof.
4
Note the madhhab context
This is the Hanafi position; other schools like Shafi'i allow a non-Muslim guardian for a non-Muslim ward but agree on the other conditions.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih for the Islam condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : بشرط ; يعني يشترط في الولي أن يكون حراً عاقلاً بالغاً مسلماً، فلا ولاية لعبد ولا صغير ولا مجنون ؛ لأنه لا ولاية لهم على أنفسهم، فأولى أن لا يكون على غيرهم ؛ إذ الولاية المتعدية فرع الولاية القاصرة ؛ ولأنَّ هذه ولايةٌ نظرية، ولا نظر في التفويض إلى هؤلاء. وأما الكافر فلا ولاية له على مسلم ومسلمة ؛ لقوله : وَلَن يَجْعَلَ الله
His statement: 'with the condition'; meaning it is required in the guardian that he be free, sane, adult, Muslim. So there is no guardianship for a slave, a minor, or an insane person, because they have no guardianship over themselves, so it is even more proper that they not have it over others; since transitive guardianship is a branch of intransitive guardianship; and because this guardianship is a guardianship of consideration, and there is no consideration in delegating to these [people]. As for the disbeliever, he has no guardianship over a Muslim man or woman; due to His saying: 'And Allah will never make for the disbelievers over the believers a way.'
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the conditions required for a wali (guardian) to be valid in marriage according to the Hanafi school. It begins by explaining the condition 'bishart' (with the condition) mentioned in the matn, meaning that the wali must possess four attributes: freedom (hurriyah), sanity ('aql), adulthood (bulugh), and Islam. The reasoning provided is that those who lack these attributes (slave, minor, insane person) have no guardianship over themselves, so they cannot have guardianship over others; transitive guardianship is derivative of intransitive guardianship. Additionally, this is a guardianship of consideration (wilayah nazariyyah), meaning the guardian must have sound judgment, which these individuals lack. Then it addresses the disbeliever: a non-Muslim cannot be a guardian for a Muslim, citing the Quranic verse 'And Allah will never make for the disbelievers over the believers a way' (Surah Al-Nisa 4:141). This establishes that a Muslim cannot have a non-Muslim guardian, even if the non-Muslim is a relative. These conditions are fundamental to the validity of the marriage contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): The guardian authorized to contract marriage on behalf of a woman. Must meet specific conditions.
حر (harr): Free person, not a slave.
تكليف (taklif): Legal responsibility; a person is mukallaf when he is sane and has reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who cannot be a wali for a Muslim woman?
A) A free adult Muslim male.
B) A sane adult Muslim male.
C) A non-Muslim male relative.
D) A paternal uncle who is free and adult.

Key Takeaway
A valid marriage guardian must be free, sane, adult, and Muslim; any deviation invalidates the wali's role.

Nikah — Marriage

Conditions and Agnatic Order of Guardianship in Marriage
Ruling with proof on conditions and order of agnate guardians

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid guardian (wali) in Hanafi marriage law and list the order of agnate guardians with preference rules.

Core Idea
Guardianship requires freedom, sanity, adulthood, and Islam; agnates are ranked by closeness and strength of lineage.

This passage from the chapter on guardianship in marriage establishes the essential qualifications of a wali and the hierarchical order of male agnates. Understanding these rules is crucial for determining who has the authority to contract a marriage when the bride lacks a direct male guardian. The text also provides a proof for preferring full siblings over half-siblings in the guardianship order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of a valid guardian
Read the opening lines and commentary: the wali must be free, sane, adult, and Muslim. Note the exception for a non-Muslim child whose guardian may be non-Muslim.
These conditions are derived from the principle that transitive guardianship depends on intransitive guardianship.
2
List the agnate guardians in order of proximity
Trace the order: paternal grandfather even if high, then brother, then brother's sons even if low, then uncle, then uncle's sons, then great-uncle, etc. The sequence continues with 'the nearest then the nearest'.
The passage uses 'part of the deceased' etc. to denote categories.
3
Understand preference by closeness of degree
When guardians are from different degrees (e.g., brother vs. uncle), the one closer in lineage takes precedence. This is the default rule.
Closeness is measured by how many steps from the deceased.
4
Apply preference by strength of kinship when degrees are equal
If two guardians are equally close (e.g., full brother and half-brother), the one with stronger kinship (full brother) is preferred. The text supports this with a hadith from Ali (r.a.) regarding inheritance.
This is a key Hanafi ruling: full sibling (a.ayni) over half-sibling (allati).

Book of Marriage, Chapter of Guardian and Compatibility. With condition of freedom and legal capacity. Then the [paternal] grandfather even if high, then the near part of origin like the brother, then his sons even if low, then the distant part of origin like the uncle, then his sons even if low, then the uncle of the father then his sons, then the uncle of the grandfather, then his sons, the nearest then the nearest, then preference by strength of kinship: meaning the full brother is given precedence over the half-brother (from father). (With condition) of freedom and legal capacity. 1) Part of the deceased (2) and his origin (2) and part of his father (4) and part of his grandfather, the nearest then the nearest they are preferred due to closeness of degree: I mean by their children in inheritance: Part of the deceased: meaning the sons, then their sons even if low, then his origin meaning the father, then the grandfather meaning father of the father even if high, then part of his father: meaning the brothers then their sons even if low, then part of his grandfather: meaning the paternal uncles, then their sons even if low, then they are preferred by strength of kinship. [1] His statement: then preference; meaning after preference due to closeness of degree, preference by strength of kinship is considered when degrees are equal, I mean that the one with two kinship ties is more entitled than the one with one kinship tie. Thus the full brother (al-Ayni), who is from father and mother, is preferred over the half-brother (al-Allati), who is from father only from another woman—and al-Allah (with fatha on the 'ayn and tashdid on the lam) means co-wife—as in the chapter of inheritance, as indicated by the hadith of Ali: 'The Messenger of Allah ruled that the full brothers of the mother inherit from each other to the exclusion of half-brothers [from the father]; a man inherits from his full brother to the exclusion of his half-brothers.' Recorded by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi. (1) [2] His statement: with condition; means it is required in the guardian that he be free, sane, adult, Muslim. So there is no guardianship for a slave, a minor, or an insane person, because they have no guardianship over themselves, so it is more fitting that they not have it over others, since transitive guardianship is a branch of intransitive guardianship, and because this guardianship is one of consideration, and there is no consideration in entrusting such [responsibilities] to these [persons]. As for the disbeliever, he has no guardianship over a Muslim man or woman, due to His statement: 'And Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers.' (2) Hence his testimony is not accepted against a Muslim, and inheritance does not occur between them. Thus in al-Binaya. (3) [1] His statement: 'in a Muslim child' is connected to the condition of Islam, meaning Islam is only required in the guardian for the marriage of a Muslim child. As for a disbelieving child, his disbelieving guardian has the authority of his marriage, due to His exalted statement: 'And those who disbelieve are allies of one another.' (4) [2] His statement: 'then the mother'; means when there are no agnates, the authority of marriage goes to the mother, then to the paternal grandmother, and in some books the opposite, then to the daughter, then to the son's daughter, then to the daughter's daughter, then to the son's son's daughter, then to the daughter's son's daughter, and so on, then to the irregular grandfather, then to the full sister, then to the consanguine sister, then to the uterine siblings, then to the uterine relatives: the paternal aunts, then the maternal uncles, then the maternal aunts, then the daughters of paternal uncles, and in this order their children, then the mawla al-muwalat, thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses. (0) (3) His statement: 'then mawla al-muwalat'; said in al-Sharifiyya: The form of mawla al-muwalat is a person of unknown lineage who says to another: 'You are my mawla, you will inherit from me if I die and pay my blood-money if I commit a crime,' and the other says: 'I accept.' According to us, this contract is valid, and the acceptor becomes an heir and a blood-money payer. If the other is also of unknown lineage, and the first says the same and he accepts, then each inherits from the other and pays blood-money. [4] His statement: 'then the judge'; his authority comes after the authority of the sultan, and the authority of the sultan comes after [the authority of previous guardians]. (1) Dhu al-rahim: every relative who is not an agnate. See: Kamal al-Diraya. (2) An-Nisa: from verse 141. (3) Al-Binaya (4): 143. (4) Al-Anfal: from verse 73. (5) Radd al-Muhtar and al-Durr al-Mukhtar (3: 78-79).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, from the chapter of marriage in the Hanafi madhhab, detailing the conditions and order of guardians (awliya') for contracting a marriage. The text begins by stating that the guardian must be free (not a slave) and legally capable (mukallaf, i.e., sane and adult). It then lists the order of guardians based on lineage: first the paternal grandfather even if high, then near male relatives like brothers, then their sons, then distant relatives like uncles, then their sons, and so on, with preference given to the nearest in degree. When degrees are equal, preference is given to the one with stronger kinship, meaning a full brother (from both parents) is preferred over a half-brother (from father only). This is supported by a hadith from Ali (r.a.) recorded by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi, which states that full brothers inherit to the exclusion of half-brothers, applied here analogically to guardianship. The commentary then explains the condition of freedom, sanity, adulthood, and Islam for the guardian. A slave, minor, or insane person cannot be a guardian because they lack authority over themselves. A non-Muslim cannot be a guardian over a Muslim, based on the Quranic verse 'And Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers' (4:141). However, for a non-Muslim child, their non-Muslim relative can be guardian, as the Quran says 'those who disbelieve are allies of one another' (8:73). After the agnates, the order of guardians includes the mother, then the grandmother, then female relatives like daughters, granddaughters, etc., then the 'irregular grandfather' (al-jadd al-fasid), then sisters, then uterine siblings, then dhu al-rahim (non-agnate relatives) like paternal aunts, maternal uncles, etc. Finally, the mawla al-muwalat (a person of unknown lineage who contracts mutual inheritance and blood-money liability) and the judge (qadi) are listed as the last resort guardians. The passage thus provides a comprehensive ranking of who has the right to give a woman in marriage when she lacks an agnate guardian.

Key Terms
العيني (al-‘Aynī): The full brother from both father and mother.
العلاتي (al-‘Allātī): The half-brother from father only (from a different co-wife).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a condition for a valid guardian (wali) in marriage?
A) The wali must be wealthy.
B) The wali must be a Muslim if the bride is Muslim.
C) The wali must be male.
D) The wali must be a scholar.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires a wali who is free, sane, adult, and Muslim; agnate guardians are ranked by closeness and strength of kinship, with full siblings preferred over half-siblings.

Guardianship Beyond Agnates: Mother, Female Relatives, and Legal Guardians in Hanafi Law
Ruling with proof on guardianship order beyond agnates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the order of guardians beyond agnates, including mother, grandmother, sisters, dhu al-rahim, mawla al-muwalat, and the judge, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding mother vs. grandmother.

Core Idea
When no agnate exists, guardianship passes to the mother, then to female relatives, then to dhu al-rahim, then to mawla al-muwalat, and finally to the judge.

This passage continues the ranking of guardians in marriage after the agnate (asabah) relatives have been exhausted. It introduces a sequence that includes the mother, grandmother, daughters, granddaughters, sisters, and non-agnate relatives (dhu al-rahim), as well as the unique Hanafi institution of mawla al-muwalat (a pseudo-relative by contract). Finally, the judge (qadi) serves as the last resort. Understanding this order is essential for determining who may contract a woman's marriage when she has no supportive male agnate.

Guided Path
1
Identify when the order goes beyond agnates
The text begins 'When there are no agnates' (inda adam al-asabat). This triggers the next level of guardians: the mother, then grandmother, etc.
Agnates are male relatives like father, brother, uncle; when none exist, females and non-agnates can act.
2
List the order: mother, grandmother, and female descendants
First the mother, then the paternal grandmother (with ikhtilaf on order), then the daughter, then the son's daughter, etc. This sequence prioritizes upward female lineage then downward female lineage.
Some books reverse mother and grandmother; the mufta bihi is mother first.
3
Include sisters and uterine siblings
After the female descendants, the text lists: the irregular grandfather (jadd fasid), then the full sister, then the consanguine sister, then the uterine siblings (awlad al-umm).
Irregular grandfather is a father's father who is not a direct agnate? Actually, it's a special category.
4
Understand dhu al-rahim and mawla al-muwalat
Dhu al-rahim are non-agnate blood relatives (maternal aunts, uncles, etc.). The text mentions paternal aunts, maternal uncles, and others. Mawla al-muwalat is a contractual relationship for unknown lineage, valid only in Hanafi law.
Finally, the judge (qadi) is the ultimate authority, preceded by the sultan.

[٤] قوله : ثم قاض ؛ ولايته متأخّرة عن ولاية السلطان، وولاية السلطان متأخّرة
(۱) ذو الرحم : كل قريب ليس بعصبة. ينظر : كمال الدراية (ق۲۲۷).
(۲) النساء : من الآية ١٤١ .

(۵) رد المحتار» و «الدر المختار» (۳ : ۷۸ - ۷۹).
Book of Marriage, Chapter of Guardian and Compatibility. With condition of freedom and legal capacity. Then the [paternal] grandfather even if high, then the near part of origin like the brother, then his sons even if low, then the distant part of origin like the uncle, then his sons even if low, then the uncle of the father then his sons, then the uncle of the grandfather, then his sons, the nearest then the nearest, then preference by strength of kinship: meaning the full brother is given precedence over the half-brother (from father). (With condition) of freedom and legal capacity. 1) Part of the deceased (2) and his origin (2) and part of his father (4) and part of his grandfather, the nearest then the nearest they are preferred due to closeness of degree: I mean by their children in inheritance: Part of the deceased: meaning the sons, then their sons even if low, then his origin meaning the father, then the grandfather meaning father of the father even if high, then part of his father: meaning the brothers then their sons even if low, then part of his grandfather: meaning the paternal uncles, then their sons even if low, then they are preferred by strength of kinship. [1] His statement: then preference; meaning after preference due to closeness of degree, preference by strength of kinship is considered when degrees are equal, I mean that the one with two kinship ties is more entitled than the one with one kinship tie. Thus the full brother (al-Ayni), who is from father and mother, is preferred over the half-brother (al-Allati), who is from father only from another woman—and al-Allah (with fatha on the 'ayn and tashdid on the lam) means co-wife—as in the chapter of inheritance, as indicated by the hadith of Ali: 'The Messenger of Allah ruled that the full brothers of the mother inherit from each other to the exclusion of half-brothers [from the father]; a man inherits from his full brother to the exclusion of his half-brothers.' Recorded by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi. (1) [2] His statement: with condition; means it is required in the guardian that he be free, sane, adult, Muslim. So there is no guardianship for a slave, a minor, or an insane person, because they have no guardianship over themselves, so it is more fitting that they not have it over others, since transitive guardianship is a branch of intransitive guardianship, and because this guardianship is one of consideration, and there is no consideration in entrusting such [responsibilities] to these [persons]. As for the disbeliever, he has no guardianship over a Muslim man or woman, due to His statement: 'And Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers.' (2) Hence his testimony is not accepted against a Muslim, and inheritance does not occur between them. Thus in al-Binaya. (3) [1] His statement: 'in a Muslim child' is connected to the condition of Islam, meaning Islam is only required in the guardian for the marriage of a Muslim child. As for a disbelieving child, his disbelieving guardian has the authority of his marriage, due to His exalted statement: 'And those who disbelieve are allies of one another.' (4) [2] His statement: 'then the mother'; means when there are no agnates, the authority of marriage goes to the mother, then to the paternal grandmother, and in some books the opposite, then to the daughter, then to the son's daughter, then to the daughter's daughter, then to the son's son's daughter, then to the daughter's son's daughter, and so on, then to the irregular grandfather, then to the full sister, then to the consanguine sister, then to the uterine siblings, then to the uterine relatives: the paternal aunts, then the maternal uncles, then the maternal aunts, then the daughters of paternal uncles, and in this order their children, then the mawla al-muwalat, thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses. (0) (3) His statement: 'then mawla al-muwalat'; said in al-Sharifiyya: The form of mawla al-muwalat is a person of unknown lineage who says to another: 'You are my mawla, you will inherit from me if I die and pay my blood-money if I commit a crime,' and the other says: 'I accept.' According to us, this contract is valid, and the acceptor becomes an heir and a blood-money payer. If the other is also of unknown lineage, and the first says the same and he accepts, then each inherits from the other and pays blood-money. [4] His statement: 'then the judge'; his authority comes after the authority of the sultan, and the authority of the sultan comes after [the authority of previous guardians]. (1) Dhu al-rahim: every relative who is not an agnate. See: Kamal al-Diraya. (2) An-Nisa: from verse 141. (3) Al-Binaya (4): 143. (4) Al-Anfal: from verse 73. (5) Radd al-Muhtar and al-Durr al-Mukhtar (3: 78-79).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, from the chapter of marriage in the Hanafi madhhab, detailing the conditions and order of guardians (awliya') for contracting a marriage. The text begins by stating that the guardian must be free (not a slave) and legally capable (mukallaf, i.e., sane and adult). It then lists the order of guardians based on lineage: first the paternal grandfather even if high, then near male relatives like brothers, then their sons, then distant relatives like uncles, then their sons, and so on, with preference given to the nearest in degree. When degrees are equal, preference is given to the one with stronger kinship, meaning a full brother (from both parents) is preferred over a half-brother (from father only). This is supported by a hadith from Ali (r.a.) recorded by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi, which states that full brothers inherit to the exclusion of half-brothers, applied here analogically to guardianship. The commentary then explains the condition of freedom, sanity, adulthood, and Islam for the guardian. A slave, minor, or insane person cannot be a guardian because they lack authority over themselves. A non-Muslim cannot be a guardian over a Muslim, based on the Quranic verse 'And Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers' (4:141). However, for a non-Muslim child, their non-Muslim relative can be guardian, as the Quran says 'those who disbelieve are allies of one another' (8:73). After the agnates, the order of guardians includes the mother, then the grandmother, then female relatives like daughters, granddaughters, etc., then the 'irregular grandfather' (al-jadd al-fasid), then sisters, then uterine siblings, then dhu al-rahim (non-agnate relatives) like paternal aunts, maternal uncles, etc. Finally, the mawla al-muwalat (a person of unknown lineage who contracts mutual inheritance and blood-money liability) and the judge (qadi) are listed as the last resort guardians. The passage thus provides a comprehensive ranking of who has the right to give a woman in marriage when she lacks an agnate guardian.

Key Terms
مولى الموالاة (Mawlā al-Muwālāt): A person of unknown lineage who makes a contract of mutual inheritance and blood-money liability with another.
ذو الرحم (Dhū ar-Raḥim): Any relative who is not an agnate ('āṣib), such as maternal aunts and uncles.
الجد الفاسد (al-Jadd al-Fāsid): The 'irregular grandfather', a grandfather who is not a direct agnate, such as mother's father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi order of guardians beyond agnates, who comes immediately after the mother?
A) The daughter
B) The paternal grandmother
C) The full sister
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
When no agnate guardian exists, Hanafi law provides a comprehensive order starting with the mother, then grandmother, female descendants, sisters, dhu al-rahim, mawla al-muwalat, and finally the judge, ensuring that every Muslim woman has a guardian for marriage.

Condition of Islam for the Guardian in Nikah
Ruling that guardian must be Muslim if child is Muslim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of Islam for the guardian when marrying a Muslim child and apply the Quranic proof to similar cases.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim guardian cannot marry a Muslim child; Islam is a binding condition for guardianship across religions.

Guardianship in marriage (wilayah al-nikah) is a critical concept in Islamic family law. While the Hanafi school grants the guardian authority over dependents, it conditions that authority on religious compatibility. This lesson examines a key ruling from the Hanafi tradition: when the child being married is Muslim, the guardian must also be Muslim. We explore the evidence from the Quran and the rationale behind this condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is the marriage (nikah) of a Muslim child by a guardian. The text establishes that the guardian's Islam is a condition (shart) for validity.
2
Understand the exception
The text states that if the child is a disbeliever, the condition does not apply; a non-Muslim guardian can marry a non-Muslim child.
The exception confirms the rule by contrast.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is Quran 8:73: 'And those who disbelieve are allies of one another.' This establishes mutual guardianship among disbelievers.
The verse is used to imply that non-Muslims cannot be guardians over Muslims.
4
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
By mafhum al-mukhalafah (converse meaning), the verse implies that non-Muslims only have authority over non-Muslims, not over Muslims.
This reasoning is central to the Hanafi position.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dimension
The Shafi'i school may allow a non-Muslim guardian for a Muslim child in certain interpretations. Compare and understand the practical difference.
The lesson will explore this in the dispute map.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإسلام في ولد مسلم دون كافر. قوله : في ولد مسلم؛ متعلّق بقيد الإسلام يعني إنما يشترط الإسلام في الولي في إنكاح ولد مسلم، وأما الولد الكافر فلوليه الكافر ولاية إنكاحه بقوله تعالى: وَالَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلِيَاءُ بَعْضٍ.
And Islam [is a condition] in the case of a Muslim child, not a disbeliever. He said: 'In a Muslim child' is connected to the restriction of Islam, meaning that Islam is required in the guardian for the marriage of a Muslim child. As for a disbelieving child, his disbelieving guardian has authority to marry him by the statement of the Exalted: 'And those who disbelieve are allies of one another.'
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the condition of Islam in the guardian for marriage. The base text states that Islam is a condition when the child being married is Muslim, but not if the child is a disbeliever. The commentator explains that the phrase 'in a Muslim child' qualifies the requirement of Islam: the guardian must be Muslim only if the child is Muslim. For a disbelieving child, the guardian's qualification follows the child's religion, based on the Quranic verse from Surah Al-Anfal (8:73) that non-Muslims are allies of each other. This verse establishes that non-Muslims have guardianship over their own, so a non-Muslim father or other relative can validly marry a non-Muslim child. The ruling is clear: the guardian's Islam is a condition for the marriage of a Muslim child, not for a non-Muslim child.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): The guardian with authority to contract marriage on behalf of a minor or dependent.
إسلام (islam): The religion of being a Muslim; for a guardian, it means being a confessor of Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The guardian must be a relative.
B) The guardian must be Muslim.
C) The guardian must be male.
D) The guardian must be the father.

Key Takeaway
A non-Muslim cannot be a guardian for a Muslim child's marriage; Islam is a necessary condition for valid guardianship across religious lines.

Hierarchy of Female Guardians After the Father
Order of female guardians after agnatic males

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the complete Hanafi hierarchy of female guardians for marriage after the agnatic male guardians, and determine who takes precedence in a given scenario.

Core Idea
When no male agnate exists, guardianship passes to the mother, then to a specific order of female relatives and distant kin.

The Hanafi school provides a detailed list of who may give a woman in marriage when her father, grandfather, brother, uncle, or other agnatic male guardians are absent. This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah outlines the order starting from the mother, then ascending through grandmothers, descending to daughters, then to sisters, uterine relatives, and finally to the ally by clientage. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correctly applying marriage contracts in the absence of male guardians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Starting Point
The hierarchy applies only when there are no agnatic male guardians (ʿaṣabāt). If any agnatic male exists, he takes precedence over all listed here.
2
Memorize the First Tier
The first female guardian is the mother. Then the maternal grandmother (mother's mother), though some books reverse this order. Then daughters, then granddaughters (daughter's daughter, son's son's daughter, etc.).
The order among daughters and granddaughters is based on proximity: the nearer in lineage comes first.
3
Continue Through the Remaining Tiers
After the grandchildren, the defective grandfather (al-jadd al-fāsid) – a grandfather who is not a true agnatic heir – comes next. Then full sister, then paternal half-sister, then children of the mother (uterine siblings). Then other uterine relatives: paternal aunts, maternal uncles, maternal aunts, daughters of paternal uncles. Finally, the ally by clientage (mawlā al-muwālāt).
The order continues with their children in the same sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ثم الأم؛ يعني عند عدم العصبات ولاية التزويج إلى الأم، ثم أم الأب، وفي بعض الكتب عكسه، ثم للبنت، ثم لبنت الابن، ثم لبنت البنت، ثم لبنت ابن الابن، ثم لبنت بنت البنت، وهكذا، ثم للجد الفاسد، ثم للأخت لأب وأم، ثمّ لأب، ثم لأولاد الأمّ، ثم لذوي الأرحام العمات ثُمَّ الأخوال، ثم الخالات، ثم بنات الأعمام، وبهذا الترتيب أولادهم، ثم مولى الموالاة.
He said: 'Then the mother' means that when there are no agnatic male guardians, the right of marriage goes to the mother, then to the mother's mother (maternal grandmother), and in some books the opposite [order], then to the daughter, then to the daughter's son's daughter, then to the daughter's daughter, then to the son's son's daughter, then to the daughter's son's daughter, and so on, then to the defective grandfather (al-jadd al-fasid), then to the full sister, then to the paternal half-sister, then to the children of the mother, then to uterine relatives: paternal aunts, then maternal uncles, then maternal aunts, then daughters of paternal uncles, and in this order their children, then to the ally by clientage (mawla al-muwalāt).
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that establishes the hierarchy of guardians (awlīyaʾ) for marriage after the agnatic male guardians (ʿaṣabāt) are absent. The commentator explains the progression starting from the mother, then ascending through female ascendants (grandmothers), then descending to daughters and granddaughters, then to defective grandfather (a grandfather who is not a true agnatic heir), then to sisters (full, then consanguine), then to uterine siblings, then to other uterine relatives (paternal aunts, maternal uncles, maternal aunts, daughters of paternal uncles), and finally to the ally by clientage. The order is based on proximity: the nearest in relation takes precedence. This is the Hanafi position as recorded in al-Durr al-Mukhtār and its commentaries.

Key Terms
عصبات (ʿaṣabāt): Agnatic male relatives (like father, grandfather, brother, uncle) who have priority in guardianship.
ذو الرحم (dhū al-riḥm): Uterine relatives: those related through females, as opposed to agnates.
الجد الفاسد (al-jadd al-fāsid): A grandfather who is not a true agnatic heir (e.g., maternal grandfather, or a paternal grandfather through a female link).
مولى الموالاة (mawlā al-muwālāt): A person who has a clientage relationship established through a contract of mutual support (muwālāt), used when there are no blood relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After the agnatic guardians are absent, who is the first female guardian according to the Hanafi hierarchy?
A) The paternal grandmother
B) The mother
C) The daughter
D) The sister

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hierarchy of female guardians for marriage is: mother, maternal grandmother, daughter, then further descendants, defective grandfather, sisters, uterine relatives, and finally the ally by clientage.

The Clientage Ally (Mawlā al-Muwālāh) as Guardian
Definition of clientage ally and its validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition and conditions of the mawlā al-muwālāh contract and identify its place in the hierarchy of marriage guardians.

Core Idea
A person of unknown lineage becomes a guardian through a valid clientage contract, inheriting and bearing tort responsibility.

In this lesson, we examine the concept of mawlā al-muwālāh, a guardian by clientage. The passage defines it as a contract between two persons of unknown lineage, making each a legal heir and guardian. We explore its validity, conditions, and why it takes precedence over the sultan and judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Read the text describing mawlā al-muwālāh as a person of unknown lineage who contracts clientage. Note the offer and acceptance.
2
List the conditions
From the text, extract conditions: unknown lineage, offer and acceptance, and mutual intent to inherit and bear torts.
The text also mentions if both are unknown, each inherits from the other.
3
Understand the legal effect
The contract makes the acceptor an inheritor and responsible for torts. This establishes a bond like kinship.
This is a legal fiction to provide a guardian.
4
Place in hierarchy
The text states that mawlā al-muwālāh is the last among heirs, so his guardianship precedes the sultan and judge.
Thus, you must check for any relative or mawlā before resorting to the sultan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ثم مولى الموالاة؛ قال في «الشريفية»: صورة مولى الموالاة شخص مجهول النسب، قال لآخر : أنت مولاي ترثني إذا مت وتعقل عني إذا جنيت، وقال الآخر : قبلت، فعندنا يصح هذا العقد، ويصير القابل وارثاً عاقلاً، وإذا كان الآخرُ أيضاً مجهول النَّسب، وقال الأول مثل ذلك وقبله، فورث كل منهما الآخر وعقل عنه. قوله : ثم قاض؛ ولايته متأخّرة عن ولاية السلطان، وولاية السلطان متأخّرة عن ولاية مولى الموالاة؛ لأن مولى الموالاة آخر الورثة، فيقدَّمُ على الأجنبي، فعند عدم جميع الورثة الولاية إلى السلطان؛ لحديث: السلطان ولي من لا ولي له، ثم إلى القاضي لقيامه مقام السلطان. قوله : في منشوره؛ الجملة صفة لقاض، والمنشور ما كَتَبَ السلطانُ فيه إني جعلتُ فلاناً قاضياً لبلدة كذا سُمّي به؛ لأن القاضي ينشده وقت قراءته على الناس، وكذا يشترط في حق نواب القاضي حصول الإذن لهم بتزويج الصغار. قوله : والأبعد يزوج بغيبة الأقرب؛ ذكر في «الهداية» و«البناية»: إذا غاب الولي الأقرب غيبة منقطعة جازَ لِمَن هو أبعد منه كالجد أن يزوجه؛ لأنَّ هذه ولاية نظرية، وليس من النظر التفويض إلى من لا ينتفع برأيه، وهو الولى الأقرب حال غيبته؛ لتعذر الانتفاع به، فالتحق بمن لا ولي له كما إذا مات الأقرب. قوله : بغيبة الأقرب؛ وأما لو تزوج الأبعد مع حضور الأقرب، فهو متوقف على إجازته. قوله : ما لم ينتظر؛ ما : موصولة، والكفؤ فاعل لينتظر، وما بعده صفته، والخبر مفعول، والحد الثاني: هو الذي نسبه الزيلعي إلى أكثر المشايخ.
He said: 'Then the ally by clientage (mawlā al-muwālāh)'. Al-Sharīfiyyah said: The form of mawlā al-muwālāh is a person of unknown lineage who says to another: 'You are my patron; inherit from me when I die and bear responsibility for my torts when I commit an offense.' The other says 'I accept'. According to us this contract is valid, and the acceptor becomes an inheritor and responsible for torts. If the other is also of unknown lineage and the first says the same and he accepts, then each inherits from the other and bears responsibility for his torts. He said: 'Then the judge (qāḍī)' – his authority is after the authority of the sultan, and the sultan's authority is after the authority of the mawlā al-muwālāh, because mawlā al-muwālāh is the last of the heirs, so he is preferred over a stranger. When all heirs are absent, authority goes to the sultan due to the hadith: 'The sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian', then to the judge because he stands in place of the sultan. His statement: 'in his writ (manshūrihī)' – the phrase describes the judge, and the manshūr is what the sultan writes: 'I have appointed so-and-so as judge over such-and-such a town' – it is called that because the judge recites it at the time of reading it to the people. Likewise, it is required for the judge's deputies that they obtain permission to marry minors. His statement: 'And the more distant marries in the absence of the nearer' – mentioned in al-Hidāyah and al-Bināyah: When the nearer guardian is absent with a disconnecting absence, it is permissible for one more distant, like the grandfather, to marry her, because this is a guardianship of consideration (wilāyah nazariyyah), and it is not considered prudent to delegate to one whose opinion cannot be benefited from, i.e., the nearer guardian during his absence, because benefiting from him is impossible; so he becomes like one who has no guardian, as if the nearer died. His statement: 'in the absence of the nearer' – but if the more distant marries while the nearer is present, it is dependent on his ratification. His statement: 'as long as he does not wait' – 'mā' is a relative pronoun, 'al-kufu'' is the subject of 'yantaẓir', and what follows is its description, and 'al-khabar' is the object. The second definition (al-ḥadd al-thānī) is what al-Zaylaʿī attributed to most of the mashāyikh.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling concerning two types of guardians: the ally by clientage (mawlā al-muwālāh) and the judge (qāḍī). It begins by defining mawlā al-muwālāh: a person of unknown lineage who contracts a mutual clientage agreement with another, whereby they inherit from each other and bear responsibility for each other's torts. This contract is valid in the Hanafi school, and the person becomes a legal heir. The text then explains the hierarchy: the judge's authority comes after the sultan's, and the sultan's after the mawlā al-muwālāh, because the mawlā al-muwālāh is the last among heirs. When no relative exists, the sultan is the guardian, based on the well-known hadith 'The sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian,' and the judge acts as his deputy. The text further discusses the condition of the judge's writ (manshūr): the sultan's written appointment is necessary for the judge to have authority to marry minors, and similarly for his deputies. Then it addresses the situation when the nearer guardian is absent: if his absence is such that contact is impossible (ghaybah munqaṭiʿah), a more distant guardian (e.g., grandfather) may marry the ward without waiting, because the purpose of guardianship is benefit, and the nearer's absence prevents benefit. However, if the more distant marries while the nearer is present, the marriage is suspended on the nearer's ratification. Finally, it clarifies that the phrase 'as long as he does not wait' refers to the more distant not being required to wait for news from the absent nearer, as long as a competent suitor (kufu') is present. This ruling is attributed to most mashāyikh.

Key Terms
مولى الموالاة (mawlā al-muwālāh): A person of unknown lineage who contracts a clientage agreement with another to inherit and bear tort responsibility.
منشور (manshūr): The writ or official document from the sultan appointing a judge, granting him authority to perform marriages, etc.
غيبة منقطعة (ghaybah munqaṭiʿah): A disconnecting absence where the guardian is out of reach and cannot be consulted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for the validity of a mawlā al-muwālāh contract?
A) The parties must be of the same tribe.
B) Both parties must be of unknown lineage.
C) The contract must be witnessed by the judge.
D) The contract must be for a fixed term.

Key Takeaway
The mawlā al-muwālāh contract creates a legal guardian for persons of unknown lineage, taking precedence over the sultan and judge.

Guardianship of the Judge and Absent Guardian Rules
Rulings on judge's guardianship and absence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the hierarchy of marriage guardians, the requirement of the judge's writ, and the rule of ghaybah munqaṭiʿah.

Core Idea
The judge acts only after all relatives and clientage allies; a disconnected absence shifts guardianship to the next in line.

This lesson covers the judge's authority as deputy of the sultan, the need for a written appointment (manshūr), and the rule that a more distant guardian may marry when the nearer is unreachable. The hadith 'The sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian' forms the basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy
The text states: judge's authority is after the sultan, sultan's after mawlā al-muwālāh. So the order is: blood relatives, mawlā, sultan, judge.
Memorize this order.
2
Understand the hadith evidence
The Prophet said: 'Al-sulṭānu waliyyu man lā waliyya lahu'. This grants the sultan guardianship, which he delegates to the judge.
This hadith is the primary textual evidence.
3
Examine the requirement of the writ
The judge's authority to marry minors is conditional upon his appointment writ (manshūr). Without it, his marriages are not valid. Deputies also need explicit permission.
The writ ensures authority is documented.
4
Apply the rule of ghaybah munqaṭiʿah
When the nearest guardian is absent in a way that contact is impossible, the next guardian may marry without waiting. This is based on the principle of wilāyah nazariyyah (guardianship of consideration).
If contact is possible, he must wait.
5
Note the condition of waiting
The phrase 'as long as he does not wait' means the distant guardian need not wait for a competent suitor; he may proceed immediately if a suitable match exists.
The ruling is attributed to most mashāyikh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ثم مولى الموالاة؛ قال في «الشريفية»: صورة مولى الموالاة شخص مجهول النسب، قال لآخر : أنت مولاي ترثني إذا مت وتعقل عني إذا جنيت، وقال الآخر : قبلت، فعندنا يصح هذا العقد، ويصير القابل وارثاً عاقلاً، وإذا كان الآخرُ أيضاً مجهول النَّسب، وقال الأول مثل ذلك وقبله، فورث كل منهما الآخر وعقل عنه. قوله : ثم قاض؛ ولايته متأخّرة عن ولاية السلطان، وولاية السلطان متأخّرة عن ولاية مولى الموالاة؛ لأن مولى الموالاة آخر الورثة، فيقدَّمُ على الأجنبي، فعند عدم جميع الورثة الولاية إلى السلطان؛ لحديث: السلطان ولي من لا ولي له، ثم إلى القاضي لقيامه مقام السلطان. قوله : في منشوره؛ الجملة صفة لقاض، والمنشور ما كَتَبَ السلطانُ فيه إني جعلتُ فلاناً قاضياً لبلدة كذا سُمّي به؛ لأن القاضي ينشده وقت قراءته على الناس، وكذا يشترط في حق نواب القاضي حصول الإذن لهم بتزويج الصغار. قوله : والأبعد يزوج بغيبة الأقرب؛ ذكر في «الهداية» و«البناية»: إذا غاب الولي الأقرب غيبة منقطعة جازَ لِمَن هو أبعد منه كالجد أن يزوجه؛ لأنَّ هذه ولاية نظرية، وليس من النظر التفويض إلى من لا ينتفع برأيه، وهو الولى الأقرب حال غيبته؛ لتعذر الانتفاع به، فالتحق بمن لا ولي له كما إذا مات الأقرب. قوله : بغيبة الأقرب؛ وأما لو تزوج الأبعد مع حضور الأقرب، فهو متوقف على إجازته. قوله : ما لم ينتظر؛ ما : موصولة، والكفؤ فاعل لينتظر، وما بعده صفته، والخبر مفعول، والحد الثاني: هو الذي نسبه الزيلعي إلى أكثر المشايخ.
He said: 'Then the ally by clientage (mawlā al-muwālāh)'. Al-Sharīfiyyah said: The form of mawlā al-muwālāh is a person of unknown lineage who says to another: 'You are my patron; inherit from me when I die and bear responsibility for my torts when I commit an offense.' The other says 'I accept'. According to us this contract is valid, and the acceptor becomes an inheritor and responsible for torts. If the other is also of unknown lineage and the first says the same and he accepts, then each inherits from the other and bears responsibility for his torts. He said: 'Then the judge (qāḍī)' – his authority is after the authority of the sultan, and the sultan's authority is after the authority of the mawlā al-muwālāh, because mawlā al-muwālāh is the last of the heirs, so he is preferred over a stranger. When all heirs are absent, authority goes to the sultan due to the hadith: 'The sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian', then to the judge because he stands in place of the sultan. His statement: 'in his writ (manshūrihī)' – the phrase describes the judge, and the manshūr is what the sultan writes: 'I have appointed so-and-so as judge over such-and-such a town' – it is called that because the judge recites it at the time of reading it to the people. Likewise, it is required for the judge's deputies that they obtain permission to marry minors. His statement: 'And the more distant marries in the absence of the nearer' – mentioned in al-Hidāyah and al-Bināyah: When the nearer guardian is absent with a disconnecting absence, it is permissible for one more distant, like the grandfather, to marry her, because this is a guardianship of consideration (wilāyah nazariyyah), and it is not considered prudent to delegate to one whose opinion cannot be benefited from, i.e., the nearer guardian during his absence, because benefiting from him is impossible; so he becomes like one who has no guardian, as if the nearer died. His statement: 'in the absence of the nearer' – but if the more distant marries while the nearer is present, it is dependent on his ratification. His statement: 'as long as he does not wait' – 'mā' is a relative pronoun, 'al-kufu'' is the subject of 'yantaẓir', and what follows is its description, and 'al-khabar' is the object. The second definition (al-ḥadd al-thānī) is what al-Zaylaʿī attributed to most of the mashāyikh.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling concerning two types of guardians: the ally by clientage (mawlā al-muwālāh) and the judge (qāḍī). It begins by defining mawlā al-muwālāh: a person of unknown lineage who contracts a mutual clientage agreement with another, whereby they inherit from each other and bear responsibility for each other's torts. This contract is valid in the Hanafi school, and the person becomes a legal heir. The text then explains the hierarchy: the judge's authority comes after the sultan's, and the sultan's after the mawlā al-muwālāh, because the mawlā al-muwālāh is the last among heirs. When no relative exists, the sultan is the guardian, based on the well-known hadith 'The sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian,' and the judge acts as his deputy. The text further discusses the condition of the judge's writ (manshūr): the sultan's written appointment is necessary for the judge to have authority to marry minors, and similarly for his deputies. Then it addresses the situation when the nearer guardian is absent: if his absence is such that contact is impossible (ghaybah munqaṭiʿah), a more distant guardian (e.g., grandfather) may marry the ward without waiting, because the purpose of guardianship is benefit, and the nearer's absence prevents benefit. However, if the more distant marries while the nearer is present, the marriage is suspended on the nearer's ratification. Finally, it clarifies that the phrase 'as long as he does not wait' refers to the more distant not being required to wait for news from the absent nearer, as long as a competent suitor (kufu') is present. This ruling is attributed to most mashāyikh.

Key Terms
مولى الموالاة (mawlā al-muwālāh): A person of unknown lineage who contracts a clientage agreement with another to inherit and bear tort responsibility.
منشور (manshūr): The writ or official document from the sultan appointing a judge, granting him authority to perform marriages, etc.
غيبة منقطعة (ghaybah munqaṭiʿah): A disconnecting absence where the guardian is out of reach and cannot be consulted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the more distant guardian to marry in the absence of the nearer?
A) The nearer must be dead.
B) The nearer must be absent with a disconnecting absence.
C) The distant must obtain the judge's permission.
D) The ward must be an orphan.

Key Takeaway
The judge's guardianship is conditional; a disconnected absence of a nearer guardian allows the next in line to act, following the hierarchy.

Nikah — Marriage
