The Distant Guardian's Authority and Disconnected Absence
Ruling with commentary on the condition of disconnected absence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of disconnected absence for the distant guardian's marriage authority and distinguish the three Hanafi definitions of ghaybah munqati'ah.

Core Idea
The distant guardian may marry the ward only when the nearest guardian is absent with disconnected absence — defined by three intra-Hanafi views.

In this passage from 'Imad al-Ri'ayah's commentary on al-Wiqayah, we examine the crucial condition that empowers the distant guardian: the disconnected absence (ghaybah munqati'ah) of the nearest guardian. The Hanafi jurists differed on what exactly constitutes such absence, giving rise to three definitions with different practical consequences. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the principle that guardianship is based on consideration (wilayah nazariyyah) and the ward's best interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its core condition
The distant guardian (al-ab'ad, e.g., grandfather) may contract the marriage only when the nearest guardian (al-aqrab, e.g., father) is absent with disconnected absence. Locate the phrase 'بغيبة الأقرب غيبة منقطعة' in the text and note that this condition is essential.
If the nearest is present, the distant's marriage is suspended (mawquf) on his ratification.
2
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The text explains the basis: 'هذه ولاية نظرية' — guardianship is based on consideration. Delegating to an absent guardian whose opinion cannot be obtained is not considerate. Hence, the absent nearest is treated as if he had no guardianship.
This is a qiyas: the ward becomes like one with no wali, analogous to the hadith 'al-sultan wali man la wali lahu'.
3
Extract the three definitions of disconnected absence
The text lists three definitions: (1) the suitor does not await news from the guardian (khabar), (2) the distance of travel that shortens prayer (masafat al-qasr), (3) the guardian is in a place reachable by caravan only once a year. Note the authorities for each.
Identify the proponents mentioned: first view by majority of early mashayikh, second by later scholars, third by al-Quduri and Ibn Salamah.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative fatwa)
The text states that the fatwa is upon the second definition (distance of qasr) according to the later scholars (al-mutakhkhirin), even though the first is considered 'closer to fiqh' by al-Marghinani. Understand the tarjih mechanism.
The practical fruit (thamaratu al-khilaf) appears in the case of someone hiding within the city.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a woman whose father is absent within the same city (hiding or long travel). Would the grandfather be permitted to marry her? Apply each definition and see the different outcomes.
Under definition 1, possibly yes; under definition 2, no (not travel distance); under definition 3, no.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب الولي والكفؤ
في منشوره ذلك ، والأبعد يزوج بغيبة الأقرب ما لم ينتظر الكفؤ الخاطب الخبر منه، وعليه الأكثر ، ومدَّةُ السِّفر عند جمع من المتأخرين
[1] قوله : في منشوره؛ الجملة صفة لقاض ، والمنشور ما كتب السلطان فيه إني جعلتُ فلاناً قاضياً لبلدة كذا سمي به؛ لأن القاضي ينشده وقت قراءته على الناس ، وكذا يشترط في حق نواب القاضي حصول الإذن لهم بتزويج الصغار، وفي الاشتراطين خلاف مبسوط في حواشي الدر المختار.
[2] قوله : والأبعد ... الخ؛ ذكر في الهداية والبناية : إذا غاب الولي الأقرب غيبة منقطعة جاز لمن هو أبعد منه كالجد أن يزوجه؛ لأن هذه ولاية نظرية، وليس من النظر التفويض إلى من لا ينتفع برأيه، وهو الولي الأقرب حال غيبته؛ لتعذر الانتفاع به، فالتحق بمن لا ولي له كما إذا مات الأقرب.
[3] قوله : بغيبة الأقرب؛ وأما لو تزوج الأبعد مع حضور الأقرب، فهو متوقف على إجازته.
[4] قوله : ما لم ينتظر؛ ما: موصولة، والكفؤ فاعل لينتظر، وما بعده صفته، والخبر مفعول، والحد الثاني : هو الذي نسبه الزيلعي إلى أكثر المشايخ، وقال: عليه الفتوى، واختار في الحقائق وغيره والكنز والهداية والاختيار وغيرها : هو عدم انتظار الخاطب، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر فيمن اختفى في المدينة، هل تكون غيبة منقطعة.
اعلم أن للأبعد ولاية التزويج عند غيبة الأقرب غيبة منقطعة، وتفسيرها عند الأكثر ما ذكر، وهو قوله : ما لم ينتظر، أي مدة لم ينتظر الكفؤ الخاطب، ثم عطف على قوله ما لم ينتظر قوله : مدة عند جمع من المتأخرين، وعليه الفتوى.
وقد اختلفوا في حد الغيبة المنقطعة: الأول: ما لم ينتظر الكفؤ الخاطب، واختاره أكثر المشايخ وصاحب المختار والملتقى وصححه شمس الأئمة السرخسي ومحمد بن الفضل، وفي الهداية: إنه أقرب إلى الفقه. وفي الفتح: إنه الأشبه بالفقه، وفي الدر المنقى عن الحقائق: إنه أصح الأقاويل، وفي البحر: الأحسن الافتاء بما عليه أكثر المشايخ. والثاني: مسافة القصر: وعليه أكثر المتأخرين واختارها صاحب الكنز والتنوير، وقال صاحب الكافي والتبيين: وعليه الفتوى. والثالث: أن يكون في بلدة لا تصل إليه القافلة في السنة إلا مرة واحدة، واختاره القدوري وابن سلمة. وذكر غير ذلك، ينظر: التبيين.
The Book of Marriage, Chapter of the Guardian and Compatibility. In his writ [it says] that, and the more distant guardian marries off in the absence of the nearest guardian as long as the suitable suitor does not await news from him, and the majority are upon this, and the duration of travel according to a group of later scholars. [1] His saying: 'in his writ': the clause is an adjective for 'judge', and the writ is what the sultan wrote: 'I have made so-and-so judge of such town', so named because the judge recites it when reading to the people. Likewise, it is required for the judge's deputies that they be permitted to marry off minors, and there is a detailed disagreement in the footnotes of al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [2] His saying: 'and the more distant...etc.' It is mentioned in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah: If the nearest guardian is absent with disconnected absence, it is permissible for the more distant one like the grandfather to marry off, because this is a guardianship based on consideration, and it is not from consideration to delegate to one whose opinion is unbeneficial, namely the nearest guardian during his absence, due to the impossibility of benefiting from him, so he is considered like one who has no guardian, as if the nearest died. [3] His saying: 'with the absence of the nearest': But if the more distant marries off while the nearest is present, it is contingent on his ratification. [4] His saying: 'as long as he does not await': 'ma' is relative, 'al-kufu'' is the subject of 'yantazir', what follows is its attribute, and 'al-khabar' is the object. The second definition is the one attributed by al-Zayla'i to the majority of the mashayikh, and he said: the fatwa is upon it, and it is chosen in al-Haqa'iq and others, and al-Kanz, and al-Hidayah, and al-Ikhtiyar and others: it is not awaiting the suitor. The fruit of the difference appears in the case of one who hides in the city: is it disconnected absence? Know that the more distant guardian has the authority to marry off upon the absence of the nearest with disconnected absence. Its interpretation according to the majority is what was mentioned, namely his saying 'as long as he does not await', i.e., a period in which the suitable suitor does not await [news]. Then he conjoined to his saying 'as long as he does not await' his saying 'a period' according to a group of the later scholars, and the fatwa is upon it. And they differed over the definition of disconnected absence: First: not awaiting the suitable suitor, and it was chosen by most of the mashayikh and the author of al-Mukhtar and al-Multaqa, and Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi and Muhammad ibn al-Fadl authenticated it, and in al-Hidayah it is closer to fiqh. In al-Fath it is more resembling fiqh, and in al-Durr al-Muntqa from al-Haqa'iq it is the soundest of sayings, and in al-Bahr the best fatwa is upon what the majority of the mashayikh hold. Second: the distance of travel (qasr); the majority of later scholars are upon it, and the author of al-Kanz and al-Tanwir chose it, and the author of al-Kafi and al-Tibyan said: the fatwa is upon it. Third: that he be in a town to which the caravan only comes once a year; it was chosen by al-Quduri and Ibn Salamah. And other definitions are mentioned; see al-Tibyan.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary from the chapter on the guardian (wali) and compatibility (kafa'ah) in the Book of Marriage. The author, 'Imad al-Ri'ayah, explains the matn from al-Wiqayah. The main ruling: when the nearest guardian (al-aqrab) is absent with a disconnected absence (ghaybah munqati'ah), the more distant guardian (al-ab'ad), such as the grandfather, may contract the marriage of the ward. This is because guardianship is based on consideration (nazar), and it is not considerate to leave the ward's interest to an absent guardian whose opinion cannot be sought. Hence, the absent guardian is treated as if dead, and the authority passes to the next in line. However, if the more distant marries while the nearest is present, it is suspended (mawquf) on the nearest's ratification. The key issue is the definition of 'disconnected absence'. The text presents three opinions among Hanafi scholars: (1) the suitor does not await news from the guardian, chosen by the majority of early mashayikh; (2) the distance of travel (qasr) that shortens prayer, adopted by most later scholars and the relied-upon fatwa; (3) the guardian is in a place reachable by caravan only once a year, chosen by al-Quduri and Ibn Salamah. The author notes that the fatwa is upon the second view according to later scholars, though the first is considered more sound by many. The practical difference (thamaratu al-khilaf) arises when the guardian hides within the city—is that considered disconnected absence? The text also includes a hadith: 'The sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian' (Ibn Majah, Tirmidhi), showing the chain of authority when no relative exists.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): The guardian with authority to contract marriage; the text discusses the nearest (aqrab) and more distant (ab'ad) guardians.
غيبة منقطعة (ghaybah munqati'ah): Disconnected absence of the nearest guardian such that correspondence cannot be awaited; defined by three opinions in this text.
الكفؤ (al-kufu'): A suitable match in terms of lineage, religion, etc.; the suitor who is a proper match for the woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) in this passage, what is the definition of disconnected absence (ghaybah munqati'ah)?
A) The suitor does not await news from the guardian
B) The distance of travel that shortens prayer (qasr)
C) The guardian is in a place reachable only once a year by caravan
D) The guardian is alive but not physically present

Key Takeaway
The distant guardian's authority to marry is conditional on the nearest guardian's disconnected absence, defined by the mufta bihi as the distance of travel that shortens prayer (qasr).

Son as Guardian of His Mentally Incapacitated Mother
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on son's guardianship over mother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on the guardianship of a mentally incapacitated woman and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether her son or father is the guardian.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the son has primacy as guardian of his mentally incapacitated mother due to precedence in agnatic inheritance (asabah).

This lesson examines a specific case in marriage guardianship: who contracts the marriage for a mentally incapacitated woman when both her son and father are alive? The classical Hanafi authorities differ on this point, and we will explore the reasoning behind each view. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principles of asabah and shafaqah within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify that the son is the guardian according to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). This is the opening statement of the passage.
The phrase 'wali al-majnunati ibnuhu' establishes the ruling.
2
Identify the opposing intra-Hanafi view
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees: he holds the father should be the guardian due to greater compassion (shafaqah).
This is a valid ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Imam Muhammad's dalil is that the father has more compassion for his daughter than the son does for his mother, making the father more suitable.
Shafaqah is a key consideration in guardianship for some scholars.
4
Analyse the majority's response
The majority respond by grounding guardianship in the order of asabah: in inheritance, the son takes precedence over the father (father gets 1/6 by fard, son gets residue by asabah). Therefore, the son should be the guardian.
This shows how faraid principles extend to guardianship.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the son is guardian, he can contract the marriage without the father's consent; if the father is guardian, the son cannot override him. The practical consequence depends on which view is followed.
The text's initial ruling indicates the relied-upon view is the son.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وولي المجنونة ابنها ولو مع أبيها): بناءً على ما ذُكِرَ أنَّ الابن مقدَّم في العصوبة على الأب.
[1] قوله: ولو مع أبيها؛ الواو: وصليةٌ، وفيه خلاف لمحمد الله، فإنّه قال: إذا اجتمع في المجنونة ابنُها وأبوها، فالولي هو الأب؛ لأنه أوفر شفقة من الابن. ولهما: ابن الابن هو المقدَّم في العصوبة، فإن الميت إذا ترك أباً وابناً يستحق الأب السدس بالفرض، والباقي للابن بالعصوبة، وهذه الولاية مبنية على العصوبة.
(And the guardian of the mentally incapacitated woman is her son, even along with her father): Based on what was mentioned, that the son is given precedence over the father in agnatic inheritance (asabah). [1] His saying: 'even along with her father'; the waw is conjunctive, and there is a disagreement from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) regarding it, for he said: When the son and father of the mentally incapacitated woman are both present, the guardian is the father because he has more compassion (shafaqah) than the son. But according to the two masters (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani may Allah have mercy on them?? Actually 'lahuma' refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? Or Sahibayn? The text says 'wa lahuma' meaning 'and for them two', likely Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? But Muhammad is objecting? Let's clarify: The phrase 'wa lahuma' indicates the view of the two companions (Sahibayn) that the son is preferred. Actually it says 'wa lahuma: ibn al-ibn huwa al-muqaddam fi al-asabah'. So the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?) hold that the son is preferred because he takes precedence in asabah. Meanwhile Muhammad holds the father due to compassion. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. So translation: And according to them (the two scholars, i.e., Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? or the Sahibayn?), the son is given precedence in agnatic inheritance, because if a deceased leaves a father and a son, the father takes the sixth as a fixed share, and the remainder goes to the son by agnatic inheritance, and this guardianship is based on agnatic inheritance (asabah).
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific ruling within the chapter on guardianship: the guardian of a mentally incapacitated woman (al-majnunah) for marriage is her son, even if her father is also present. The author bases this on the principle of precedence in agnatic inheritance (asabah), where the son is ahead of the father. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds that the father should be the guardian because he has greater compassion (shafaqah) for his daughter. The other two—Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two companions, Sahibayn)—hold that the son takes priority because guardianship is built upon the order of asabah. They provide a fara'id example: if a deceased leaves a father and a son, the father gets a fixed sixth (since he is an heir by fard), and the son gets the remainder as asabah. Hence, the son is stronger in asabah, so he should be guardian. The text does not explicitly state which view is mufta bihi, but the opening statement 'Walii al-majnunati ibnuhu' indicates the relied-upon position in the matn.

Key Terms
المجنونة (al-majnunah): A mentally incapacitated woman (whether from birth or later) who cannot consent to marriage herself.
عصوبة (asabah): Agnatic relationship that confers inheritance priority; the text uses it to determine guardianship order.
شفقة (shafaqah): Compassion and care; used by Imam Muhammad to argue for the father's priority as guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The judge decides based on the woman's best interest

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the guardian of a mentally incapacitated woman is her son if present, even with her father, due to the son's precedence in agnatic inheritance (asabah).

Son's Guardianship Over Insane Mother: 'Asabah Priority
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wali priority for insane woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on who is the wali for an insane woman when both her son and father are present, and explain the 'asabah reasoning that establishes the son's priority.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, guardianship in marriage follows 'asabah priority, so the son outranks the father.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the marriage guardianship of an insane woman. The base text of al-Wiqayah asserts that her son is the guardian even if her father is alive, relying on the principle of 'asabah. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani dissented, arguing that the father's greater compassion should prevail. By analyzing the reasoning of the Sahibayn, we will see why the son's precedence is the adopted position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine who has the right to contract the marriage of an insane woman when both her son and father are alive.
The issue is about wilayat al-ijbar for a woman who lacks legal capacity.
2
State the base text position
The matn of al-Wiqayah declares the son is the wali even if the father is present.
This is the starting point of the dispute.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dissent
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani held that the father is the wali because he has greater compassion (shafaqah).
This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's counter-argument
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the dominant transmission) argue that guardianship is based on 'asabah, not compassion. They point to inheritance rules: a son takes residue over the father's fixed share.
The analogy to inheritance grounds the priority in a clear textual principle.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the son is the wali, as adopted by the majority and the commentator.
The father's view is not operative for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وولي المجنونة ابنُها ولو مع أبيها : بناءً على ما ذُكِرَ أنَّ الابن مقدَّم في العصوبة على الأب. [قوله : ولو مع أبيها؛ الواو : وصليةٌ، وفيه خلاف لمحمد الله، فإنّه قال : إذا اجتمع في المجنونة ابنُها وأبوها، فالولي هو الأب؛ لأنه أوفر شفقة من الابن. ولهما : ابن الابن هو المقدَّم في العصوبة، فإن الميت إذا ترك أباً وابناً يستحق الأب السدس بالفرض، والباقي للابن بالعصوبة، وهذه الولاية مبنية على العصوبة.]
And the guardian of the insane woman is her son, even if with her father: based on what was mentioned that the son is given precedence in 'asabah over the father. [His saying: 'even if with her father'; the waw is conjunctive, and there is a disagreement regarding Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), for he said: When both her son and her father are present for the insane woman, the guardian is the father because he has more compassion than the son. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani): The son of the son (i.e., the son) is given precedence in 'asabah, for if a deceased leaves a father and a son, the father gets one-sixth by prescribed share, and the remainder goes to the son by 'asabah, and this guardianship is based on 'asabah.]
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding who has the right to marry off an insane woman when both her son and her father are present. The base text of al-Wiqayah states that the son is the guardian even in the presence of the father, based on the principle that the son takes precedence over the father in 'asabah (residuary inheritance). The commentator then notes a disagreement attributed to Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, who held that the father is the guardian because he is more compassionate towards the woman. However, the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani in the dominant transmission, i.e., the Sahibayn) maintain that the son is given precedence because 'asabah determines priority in guardianship, and in inheritance, when a deceased leaves a father and a son, the father receives a fixed one-sixth while the son takes the remainder as 'asabah. Since 'asabah makes the son closer, he is preferred for guardianship over the insane woman.

Key Terms
عصوبة (asabah): Residuary inheritance through male agnates; also the basis for priority in guardianship.
شفقة (shafaqah): Compassion or paternal affection; cited by Imam Muhammad as the basis for preferring the father.
ولاية (wilayah): Guardianship or authority, particularly the right to contract marriage for another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, who is the wali for an insane woman when her son and father are both present?
A) The father
B) The son
C) The grandfather
D) They both are equally valid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, marriage guardianship for an insane woman is determined by 'asabah priority, making the son the guardian over the father.

Kafa'ah: Is Equivalence Required from Both Spouses?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafa'ah direction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on kafa'ah, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the ruling to a marriage scenario.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, kafa'ah is only considered from the husband's side, not the wife's.

The concept of kafa'ah (equivalence in lineage, religion, profession, etc.) is a key consideration in marriage validity. However, there is a well-known difference within the Hanafi school: does the wife also need to be 'equivalent' to the husband? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that the relied-upon view is that kafa'ah is considered only from the man's side, and the opposing view of the Sahibayn is rejected by later authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is about whether kafa'ah is required from both the husband and the wife, or only from the husband.
This affects the validity of a marriage between a high-status man and a low-status woman.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that kafa'ah is only required from the man. His dalil: the husband is the 'mustafarrish' (the one who takes to bed), so the low status of the bed does not anger him. The texts (ahadith) command marrying equals but focus on the man's choice.
Note the term mustafarrish is a key legal concept here.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that kafa'ah is considered from both sides. Their proof: general analogy – just as the man cares about the woman's status, so does she care about his.
This is a qiyas argument, which Abu Hanifa's specific textual reasoning overrides.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and tarjih
The later authorities (al-Bada'i', al-Nahr, al-Bahr) reject the Sahibayn's view, claiming that non-consideration from the woman's side is agreed upon (ittifaqi). The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position.
The tarjih is based on the strength of the textual proofs and practical implications.
5
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Consider a case: a woman from a low-status family marries a nobleman. According to the mufta bihi, the marriage is valid because kafa'ah is only required from the man. The Sahibayn would allow the man's family to object.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf – the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعتبر الكفاءة في النكاح : [٢] قوله وتعتبر الكفاءة ... الخ؛ الوجه فيه : أن انتظام المصالح إنما يكون بين الزوج والزوجة عند التوافق والألفة، وهما يكونان بين المتكافئين عادةً، فإن الشريفة حسباً أو نسباً تأبى أن تكون فراشاً للخسيس، فكذا اعتبرها الشارع حيث قال : «تخيروا لنطفكم وانكحوا الأكفاء» أخرجه ابن ماجة والحاكم. وقال : «إذا جاءكم الأكفاء فانكوحهنَّ ولا تربَّصوا لهنَّ الحدثان» : أي الموت، أخرجه الحاكم، وفي الباب أخبار أخر، وهي معتبرة من جانب الرجل اتفاقاً، ولا تعتبر من جانبها؛ لأن الزوج مستفرش فلا تغيظه دناءة الفراش، هذا عنده. وعندهما : تعتبر في جانبها أيضاً كذا في «الظهيرية»، ورده في «البدائع» و«النهر» و«البحر» وغيرها وذكروا أن عدم اعتبار الكفاءة من جانبها اتفاقي. [١]قوله : نسباً؛ أي من جهة النسب فإنه مما تقع به المباهاة والتفاضل في الدنيا، فشريفةُ النّسب تنكرُ وتستأنف من أن تكون تحت رذيل النسب وإن لم يكن مجرد هذه الشرافة موجباً للفضل في الآخرة عند الله، يدل عليه قوله : إِنَّ أَكْرَمَكُمْ عِندَ اللهِ أَنْقَلَكُمْ، وحديث : «من بطأ به عمله لم يسرع به نسبه» أخرجه مسلم.
And kafa'ah (equivalence) is considered in marriage: [2] His saying 'And kafa'ah is considered...' etc.; the reasoning is that the arrangement of interests occurs between husband and wife only when there is compatibility and affection, and these usually exist between those who are equivalent. For a woman noble in lineage or descent refuses to be a bed for a lowly man. Thus the Lawgiver considered it where he said: 'Choose for your semen and marry the equivalents'—reported by Ibn Majah and al-Hakim. And he said: 'When equivalents come to you, marry them and do not delay on account of events'—meaning death, reported by al-Hakim, and there are other reports on the topic. It is considered from the man's side by agreement, but not from her side because the husband is the one who takes the wife to bed, so the baseness of the bed does not anger him. This is his (Abu Hanifa's) view. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is considered from her side as well—so recorded in al-Zahiriyyah, but it is rejected in al-Bada'i', al-Nahr, al-Bahr, etc., and they mentioned that not considering kafa'ah from her side is agreed upon. [1] His saying: 'by lineage'—meaning in terms of lineage, for it is a source of pride and merit in this world. A woman noble in lineage dislikes and is offended to be under a man of low lineage, even if this nobility alone does not bring merit in the afterlife with Allah. This is indicated by His saying: 'Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you' (49:13) and the hadith: 'Whoever is slowed by his deeds will not be hastened by his lineage'—reported by Muslim.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage discusses the consideration of kafa'ah (equivalence or suitability) in marriage. The commentator explains that social interests require compatibility and affection, which are typically found between equals. A woman of high lineage or status naturally would not want to be married to a man of low status. The Lawgiver supports this with ahadith commanding Muslims to marry equals. The key point of contention within the Hanafi school is whether kafa'ah is considered only from the husband's side (i.e., the man must be equal to the woman) or also from the wife's side. According to Abu Hanifa, kafa'ah is considered only from the man's side because the husband is the one who 'uses the bed' (mustafarrish), so the lowliness of the bed does not anger him. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two Sahibayn) hold that kafa'ah is considered from both sides. The commentator notes that this view is rejected by later authorities like al-Bada'i', al-Nahr, and al-Bahr, who state that the non-consideration from the woman's side is agreed upon (ittifaqi). The passage then elaborates on lineage (nasab) as one factor of kafa'ah, citing Quran and Hadith to show that while lineage is a worldly consideration, true nobility is taqwa.

Key Terms
كفاءة (kafa'ah): Equivalence or suitability between spouses, considering lineage, religion, profession, etc.
مستفرش (mustafarrish): The one who takes the other to bed (husband); the active party in the marital bed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a woman of low lineage marries a man of high lineage, is the marriage valid?
A) No, because kafa'ah is required from both sides
B) Yes, because kafa'ah is only required from the man's side
C) It depends on the guardian's approval
D) The marriage is invalid only if the woman's family objects

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, kafa'ah is only considered from the husband's side; the wife's lineage does not affect the validity of the marriage.

Kafā'ah in Lineage: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on kafā'ah in nasab with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relied-upon Hanafi position on kafā'ah in lineage, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and state the proofs and practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
Compatibility in lineage is considered only from the husband's side; the wife's side does not affect the marriage's validity.

This lesson focuses on an important internal debate within the Hanafi school: does compatibility in lineage (kafā'ah fī al-nasab) need to be considered from the wife's side as well? The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah clarifies that while the Sahibayn held it should be considered from both sides, the majority and relied-upon position is that it is only considered from the husband's side. Understanding this ikhtilaf and its justification deepens your grasp of how Hanafi jurists reason with proofs (dalīl) and weigh differing opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
The passage addresses whether kafā'ah (compatibility) in lineage is a condition that must be met from both spouses or only from the husband. Recognize that the dispute is not about whether kafā'ah is a condition, but whose side it applies to.
Kafā'ah includes lineage, profession, wealth, and piety; here the focus is on lineage.
2
State the Ruled-Upon Hanafi Position
According to the relied-upon view (muftā bihī) of the Hanafi school, compatibility is considered only from the husband's side. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and is agreed upon by later Hanafi authorities.
The text says 'مُعْتَبَرَةٌ مِنْ جَانِبِ الرَّجُلِ اتِّفَاقًا' – considered from the man's side by agreement.
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) held that compatibility should also be considered from the wife's side. This is based on an analogy: just as the husband's dignity is protected, so is the wife's. Identify this as a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
The opposing view is noted in sources like al-Zahiriyyah and then rejected.
4
Trace the Proofs (Adillah)
Both sides use the same hadiths ('Choose for your seed' and 'When a compatible man comes to you') but interpret them differently. Abu Hanifa emphasizes that the husband is the 'mustafrish' (one who takes the bed), so the wife's low lineage does not harm him. The Sahibayn emphasize the general command to seek compatibility.
The dalīl is a hadith (ahad), with zannī thubūt and dalālah.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilāf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, a high-born woman's marriage to a low-born man is valid even without guardian approval regarding lineage. Under the Sahibayn's view, the marriage would be invalid if the woman's side is incompatible. The later Hanafi masters have generally adopted Abu Hanifa's position.
The tarjīḥ is performed by al-Kasani and others, declaring the contrary view as rejected (mardūd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب الولي والكفؤ
الكفاءة نسباً: الزوج والزوجة عند التوافق والألفة، وهما يكونان بين المتكافئين عادةً، فإن الشريفة حسباً أو نسباً تأبى أن تكون فراشاً للخسيس، فكذا اعتبرها الشارع حيث قال: «تخيروا لنطفكم وانكحوا الأكفاء»، أخرجه ابن ماجة والحاكم. وقال: «إذا جاءكم الأكفاء فانكحوهن ولا تربصوا لهن الحدثان»: أي الموت، أخرجه الحاكم، وفي الباب أخبار أخر، وهي معتبرة من جانب الرجل اتفاقاً، ولا تعتبر من جانبها؛ لأن الزوج مستفرش فلا تغيظه دناءة الفراش، هذا عنده. وعندهما: تعتبر في جانبها أيضاً كذا في «الظهيرية»، ورده في «البدائع» و«النهر» و«البحر» وغيرها وذكروا أن عدم اعتبار الكفاءة من جانبها اتفاقي.
Book of Marriage, Chapter of Guardian and Compatibility. Compatibility in lineage: [compatibility occurs] between husband and wife in harmony and affection, and it is normally between those who are equal. For the noble woman, in lineage or social status, refuses to be a bed for a lowly man. Thus the Lawgiver considered it, when He said: 'Choose for your seed and marry the compatible.' Ibn Majah and al-Hakim narrated it. And He said: 'When a compatible man comes to you, marry them and do not wait for them for events [i.e., death].' Al-Hakim narrated it. There are other narrations on the topic. Compatibility is considered from the man's side by agreement, and not considered from her side; because the husband is the one who takes the bed, so the lowliness of the bed does not anger him. This is according to him [Abu Hanifa]. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]: it is also considered from her side, as in al-Zahiriyyah. But this was rejected in al-Bada'i', al-Nahr, al-Bahr, and others, and they mentioned that the non-consideration of compatibility from her side is agreed upon.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on kafā'ah (compatibility) in marriage with respect to lineage (nasab). The author begins by stating that compatibility is a matter of harmony and affection between spouses and is normally found between equals. A woman of high lineage or social status naturally refuses to be with a lowly man, and the Shari'ah supports this, citing hadiths such as 'Choose for your seed' and 'When a compatible man comes. . .' The key legal issue is whether compatibility is considered from the woman's side. Abu Hanifa holds that it is considered only from the man's side, because the man is the one who 'takes the bed' (i.e., the husband is the active party in the marriage, so the wife's low lineage does not harm him). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that compatibility is also considered from the woman's side. However, the author notes that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa, and the contrary view is rejected by major later works.

Key Terms
الكفاءة (al-kafā'ah): Equality or compatibility between spouses in lineage (nasab) and other qualities, such that the marriage is suitable and harmonious.
مستفرش (mustafrish): The one who takes the bed; i.e., the husband who is the active party in the marriage relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is considered only for Quraysh.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, compatibility in lineage is a condition only from the husband's side; the wife's lineage does not affect the validity of the marriage.

Definition of Quraysh and Arab for Compatibility in Kafā'ah
Defines the genealogical boundary for kafā'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of Quraysh and Arab for purposes of kafā'ah and identify the genealogical boundary between them.

Core Idea
Quraysh are all descendants of Nadr bin Kinanah; non-Quraysh Arabs form a separate compatibility group.

In Hanafi marriage law, compatibility (kafā'ah) requires equality in lineage, among other factors. This passage clarifies two crucial categories: Quraysh and non-Quraysh Arabs. Understanding the precise genealogical definition of Quraysh is essential to applying the ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text states two compatibility groups: Quraysh with Quraysh, and Arabs (meaning non-Quraysh) with each other. Note that 'Arabs' here is a technical term excluding Quraysh.
This resolves the apparent redundancy.
2
Understand the genealogical definition of Quraysh
The text defines Quraysh as everyone descended from Nadr bin Kinanah. Those whose lineage goes above Nadr (i.e., beyond him) are not Quraysh.
Nadr is the twelfth grandfather of the Prophet.
3
Recognize that 'Arabs' excludes Quraysh
The commentator explains that the mention of 'Arabs' after 'Quraysh' does not include Quraysh. Instead, it refers to all other Arab tribes.
Thus a Qurayshi is not automatically kufu for a non-Qurayshi Arab.
4
Apply the definition to the historical example
The text gives the example of Ali (Hashimi) marrying his daughter to Umar (Adawi). Both are Quraysh despite different sub-tribes, so the marriage was valid in terms of lineage compatibility.
This proves that within Quraysh, all sub-tribes are equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقريش بعضُهم كفو لبعض، والعرب بعضُهم كفؤ لبعض: أي العرب الذين لم يكونوا من قريش بعضُهم أكفاء لبعض. اعلم أن كل من هو من أولادِ نَضر بن كنانة قريش، وأما أولادُ مَن هو فوق النضر فلا. [قوله: فقريش ... الخ] أشار به إلى أنه لا تفاضل فيما بينهم من الهاشمي والنوفلي والتيمي والعدوي وغيرهم، ولهذا زوج علي بن أبي طالب له، وهـو هاشمي ابنته أم كلثوم بنت فاطمة من عمر بن الخطاب، وهو عدوي؛ لأنه من قريش. فإن الخلفاء الأربعة كلهم منهم، وقريش تصغير قرش بالكسر، وهو دابة من أعظم دواب البحر، تمنع السفن من السير في البحر، وتكسر السفينة، وبه سميت قريش قريشاً لغلبتهم وقهرهم على ما سواهم من العرب. [قوله: أي العرب] أشار به إلى دفع ما يقال: إن قريشاً أيضاً قبيلة من العرب، فما وجه ذكر العرب بعد ذكره مع أنه يقتضي أن يكون القرشي كفؤاً لغير القرشي، وليس كذلك، وحاصلُ الدفع: أن المراد بالعرب غير قريش. [قوله: من أولاد نضر بن كنانة] هو الجد الثاني عشر للنبي، فإنَّه محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم بن عبد مناف بن قصي بن كلاب بن مرة بن كعب بن لؤي بن غالب بن فهر بن مالك بن نضر بن كنانة بن خزيمة بن مدركة بن إلياس بـن مضر بن نزار بن معد بن عدنان، فمن هو من أولاد النضر يسمى قريشاً، ومَن ينتسب فوقه ليس بقريشي.
Quraysh are compatible with each other, and Arabs are compatible with each other: meaning those Arabs who are not from Quraysh are compatible with each other. Know that everyone who is from the children of Nadr bin Kinanah is Quraysh, while the children of those above Nadr are not. [His saying: Quraysh... etc.] He indicated by this that there is no differentiation among them between Hashimi, Nawfali, Taymi, Adawi, and others. For this reason, Ali ibn Abi Talib—who is Hashimi—married his daughter Umm Kulthum bint Fatimah to Umar ibn al-Khattab, who is Adawi, because he is from Quraysh. Indeed, the four caliphs are all from them. Quraysh is a diminutive of qirsh (with kasrah), which is a great sea creature that prevents ships from sailing and breaks the ship; Quraysh were named Quraysh for their dominance and conquest over other Arabs. [His saying: meaning Arabs] He indicated by this to refute what might be said: Quraysh is also a tribe of Arabs, so what is the point of mentioning Arabs after mentioning them, since it would imply that a Qurayshi is compatible with a non-Qurayshi, which is not the case. The gist of the refutation is that by 'Arabs' is meant non-Quraysh. [His saying: from the children of Nadr bin Kinanah] He is the twelfth grandfather of the Prophet, for he is Muhammad bin Abdullah bin Abdul Muttalib bin Hashim bin Abd Manaf bin Qusayy bin Kilab bin Murrah bin Ka'b bin Lu'ayy bin Ghalib bin Fihr bin Malik bin Nadr bin Kinanah bin Khuzaymah bin Mudrikah bin Ilyas bin Mudar bin Nizar bin Ma'add bin Adnan. So whoever is from the children of Nadr is called Quraysh, and whoever traces lineage above him is not Qurayshi.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a commentary clarifying the categories of kafā'ah. It states that Quraysh are compatible with each other regardless of sub-tribe (Hashimi, Umawi, etc.), and similarly non-Quraysh Arabs are compatible with each other. The example of Ali marrying his daughter to Umar (both Quraysh but different clans) is given. The passage also defines Quraysh linguistically (from qirsh, a sea creature) and genealogically: anyone descended from Nadr bin Kinanah is Quraysh; those above Nadr are not Quraysh. This resolves the apparent redundancy of mentioning 'Arabs' after 'Quraysh'—Arabs here means non-Quraysh, so the ruling is that all Quraysh are compatible with each other, and all non-Quraysh Arabs are compatible with each other, but a Qurayshi is not automatically compatible with a non-Qurayshi Arab (there is no cross-category compatibility in lineage).

Key Terms
قريش (Quraysh): The tribe of the Prophet Muhammad (صلى الله عليه وسلم), defined here as all descendants of Nadr bin Kinanah.
النضر بن كنانة (al-Nadr bin Kinanah): The twelfth grandfather of the Prophet, considered the ancestor of all Quraysh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) All descendants of Nadr bin Kinanah.
C) Only the Banu Hashim.
D) Anyone from the Arabian Peninsula.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, Quraysh are genealogically defined as descendants of Nadr bin Kinanah, and they form one compatibility group; all other Arabs form another.

Kafa'ah between Quraysh and Non-Quraysh Arabs
Commentary defines Quraysh lineage and explains the kafa'ah rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafa'ah among Quraysh and non-Quraysh Arabs and identify the lineage definition of Quraysh.

Core Idea
Quraysh are compatible among themselves, and non-Quraysh Arabs are compatible among themselves.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key ruling on marriage compatibility (kafa'ah) in the Hanafi school. It defines who exactly is considered Quraysh for the purpose of this ruling and explains that compatibility is within two distinct groups: Quraysh and non-Quraysh Arabs. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the conditions of marriage in the Hanafi madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups defined for kafa'ah
Locate in the passage the two groups mentioned: Quraysh and 'Arabs'. Note that 'Arabs' here means non-Quraysh Arabs, as explained by the commentator.
2
Define Quraysh lineage
Find the definition of Quraysh: all descendants of Nadr ibn Kinanah. The commentator gives the full lineage of the Prophet to show that Nadr is the common ancestor.
The passage mentions that those above Nadr are not Quraysh, which is critical for applying the ruling correctly.
3
Extract the ruling on compatibility
The matn states: 'Quraysh are compatible with each other, and Arabs are compatible with each other.' The commentary clarifies that this means compatibility is within each group, not across groups.
4
Understand the example given
The commentator illustrates the ruling with the marriage of Ali ibn Abi Talib's daughter to Umar ibn al-Khattab, showing that all Quraysh clans are equal for kafa'ah.
This example also shows that there is no distinction between Hashimi, Adawi, etc. within Quraysh.
5
Note the etymological background
The passage includes the story of how Quraysh got its name from a sea creature. This is supplementary but helps remember the tribe's identity.

Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah. So Quraysh are compatible with each other, and Arabs are compatible with each other. Meaning: Arabs who are not from Quraysh are compatible with each other. Know that everyone from the children of Nadr ibn Kinanah is Quraysh; as for the children of those above Nadr, they are not. His statement: 'so Quraysh...' indicates that there is no distinction among them between Hashimi, Nawfali, Taymi, Adawi, and others. For this reason, Ali ibn Abi Talib, a Hashimi, married his daughter Umm Kulthum bint Fatima to Umar ibn al-Khattab, an Adawi, because he was from Quraysh. Indeed, the four caliphs were all from them. 'Quraysh' is a diminutive of 'qirsh' (with kasra), a creature from the greatest sea creatures, preventing ships from sailing and breaking the ship, and thus Quraysh were named for their dominance and conquest over other Arabs. His statement: 'meaning: Arabs' is to refute what might be said that Quraysh are also a tribe of Arabs, so what is the point of mentioning 'Arabs' after it, implying that a Qurayshi is compatible with a non-Qurayshi, which is not the case. The summary of the refutation is that 'Arabs' here means non-Quraysh. His statement: 'from the children of Nadr ibn Kinanah' is the twelfth grandfather of the Prophet, for he is Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Abdul Muttalib ibn Hashim ibn Abd Manaf ibn Qusayy ibn Kilab ibn Murrah ibn Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy ibn Ghalib ibn Fihr ibn Malik ibn Nadr ibn Kinanah ibn Khuzaymah ibn Mudrikah ibn Ilyas ibn Mudar ibn Nizar ibn Ma'add ibn Adnan. So whoever is from the children of Nadr is called Quraysh, and whoever traces lineage beyond that... And it is said that Nadr was named for his beauty, and he is the twelfth grandfather of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and he is Quraysh according to the preponderant view. And it is said that he was named Quraysh because of a narration from Ibn Abbas that Nadr was on a ship and a sea creature called Quraysh appeared; he shot it with an arrow, killed it, cut off its head, and brought it to Mecca. Or it is said that his children were named Quraysh due to their dominance and conquest over other tribes, just as this creature dominates other sea creatures. And other views are mentioned. See: Saba'ik al-Dhahab p. 62, al-A'lam 8:358.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement regarding kafa'ah (marriage compatibility) among Arabs. The author first defines Quraysh as the descendants of Nadr ibn Kinanah, noting that those above Nadr in lineage are not Quraysh. He then explains that the matn's statement 'Quraysh are compatible with each other' means there is no distinction among them based on specific clans (like Hashimi, Nawfali, etc.), and he provides the historical example of Ali ibn Abi Talib (a Hashimi) marrying his daughter to Umar ibn al-Khattab (an Adawi) to illustrate that all Quraysh are equal in lineage for compatibility. Next, he clarifies that the matn's mention of 'Arabs' (after mentioning Quraysh) refers specifically to non-Quraysh Arabs, and that they too are compatible among themselves. This refutes the possible misunderstanding that a Qurayshi might be compatible with a non-Qurayshi Arab; rather, compatibility is within each group. Finally, he details the lineage of the Prophet to show that Nadr ibn Kinanah is the common ancestor of all Quraysh, and he includes a footnote explaining the etymology of 'Quraysh' and 'Nadr' with various narrations.

Key Terms
كفؤ (kuf'): One who is equal or compatible in marriage, specifically in lineage and other criteria.
قريش (quraysh): The tribe of the Prophet Muhammad, defined here as the descendants of Nadr ibn Kinanah.
نضر بن كنانة (Nadr ibn Kinanah): The twelfth grandfather of the Prophet; his descendants are Quraysh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Any Arab who traces lineage to Fihr ibn Malik
B) Any descendant of Nadr ibn Kinanah
C) Any Arab from the tribe of Quraysh regardless of lineage
D) Any descendant of Adnan

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, kafa'ah in lineage is observed: Quraysh are compatible only with Quraysh, and non-Quraysh Arabs are compatible only with their own group.

Kafa'ah among Non-Arabs: Islam and Freedom as Criteria
Ruling on kafa'ah criteria for non-Arabs: Islam and freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criteria for kafa'ah among non-Arabs, distinguishing the hierarchy of Muslim ancestry and the role of freedom.

Core Idea
For non-Arabs, kafa'ah is based on Islam (ancestral Muslims) and freedom, not lineage.

The concept of kafa'ah (marriage compatibility) in Hanafi fiqh ensures that spouses are suitable for each other. For Arabs, lineage is a primary criterion, but for non-Arabs (al-Ajam), who have lost their genealogical records, the madhhab bases compatibility on the degree of Islam in one's ancestry and on freedom. This lesson unpacks the precise rulings from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal status
What is kafa'ah? It is a condition for a valid marriage that gives the guardian the right to object if there is incompatibility. The Hanafi school considers it wajib to observe.
2
Determine the criteria for non-Arabs
For non-Arabs, kafa'ah is based on two factors: Islam (measured by the number of Muslim ancestors) and freedom (hurriyah). Lineage is not considered because non-Arabs have lost their genealogical traditions.
3
Understand the hierarchy of Muslim ancestry
The text establishes a hierarchy: a man with two Muslim parents (father and grandfather) is compatible with a woman who has more Muslim ancestors; a man with one Muslim father is compatible with a woman with one Muslim father, but not with one who has two; a man who is Muslim by himself (no Muslim ancestors) is not compatible with a woman who has even one Muslim father.
The grandfather (second generation) is the baseline for lineage in terms of kafa'ah; additional ancestors are considered extra virtue.
4
Distinguish between Arabs and non-Arabs
For Arabs, kafa'ah is based on lineage, not on Islam, religiosity, or profession. For non-Arabs, lineage is disregarded and replaced by Islam and freedom.
5
Identify additional criteria
Besides Islam and freedom, wealth is also considered for non-Arabs, as mentioned in Jami' al-Rumuz, though the text notes further discussion on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب الولي والكفؤ وفي العجم إسلاماً ، فذو أبوين في الإسلام كفو لذي آباء فيه ، ومسلم بنفسه غير كفؤ لذي أب فيه، ولا ذو أب فيه لذي أبوين فيه ، وحرية وإنما خُص الكفاءة في النَّسب بالعرب ؛ لأنَّ العجم ضيعوا أنسابهم. (٢) وفي العجم إسلاماً ، فذو أبوين في الإسلام كفو لذي آباء فيه ، ومسلم بنفسه غير كفؤ لذي أب فيه ، ولا ذو أب فيه لذي أبوين فيه. وحرية (٤) بما فوقه كأولاد خزيمة وغيره ليس بقرشي. قوله : لأن العجم ؛ المراد بهم من لم ينتسب إلى إحدى قبائل العرب، وعامة أهل الأمصار والقرى في بلادنا في زماننا منهم سواء تكلموا بالعربية أو غيرها إلا من كان له منهم نسب معروف كالمنتسبين إلى أحد الخلفاء أو إلى الأنصار وغيرهم. والحاصل أن النسب لَمَّا كان ضائعاً في العجم فهم لم يهتموا ببقاء شرافة النسب وتحصيلها كاهتمام العرب والتزامهم ؛ ولذا لم تعتبر الكفاءة فيهم نسباً بـل بحسب أوصاف أخر، والعرب لا تعتبر فيهم الكفاءة إسلاماً، كما في «المحيط» و«النهاية»، ولا الديانة، كما في «النظم»، ولا الحرفة، كما في «جامع المضمرات». وأما البواقي أي الحرية والمال، فالظاهرُ من عباراتهم أنه معتبر فيهم كذا في جامع الرموز، وفيه كلام سيأتي. قوله : فذو أبوين فيه ؛ أي من كان أبوه وجده مسلمين كفؤا لِمَن له آباء في الإسلام ؛ لأنّ النسب يتم بالجد فما عداه فضل، نعم ليس من أسلم بنفسه وآباؤه كانوا كافرين كف لِمَن له أب في الإسلام ، وكذا ليس ذو أب واحد في الإسلام كفء لذي آباء أو أبوين فيه. قوله : لذي أب فيه ؛ إلا أن يكون فيه أيضاً منقص فتحصل المكافأة، وكذا ذكر في «الذخيرة»: ذكر ابن سماعة الله في الرجل يسلم بنفسه والمرأة معتقة أنه كفء لها. قوله : وحرية ؛ عطف على «إسلاماً»: أي تعتبر الكفاءة في العجم بحسب
Book of Nikah, Chapter of the Guardian and Compatibility. And among non-Arabs, compatibility is by Islam: one with two Muslim parents is compatible with one having more ancestors Muslim; one who is Muslim by himself is not compatible with one having a Muslim father; nor is one with a Muslim father compatible with one having two Muslim parents; and by freedom. And compatibility in lineage is specified for Arabs because non-Arabs have lost their lineages. (2) And among non-Arabs, compatibility is by Islam: one with two Muslim parents is compatible with one having more ancestors Muslim; one who is Muslim by himself is not compatible with one having a Muslim father; nor is one with a Muslim father compatible with one having two Muslim parents. And freedom (4). Including what is above him like the children of Khuzaymah and others, they are not Quraysh. His statement: 'because non-Arabs' refers to those who do not trace lineage to any Arab tribe, and most people of towns and villages in our country nowadays are among them, whether they speak Arabic or not, except those who have a known lineage such as those tracing to one of the caliphs or the Ansar, etc. The summary is that since lineage is lost among non-Arabs, they did not care to preserve nobility of lineage like Arabs did; therefore, compatibility is not considered by lineage but by other attributes. For Arabs, compatibility is not considered by Islam, as in al-Muhit and al-Nihayah, nor by religiosity, as in al-Nazm, nor by profession, as in Jami' al-Mudmarat. As for the remaining attributes, i.e., freedom and wealth, it appears from their statements that they are considered; as in Jami' al-Rumuz, and there is discussion to come. His statement: 'one with two Muslim parents' means one whose father and grandfather were Muslims is compatible with one who has more Muslim ancestors because lineage is determined by the grandfather; beyond that is extra. Yes, one who embraced Islam by himself while his ancestors were disbelievers is not compatible with one who has a Muslim father; likewise, one with a single Muslim father is not compatible with one having more Muslim ancestors or two Muslim parents. His statement: 'for one with a father therein' except if there is also a defect, then compatibility is achieved. Similarly, it is mentioned in al-Dhakhirah: Ibn Sama'ah mentioned regarding a man who embraces Islam by himself and a woman who is a freed slave, that he is compatible for her. His statement: 'and freedom' is conjuncted to 'Islam': meaning compatibility among non-Arabs is considered by...
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on kafa'ah (marriage compatibility) for non-Arabs (al-Ajam) in the Hanafi madhhab. The matn states that for non-Arabs, compatibility is based on Islam: one whose father and grandfather are Muslims (i.e., two generations) is compatible with one who has even more Muslim ancestors; however, a person who is Muslim by himself (with non-Muslim ancestors) is not compatible with one who has a Muslim father, and one with a Muslim father alone is not compatible with one with two Muslim parents. Additionally, freedom (hurriyah) is also a criterion. The commentary explains that lineage is not considered for non-Arabs because they have lost their genealogies; therefore, compatibility is based on other attributes. For Arabs, compatibility is not based on Islam, religiosity, or profession, but for non-Arabs, freedom and wealth are considered. The commentary then details the scenarios: a person with two Muslim parents is considered equal to one with more Muslim ancestors because lineage is determined by the grandfather (the second generation), and more ancestors are considered extra virtue. A Muslim by himself is not equal to one with a Muslim father, and one with a Muslim father is not equal to one with two Muslim parents. The commentary also notes an exception: if both parties have a defect, then compatibility may be established. The text ends mid-sentence, indicating further discussion on the consideration of freedom.

Key Terms
عجم (Ajam): Non-Arabs; those not tracing lineage to the Arab tribes.
ذو أبوين في الإسلام (dhu abawayn fi al-Islam): One whose father and grandfather (i.e., two generations) are Muslims.
حرية (hurriyah): Freedom; being freeborn or freed slave status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is considered compatible with a non-Arab woman whose father and grandfather are Muslims?
A) A man who is Muslim by himself with non-Muslim ancestors
B) A man whose father is Muslim but grandfather is not
C) A man whose father and grandfather are both Muslims
D) A man with more than two Muslim ancestors

Key Takeaway
For non-Arabs, kafa'ah is based on the number of Muslim ancestors and freedom, not on lineage.
